# Notes

Thank you everyone for putting together this file!! Here is each
contributors explanation of the file.

Andrew \-\-- This file is intended to be used against military affs with
an advantage related to miscalc, nuclear escalation, etc (think domain
awareness) AND an advantage related to hegemony. The file aims to solve
the first with an advantage counterplan and impact turns the second.The
advantage counterplan does three things:\
1.    It creates hotlines\-\--if escalations escalate the president can
get on the phone to try and deescalate tensions. The evidence in this
file is intended as an addendum to the hotlines counterplan found in the
starter packet domain awareness file.\
2.    It institutes a policy of virtual nuclear deterrence\-\--it
disarms the U.S.'s nuclear arsenal but maintains the capability to
quickly build it back up.\
3.    It institutes a no first use policy which aims to signal to
adversaries that the United States isn't aggressive.The impact turn
portion of the file basically boils down to hegemony bad for x reasons
(bad for climate leadership, general military hedge, LIO bad etc.)

Tomas \-- You can use this file against affs in whatever you way you
please, but the vision for this file is to impact turn a
deterrence/assurance aff while solving residual miscalc offense with the
advantage counterplans. Most of the impact turns in this file are
contextual to either U.S. deterrence or something to do with the US-led
order, that meaning heg/LIO/alliance network, which are all things that
quite a few affs have been defending so every aff should link to
something in here. The file also contains a few revisionism updates as
well as impact defense to some scenarios.The counterplans are meant to
be a supplement for the "Hotlines CP" in the Domain Awareness file, in
that it solves miscalculation scenarios that many affirmatives present.
The file includes supplements to that counterplan while also containing
2 other planks that accomplish a similar purpose through perhaps a
better mechanism, those being the NFU and VND counterplans.

Terry \-- The file contains 3 CP\'s that are centered around solving
deterrence: miscalc CP, hotlines CP, NFU CP.\
The Miscalc Counterplan is predicated on the idea of \"Virtual
Deterrence\" in order to lead global disarmarment. However, this
doesn\'t really have alot of good extension cards and probably needs
some more work but, there are some strong answers to it. The same
explanation applies to the miscalc CP. The Hotlines CP is an extension
of the counterplan that was put out in the Military Logistics file by
BFHR and aims are making it a more viable off to run in the block or the
1NC. The counterplan specficially focuses/leans in on the say yes
arguments, but I think the aff card for PDB and the revisionism deficit
are still pretty devastating. The rest of the file is based on various
net benefits to why deterrence, assurances, and heg is bad which should
be used in conjunction with the counterplans which all aim at utilizing
soft power to circumvent nuclear escalation/miscalc scenarios instead of
hard power deployment.The aff answers are a little bit very uneven. This
file was made with the intention that affs and other camp files have
already put out the aff side for these arguments and that we are only
adding to beef up the neg a little bit lot more. The best part of the
file is probably the deterrence bad scenarios and the climate heg
scenarios because they are pretty unique.

Linchang \-\-- This file contains two big pieces of offense that are
most strategic versus the domain awareness AFF but is applicable to
future miscalculation and cooperation AFFs. First, an advantage
counterplan which resolves any miscalculation scenarios that are
specifically premised on deterrence. The most strategic utility of the
counterplan is probably the NFU plank which provides a better way to
reduce the risk of miscalculation while avoiding "deterrence" links and
avoids the net benefit to strat stab.\
Second, is the impact turns on case. Can be read as a big-stick hegemony
or deterrence bad scenario but is most strategic when utilized as
offense against any combination of Russia-China-US cooperation is key
warrants on this topic. They're also applicable to specific scenarios on
AFF's later in the year (ie. assurances bad, hard power bad, etc.)

Claire \-\-- This file has a lot of good answers vs. the Domain
Awareness aff, but the arguments can definitely be applied to similar
affs that rely on intense military action to increase deterrence and
reduce tensions in the Arctic. There's two strategies vs the two
advantages that are developed here --\
The advantage counterplan (with planks about NFU agreements, hotlines...
aka the hotlines CP from the starter packet doc, and virtual nuclear
deterrence) are meant to solve advantages about decreasing the risk of
miscalc/decreasing tensions. The second part of the file contains impact
turns to aff advantages about the importance of US heg and soft power as
a means to deter rivals, so most of the arguments in that section can be
summarized as US heg bad/deterrence through US \[hard\] power bad.

Guthrie \-\-- This file contains one-two off-case positions: an
advantage CP that solves the risk of miscalculation without increasing
US HEG (through the planks of virtual nuclear deterrence, hotlines, and
no-first use policies) and then possibly a HEG link to a FEM IR K. To
respond to advantages about alliances to counter Russia/China, there are
more cards about why deterrence or US heg is bad (for various reasons
including climate leadership, LIO, etc.). Essentially, it works very
well against any AFFs that rely upon realist military solutions to deter
Russia/China and strengthen US influence. The virtual nuclear deterrence
suggests that the US disassemble all of its nuclear capabilities but
maintain the ability to construct them. The hotline refers to increased
communication between China and the USA. The NFU plank refers to the US
officially confirming the principle of \"no-first use\" ie. that the US
will never launch its nuclear weapons first. This solves miscalc and
strat stab and doesn\'t link to HEG bad.

Haley \-\-- File Explanation:This file has impact turns to most military
affs on this topic. You can choose to impact turn certain scenarios the
aff defends (e.g. Climate Leadership) or the whole thing (e.g. Heg Bad).
This means this file also has responses to a lot of aff tricks. It makes
it much harder to get out of 'no but our hegemony is good' when the
negative has a specific straight turn to their version of hegemony. For
example this file has responses to liberal international order,
deterrence, assurances, and hard power, which are all popular
advantages. This file was structured to be run against affs like domain
awareness that have a miscalculation scenario, and a competitiveness
scenario. To deal with this, theres an advantage counterplan with
multiple planks to solve de-escalation/miscalculation/and potentially
deterrence without linking to the impact turn.A couple things to be
careful about:\
Some cards in this file may be slightly contradictory. If you're reading
the advantage counterplan plank that does virtual deterrence, it's best
not to read that with deterrence bad (or kick the plank/concede
deterrence at some point). Theres multiple options for negative strategy
but if you combine too much you might end up double turning yourself.
Some cards say US Heg is declining now but clinging to it lets
revisionist actors like Russia and China fill in, but some other cards
might say that Russia and china aren't revisionist. Most parts of this
file aren't contradictory with other DAs like Russia PTX, Relations, or
even stuff like the Security K but the aff might be able to cross apply
some things. It also probably doesn't wouldn't work very well with China
Soft Power.Good luck and have fun! Happy Impact Turning!

Eva \-\-- The main strategy for this file was based around a 1nc for
domain awareness or any aff with a deterrence advantage and an 
assurance advantage since those are looking like the most popular kinds
of affs so far.  This file was also heavily based on the case neg
already out for domain awareness like the hotlines CP but the turns are
the biggest part of this file, and the most important.For the assurance
advantage we made a advantage counterplan with hotlines, NFU, and
miscalc planks. The net benefit to the CP is the turns (heg bad/
deterrence bad etc) which turns the deterrence advantage. NFU is a
really strategic plank since it disproves perm shields the link. The heg
bad turn is the most substantial part of this file because its the most
strategic even without counterplans but it also takes the longest to
extend since you need to prove multipol is better or the current LIO is
bad. Overall I think this file will be super useful even if you dont use
everything from it because each part can be applied in so many ways to
so many different affs.

Dalton \-- Something important to note is that if you have other
advantage counterplans which solve other (non-heg) scenarios you can
read them with this file and impact turn the heg stuff, but this file's
advantage counterplans are focused on beating affs like domain awareness
with a heg scenario and a miscalc scenario, so I'll be talking in the
context of that. I think the best way to utilize this file is to read an
Advantage Counterplan that solves any 'miscalc' impact (i.e. NFU,
Hotlines, etc.) whilst impact turning any and all 'deterrence',
'assurance', 'LIO Good', etc. type of scenarios, since this prevents
them from getting any offense from the part of the case that doesn't
rely on the U.S. being great and Russia and China being the most evil
countries ever, as the advantage counterplan doesn't take up too much
time and allows you to moot around half or more of the 1AC and focus the
debate on heg good/bad, which is a really good position for the negative
to be in considering how massive the block is and how well prepared the
negative probably is for these debates compared to the aff.I think the
aff's best bet is probably to straight up kick their non-heg scenarios
and focus on heg good (like transition wars, Russia and china evil,
etc.) or focus on the non-heg scenario and spend a lot more time on the
counterplan, since if you try to do both you will do neither. I think
both routes are good, but you probably want to do the former if your
miscalc scenario is fast, since it'll probably kill everyone before heg
kills us. However, if their scenario is more along the lines of
something like "Plan causes Taiwan War" you should probably go more for
arguments like 'US heg key to deterrence' and 'china/russia evil'.\
"I  car seat headrest" -- isaac kim (Thomas please keep this in this is
ESSENTIAL for the explanation)

Devi \-\-- The file consists of many key applicable elements for both
affirmative and negative teams to apply to any argument. Some are
generic and some are specific. It starts with generic impact scenarios
and why they are good or bad (Ex. heg good, prolif good, ect) so you can
turn the AFF impact no matter their case. There are so many great cards
that you can use to create DA's, CPs, and kritik specific links.\
There are also three counterplans. First, the Hotlines CP which has been
updated from the one in Domain Awareness AFF and has better specific
links. Neg answers have also been added talking about alt reasons why
hotlines fail. Next is the Miscalculation CP because this is going to be
a common aff impact. This specific CP is still in the works but it\'s
started for you and you can add generics or cut your own cards. Finally
there is the NFU CP (no first use) which says that the US formally says
that they won\'t launch nukes first which convinces other countries
(russia, china, ect) and you can run strat stab DA and no miscalc turn
for potentially OFF case positions.\
You can also change the tags of some cards to make them case specific.
For example, Domain Awareness -\> tech fails card -\> domain awareness
tech (name ones in the card) fails to link better to the AFF which is
good for both sides.\
Finally there are AFF answers, although not as substantial as the NEG
cards but there are other generics you can find in other files put out
in the UM Camp Files.\
Overall this is a great file to use for any impact scenario, to turn
case, DA, CP, ect. and you can use these on the case page for extra
offense and defense. All the cards are applicable anywhere in the
debate.

# ADV CPs

## NFU

### 1NC

#### The United States Federal Government should institute a no-first use policy. 

#### It [solves]{.underline}. NFU sends a credible signal which spurs [risk mitigation]{.underline} and [follow-on]{.underline} better in [all domains]{.underline}.

Lewis A. **Dunn 21** \[02-11-21, Lewis A. Dunn, Independent Consultant,
former US ambassador to the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty Review
Conference; "Adversarial strategic competition between China
and the United States: understanding and mitigating its risks";
https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s42533-021-00070-2\]//lzhao

In important respects, however, the situation is different today.
[[There is a]{.underline} [greater **need to find ways to
signal**]{.underline}]{.mark} [mutual **[nuclear restraint]{.mark}** and
to clarify each other's respective strategic intentions.]{.underline} US
allies in Asia, no less than US officials past and present, moreover,
can see that Chinese nuclear modernization is intended to [sustain US
vulnerability.]{.underline} Refusing to formally acknowledge such
vulnerability will not change that reality. Political criticism can
still be expected. However, [it [could]{.mark} well [be muted if by
lessening Chinese concerns about US intentions]{.mark}, that statement
pays of **[with lessened pressures for Chinese nuclear
modernization]{.mark}**]{.underline}[, [restraint
in]{.underline}]{.mark} Chinese **[[behavior in other
domains]{.underline}]{.mark}** impacting (including counter-space),
[[and overall greater]{.underline}
**[transparency]{.underline}**]{.mark} [concerning China's nuclear
intentions, plans, and programs]{.underline}. Both countries would
benefit. Thus, a new look at such a joint statement is warranted.
[[A]{.mark} related joint [statement would be a]{.mark} "**Mutual
[No-First-Strategic-Attacks]{.mark}**["
pledge]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}16 This pledge would encompass, but
[extend beyond a mutual commitment to no-first-use]{.underline} of
nuclear weapons. Regardless of the means, [[it would]{.underline}
[include first attacks **against nuclear
deterrence**]{.underline}]{.mark} [delivery systems, space- or
ground-based **[NC3]{.mark}**, **[early-warning]{.mark}** architecture,
national command authorities, [and **homeland
defenses**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} A no-first-strategic-attacks pledge
could be a stand-alone statement or be folded into the broader statement
on stabilizing the mutual deterrence relationship. At one level, a joint
no-first-strategic attacks [[pledge]{.mark} again [would be an]{.mark}
[important **signal** of strategic intent]{.mark}]{.underline}[.]{.mark}
As such, [[it could reduce pressures]{.underline}]{.mark} for
competitive armament. Negotiation or exchanges on such a statement,
e.g., how to define a strategic attack, also would provide insights into
each country's thinking as well as an opportunity to clarify red lines
and [[address **misperceptions**]{.mark}.]{.underline} If realized, a
no-first-strategic attacks commitment also could be expected to
influence elements of each country's military programs and [force
posture of concern to the other country]{.underline}, from sizing of US
homeland missile defenses and longer range **[[hypersonic]{.mark}
missile capabilitie[s]{.mark}]{.underline}** to Chinese [counter-space
capabilities to a shift to a launch-on-warning nuclear
posture]{.underline}. Backed by restraint in practice, [it would [help
**mitigate the risk of conflict** escalation]{.underline}]{.mark}
[toward the nuclear threshold]{.underline}. Its violation in the midst
of conflict would come at significant risk, not least, possible counter
escalation based on an expectation of a more complete breakdown of
mutual restraint. Chinese [[officials have]{.mark} long [emphasized
China's no-first-use]{.mark} of nuclear weapons]{.underline} policy
[[and **called on other nuc**]{.mark}**lear-armed [states to]{.mark}
[adopt]{.mark} comparable [policies.]{.mark}**]{.underline} Chinese
participants in the semi-official Track 1.5 dialogue also have [stressed
that [a **US commitment**]{.mark} to no-first-use of nuclear weapons
[would be]{.mark} **highly [reassuring to China]{.mark}**]{.underline}
as an indication of US strategic intentions.17 A US commitment to
no-first-strategic attacks also would bring [more practical benefits for

i[mpacts on US programs]{.underline} and posture. Nonetheless, a [more

as part of its anti-access, anti-intervention posture (e.g., strikes
against dual-purpose US satellites).

### 2NC/1NR\-\--Solvency

#### NFU is key to deescalating tensions with adversaries through soft power diplomacy -- That reduces the risk of miscalculation without the traps of US hard power. 

**Tannenwald, '19** (Nina Tannenwald is an American political scientist.
She is a senior lecturer in the Department of Political Science at Brown
University, and a former director of Brown\'s International Relations
Program.) It\'s Time for a US No-First-Use Nuclear Policy (May 2019).
Texas National Security Review: Volume 2, Issue 3.
<https://repositories.lib.utexas.edu/server/api/core/bitstreams/03648dfd-a149-4201-b725-546215ffd77e/content> -
[GL]{.mark}

As Kingston Reif and Daryl Kimball of the Arms Control Association have
argued[, "[a clear U.S. no first-use policy would reduce the risk of
Russian or Chinese nuclear miscalc]{.mark}ulation during a crisis by
alleviating concerns about a devastating U.S. nuclear
first-strike."]{.underline}24 This would mean that the United States
would rely on nuclear weapons only to deter nuclear attacks. [[Adopting
this approach would]{.mark} involve more than "cheap talk," for it would
[require meaningful doctrinal]{.mark} and operational
[changes]{.mark}]{.underline}.25 Specifically, it would allow the United
States to adopt a less threatening nuclear posture. It would eliminate
first-strike postures, preemptive capabilities, and other types of
destabilizing warfighting strategies. It would emphasize restraint in
targeting, launch-on-warning, alert levels of deployed systems,
procurement, and modernization plans. In other words[, [it
would]{.underline}]{.mark} [help shape the physical qualities of nuclear
forces in a way that renders them unsuitable for missions other than
deterrence of nuclear attacks.]{.underline}26 Implementing these steps
would [[significantly reduce the risk of accidental, unauthorized,
mistaken, or preemptive use]{.mark}.]{.underline} [The removal of
threats of a nuclear first strike would [also strengthen
strategic]{.mark} and crisis [stability]{.mark}]{.underline}.27 Of
perhaps equal importance[, [adopting an NFU policy would]{.mark} help
[address humanitarian concerns]{.mark} and reduce the salience of
nuclear weapons.]{.underline}28 Likewise, it would "be more consistent
with the long-term goal of global nuclear disarmament [and would [better
contribute to]{.underline}]{.mark} [US [nuclear nonproliferation
objectives]{.mark}."29 [A multilateral NFU pledge would have **even
more** benefits]{.mark}]{.underline}. It would [[move Russia and
Pakistan away from their high-risk doctrines and reduce]{.mark} a source
of [Russia-NATO tensions]{.mark}. A common [NFU policy would help
anchor]{.mark} the [existing NFU policies of China and India]{.mark} and
implicitly acknowledge their leadership in this area]{.underline}, a
virtue when middlepower states are feeling disenfranchised from the
global nuclear order. Some analysts have questioned whether[, [in an
asymmetric conflict]{.mark}, an [American NFU policy would]{.mark}
actually [help reduce the risk of nuclear escalation]{.mark} by an
adversary]{.underline}. The United States is so conventionally dominant,
they argue that, in a crisis, a country like North Korea might employ
nuclear weapons preemptively because the United States could take out
North Korean targets even with just conventional weapons.30 It is true
that an NFU policy might make no difference in such a situation. Still,
it might nevertheless remove at least one source of crisis instability.
Most importantly, however, in an era of "multi-front" deterrence, North
Korea is not the only adversary [and]{.mark} a [U.S. NFU policy would
remain valuable [in less asymmetric conflicts]{.mark}]{.underline}. A
second concern is that a real NFU strategy would require a greater
commitment to a countervalue targeting strategy --- targeting civilians
rather than nuclear silos --- and thus run up against moral and legal
rules prohibiting the direct targeting of civilians.31 This is a
legitimate point. However, current U.S. counterforce targeting policy
will likely result in massive civilian casualties as "collateral
damage," making the risk to civilians of an NFU strategy little
different.3 [[**The United States ought to unilaterally adopt an NFU
policy**, and ask other nuclear-armed states to do the same. This would
constitute the formal adoption of what is already]{.mark} essentially
[de facto U.S. policy]{.mark}]{.underline}.33 A [[U.S. NFU policy would
create political space for Russia to follow suit]{.mark}: For Russia to
consider NFU, its concerns about U.S. ballistic missile defenses,
imbalances in conventional forces, and issues of NATO enlargement would
need to be addressed. The United States would also need to tackle the
issue of extended deterrence with its allies and move toward
conventional extended deterrence]{.underline}.34 India and Pakistan
would need a modus vivendi on Kashmir, while the United States and North
Korea would need to sign a non-aggression pact. In fact, [[the
U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [could]{.mark} actually [negotiate
a]{.mark} mutual NFU [agreement with North Korea]{.mark}.]{.underline}
The United States is extremely unlikely to use nuclear weapons first on
North Korea, therefore an agreement that provided a basis for [imposing
some restraint on the North Korean arsenal would be [in America's
interest]{.mark}]{.underline}.35 Doctrinal and operational changes would
need to follow such a declaration. China's restrained nuclear arsenal
provides the best example of an NFU pledge implemented in practice.
Unlike the United States and Russia, [[China keeps its warheads and
missiles separated]{.mark}. It has not developed precision-strike
nuclear war-fighting capabilities, such as tactical nuclear weapons,
[and it does not keep its forces on "launch-on-warning"
alert]{.mark}.]{.underline} China has also invested heavily in
conventional military modernization so that it would not have to
consider nuclear escalation in a conventional war.36 [[India, too, keeps
its warheads and missiles separate in support of its NFU
pledge]{.underline}]{.mark}, though some analysts argue that India's NFU
policy does not run especially deep and that it "is neither a stable nor
a reliable predictor of how the Indian military and political leadership
might actually use nuclear weapons."37 Nevertheless, both countries'
operational postures reflect (to some degree) their NFU policies.38
[[The United States and the other nuclear powers should move in this
direction]{.mark}.]{.underline}

#### CP solves miscalculation and strategic stability

**Kimball, '18** (Daryl G. Kimball is the Executive Director of the Arms
Control Association, October 2018, \"The Case for a U.S. No-First-Use
Policy\", The Arms Control Association,
<https://www.armscontrol.org/act/2018-10/focus/case-us-no-first-use-policy> -
[GL]{.mark}

Stanley Kubrick's classic 1964 film "Dr. Strangelove" delivers an eerily
accurate depiction of the absurd logic and catastrophic risks of U.S.
and Russian Cold War nuclear deterrence strategy, but for one key
detail: President Merkin Muffley was wrong when he said, "It is the
avowed policy of our country never to strike first with nuclear
weapons." But it should be. Fortunately, the nuclear "doomsday machine"
has not yet been unleashed. [Arms control agreements have led to
significant, verifiable reductions in the U.S. and Russian nuclear
arsenals, the two countries have ceased nuclear testing, and they have
tightened checks on nuclear command and control. But [the potential for
a catastrophic nuclear war remains]{.mark}. [The core elements of Cold
War-era U.S. nuclear strategy are]{.mark} largely [the same]{.mark},
[including the option to use nuclear weapons first and the]{.mark}
maintenance of prompt-launch [policies that]{.mark} still [give the
president unchecked authority to order]{.mark} the use of [nuclear
weapons]{.mark}.]{.underline} Today, the [[U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [and Russia deploy]{.mark} massive strategic nuclear
[arsenals consisting of up to 1,550 warheads]{.mark} on each
side]{.underline}, as allowed under the 2010 New Strategic Arms
Reduction Treaty. These numbers greatly exceed what it would take to
decimate the other side and are far larger than required to deter a
nuclear attack. Worse still, [each side maintains the capability to fire
a significant portion of its land- and sea-based missiles promptly and
retains plans to launch these forces, particularly land-based missiles,
under attack to guard against a "disarming" first strike. U.S. and
Russian leaders also still reserve the option to use nuclear weapons
first.]{.underline} As a result, President Donald [[Trump]{.mark}, whom
Defense Secretary Jim Mattis reportedly [described as having the
intellect of a "fifth-]{.mark} or sixth-[grader]{.mark}," [has the
authority to order the launch]{.mark} of some [800 nuclear warheads
within about 15 minutes]{.mark}, with hundreds more weapons remaining in
reserve. [No other]{.mark} military or civilian [official must approve
the order. Congress]{.mark} currently [has no say]{.mark} in the
matter.]{.underline} Continuing to vest such destructive power in the
hands of one person is undemocratic, irresponsible, unnecessary and
increasingly untenable. [Cavalier and [reckless statements from
Trump]{.mark} about nuclear weapons use [only underscore the folly of
vesting]{.mark} such [unchecked authority in one
person]{.mark}]{.underline}. Making matters worse, the Trump
administration's Nuclear Posture Review expands the range of
contingencies and options for potential nuclear use and proposes the
development of "more-usable" low-yield nuclear weapons in order to give
the president the flexibility to respond quickly in a crisis, including
by using nuclear weapons first in response to a non-nuclear attack. [The
reality is that [a launch-under-attack policy is unnecessary because
U.S. nuclear force]{.mark}s and command-and-control systems [could
withstand even a massive attack]{.mark}. Given the size, accuracy, and
diversity of U.S. forces, [the remaining nuclear force would be more
than sufficient]{.mark} to deliver a devastating blow to any nuclear
aggressor]{.underline}. In addition[, [keeping]{.underline}]{.mark}
[strategic [forces on launch-under-attack mode **increases the risk of
miscalculation and misjudgment**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} Throughout
the history of the nuclear age, there have been several incidents in
which false signals of an attack have prompted U.S. and Russian
officials to consider, in the dead of the night and under the pressure
of time, launching nuclear weapons in retaliation. No U.S. leader should
be put in a situation that could lead to the use of nuclear weapons
based on false information. [Retaining the option [to use nuclear
weapons first is fraught with]{.mark} unnecessary
[peril]{.mark}.]{.underline} Given the overwhelming conventional
military edge of the United States and its allies, there is no plausible
circumstance that could justify legally, morally, or militarily the use
of nuclear weapons to deal with a non-nuclear threat. [Even in the event
of a conventional military conflict with Russia, China, or North Korea[,
the first use]{.mark} of nuclear weapons [would be counterproductive
because it]{.mark} likely [would trigger]{.mark} an uncontrollable,
potentially [suicidal all-out nuclear exchange]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Some in Washington and Brussels believe Moscow might use or threaten to
use nuclear weapons first to try to deter NATO from pressing its
conventional military advantage in a conflict. Clearly, a nuclear war
cannot be won and should not be initiated by either side. [The threat of
[first use]{.mark}, however, cannot overcome perceived or real
conventional force imbalances and are [not an effective substitute
for]{.mark} prudently [maintaining U.S. and NATO conventional forces in
Europe]{.mark}]{.underline}. As the major nuclear powers race to develop
new nuclear capabilities and advanced conventional-strike weapons and
consider using cybercapabilities to pre-empt nuclear attacks by
adversaries, [[the risk that one leader may be tempted]{.mark} to use
nuclear weapons first [during a crisis likely will grow]{.mark}**. [A
shift to a no-first-use posture]{.mark}, on the other hand, [would
increase strategic stability]{.mark}.**]{.underline} Although the Trump
administration is not going to rethink nuclear old-think, leaders in
Congress and the next administration must re-examine and revise outdated
nuclear launch policies in ways that reduce the nuclear danger.
[[Shifting to a formal policy stating that the United States will not be
the first to use nuclear weapons]{.mark} and that the sole purpose of
nuclear weapons is to deter nuclear attack [would be a
significant]{.mark} and smart step in the right direction.]{.underline}

### 2NC/1NR\-\--Solvency, China

#### NFU solves tensions with China 

Hans **Binnendijk**, 2-7-20**23** \"Towards Nuclear Stewardship with
China\", Taylor & Francis,
<https://www-tandfonline-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1080/00396338.2023.2172846//evchkra>

With the rising risk of complex crises and military escalation in the
Pacific region, [[the United States]{.mark} [should invite China
into]{.mark} a process of nuclear restraint and confidence-building,
which we call '**[nuclear stewardship']{.mark}**. This process could
[start]{.mark} with a [joint bilateral declaration that neither]{.mark}
superpower [would]{.mark} [use nuc]{.mark}lear weapon[s first against
the other or its]{.mark} formal [allies]{.mark}. This would acknowledge
that neither side could gain by striking first with a nuclear device.
[This]{.mark} declaration [could]{.mark} be the [lead]{.mark}ing edge of
a [broader]{.mark} set of [discussions on strategic stability
and]{.mark} eventual implementation of confidence-building measures
designed to enhance mutual understanding and [trust]{.mark} in the
US--Chinese nuclear relationship.]{.underline}

While some might argue that a no-first-use (NFU) pledge is flawed
because it could be ignored in a crisis[, it would nonetheless [help
start a process]{.mark} aimed [at **reducing mutual suspicion**]{.mark}
about the nuclear motives of the other party. And such a pledge could be
reinforced in peacetime by monitoring the military exercises of the
other party and in a crisis by America's overwhelming nuclear strength.
[The **benefits** ]{.mark}**far [outweigh the
risks]{.mark}**.]{.underline}

[The Benefits of No First Use]{.underline}

For the United States and its allies, [[NFU has several]{.mark} military
and political [benefits]{.mark}. First, [it would **enhance crisis

[response]{.mark}]{.underline} after U.S. interests have been attacked
with nuclear weapons. The threat to use nuclear weapons in response to a

Third, NFU could help assuage some of the recent criticisms of U.S.

### AT: Proliferation

#### First use makes proliferation more likely.

**Evans 21** \[Gareth Evans, Professor the Hon AC KC FASSA FAIIA;
Distinguished Honorary Professor, Australian National University;
Chancellor of ANU 2010--2019; former Australian Foreign Minister
1988--1996 and Cabinet Minister 1983--1996 (Attorney General, Resources
and Energy, Transport and Communications); President and CEO,
International Crisis Group 2000--2009; Co-chair, International
Commission on Nuclear Non-proliferation and Disarmament
(Australia--Japan) 2008--2010; founding convenor and former Chair,
Asia-Pacific Leadership Network for Nuclear Non-Proliferation and
Disarmament; initiator of the Canberra Commission on the Elimination of
Nuclear Weapons; Co-chair, International Advisory Board, Global Centre
for the Responsibility to Protect; 5-5-2021, "Revisiting the case for no
first use of nuclear weapons," Bulletin of the Atomic Scientists,
<https://thebulletin.org/2021/05/revisiting-the-case-for-no-first-use-of-nuclear-weapons/>,
SHCP AB\]

**Why not no limits instead of no first use? **The case for no first use
rests on two main pillars: keeping the option of first use is
dangerous*,* and it is unnecessary. One could also conceivably invoke a
further argument that any first use would be potentially illegal under
international humanitarian law. But the relevant considerations
here---necessity, proportionality, and so on---are just as applicable to
retaliatory use as they are to first use, and as such don't advance the
present argument.

*A no-limits option is dangerous.* Retaining a first-use option is
dangerous, both for wider global peace and security and often for
nuclear-armed states' own interests, in five main ways:

A nuclear-armed state that keeps a first-strike option runs the risk of
an adversary misreading its intentions and, fearing decapitation,
launching a preemptive strike of its own, precipitating an otherwise
wholly avoidable nuclear war.

A nuclear-armed state that fears a surprise first-use attack from
another which has kept open that option is more likely to put its own
forces on extreme launch alert, thereby increasing the risk of human or
system error or miscalculation causing a launch which precipitates the
very catastrophe it is trying to avert.

[[Refusing to adopt]{.mark} no first use [encourages vertical]{.mark}
nuclear [proliferation]{.mark}, which incentivises potential
[adversaries]{.mark} to [upgrade their]{.mark} own nuclear [forces to
deny]{.mark} their [opponents]{.mark} a [first-use advantage or gain one
themselves]{.mark}. That way fosters nuclear arms races, with all the
multiplication of risk these necessarily involve.]{.underline}

[Refusing to adopt no first use inherently [encourages
horizontal]{.mark} nuclear [proliferation]{.mark}---[adding]{.mark} new
nuclear-armed [states to]{.mark} the [mix]{.mark}---with all the risks
of both deliberate and accidental nuclear exchange that come with that.
When a state with any kind of conventional capability insists that it
needs nuclear weapons to deter or defeat non-nuclear attacks, it
necessarily concedes that right to any other country fearing, or
claiming to fear, such attack.]{.underline}

Major-power refusal to embrace no first use can make it harder to wind
back breakouts by others actually or apparently under way. Insisting
that "all options are on the table," as the Bush administration did with
Tehran, played into the hands of Iran's domestic hardliners and made
negotiated nuclear risk reduction much more difficult to achieve.
Arguably, a clear US no-first-use policy would also help alleviate at
least some of the anxieties currently inhibiting progress on North
Korean denuclearization.

**Center For Arms Control and Non-Proliferation, nd**. (The Center for

chemical and biological weapons through Congressional engagement and
public education.)
<https://armscontrolcenter.org/issues/no-first-use/no-first-use-myths-vs-realities/> -

acquiring nuclear weapons are strong deterrents against nuclear

obligations. [U.S. allies understand that developing nuclear weapons in
contravention of their Non-Proliferation Treaty obligations
would]{.mark} severely [disrupt]{.mark} alliance [relationships]{.mark}
and [would]{.mark} certainly [have a greater negative impact than a
shift in U.S. declaratory policy]{.mark}. [U.S. allies have no need to
pursue nuclear weapons, as]{.mark} there is no reason to question the
credibility of U.S. extended deterrence]{.underline}. [[An NFU policy
will have no effect on the ability of the U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates
[to deter threats to its allies' security with **its robust conventional
forces and the threat of nuclear retaliation**]{.mark} in response to a
nuclear attack.]{.underline}

## Virtual Nuclear Deterrence

### 1NC\-\--Shell

#### The United States federal government should institute a policy of virtual nuclear deterrence.

#### The counterplan disarms all nuclear weapons while maintaining the capability to quickly build them back up\-\--that eliminates the risk of Miscalc and sends a signal of non-aggression that paves the way for global disarmament 

**Horvath 98** \[George Paloczi‑Horvath, freelance journalist
specializing in defence, engineering & energy
(15+ yrs); BA (Hons) History, University College London; advanced
studies, War Studies Dept., King's College London; former Editor,
Defence magazine; current Defence Correspondent, The Engineer; Deputy
Editor, NAVINT naval newsletter; contributor on nuclear weapons and
defence to numerous outlets; witness author to UK Parliamentary Foreign
Affairs Committee inquiry on post‑Cold‑War proliferation & arms control;
author, UK Defence Forum Trident review (Strategic Defence Review
submission); Ph.D. researcher on British nuclear‑weapons history,
University of Hull, 10‑1998, "Virtual Nuclear Capabilities and
Deterrence in a World Without Nuclear Weapons," VERTIC Research
Report No. 3,
https://www.vertic.org/media/Archived_Publications/Research_Reports/Research_Report_3_Paloczi-Horvath.pdf,
SHCP AB\]

(Errors in text come from text extraction)

[In its most straightforward form, \'virtual nuclear capabilities\'
([VNC]{.mark}) are [possessed]{.mark} [by a state which has]{.mark}, or
could have [access to]{.mark} sufficient quantities of [weapons-usable
fissile material]{.mark} and/or the ability to produce it, and which
also possesses the requisite technical equipment, materials and [skills
to produce]{.mark} a nuclear [device]{.mark}.]{.underline} \'Virtual
nuclear deterrence\' (VND) could be the assumed benefit a state might
wish to derive from its VNC, especially if that state can use its
fissile material, equipment and skills to produce a nuclear device in a
period of only weeks or months. This definition of VND can be modified
to suit several different types of VND posture. The term
\'weapons-usable\' is employed here because of the proven ability of
nuclear powers to build nuclear weapons lIsing reactor-grade, rather
than weapons-grade fissile material. The United States is known to have
conducted at least two nuclear tests using reactor-grade material and
Sweden also investigated this technology. Other nuclear powers, notably
India, may also have conducted research into this option, which would
become much more significant after any future ban on the manufacture of
weaponsgrade fissile material takes effect. This report assumes that
after a nuclear disarmament treaty the former nuclear powers might still
have faith in their understanding of the concept of \'deterrence\' in
the context of their \'virtual nuclear arsenals\'. They would do so
because there is no reason to suppose that such a treaty would
necessarily, in their eyes, invalidate previous deterrence theories.
Opponents of nuclear weapons have often felt uncomfortable with-if not
contemptuous of-nuclear deterrence theory. But for those who believe
that nuclear weapons do deter, the core of nuclear deterrence theory was
always the impossibility of disproving, at least as long as nuclear
weapons were never used, that possession of nuclear weapons would deter
a hostile power. The same arguments about deterrence theory would all
continue to be aired and would no doubt still be the source of intense
debate after any future nuclear disarmament treaty was implemented. The
debate would arguably even strengthen deterrence. For the record,
nuclear deterrence at the end of the Cold War was assumed to mean the
following strategy, as outlined by the NATO heads of state and
government in their London Declaration of 6 July 1990:1 These \[nuclear
weapons\] will continue to fulfil an essential role in the overall
strategy of the Alliance to prevent war by ensuring that there are 110
circumstances in which nuclear retaliation might be discounted. By 1998,
in the British Strategic Defence Review. one justification of nuclear
deterrence theory had been modified:2 Deterrence is about preventing war
rather than fighting it \... The Government wishes to see a safer world
in which there is no place for nuclear weapons \.... while large nuclear
arsenals and risks of proliferation remain, our n11nlmum deterrent
remains a necessary element of our security. Clearly the current nuclear
weapon states (NWS) and de bcrn nuclear weapon states (DfNWS) would
satisfy the basic VNC requirements of possessing fissile material and
the relevant technologies even after they had dismantled their nuclear
arsenals under the provisions of a nuclear disarmament treaty. This
would be rhe case even if all their weapons-grade fissile material had
been safeguarded. A number of other countries would also be in the same
category, even though the great majority are currently parties to the
nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty (NPT) and most are nor judged to be
\'nations of concern\'. A country which has possessed nuclear weapons
take a long time for various South Africans\' tacit knowledge ahout
nuclear weapons assembly to dissipate. Without documentation and the
ordered dissemination of tacit knowledge, it could be said that such
knowledge will only disappear when the scientists involved actually pass
away- as long as they do not commit the essentials of their tacit
knowledge and skills to others, or leave it on paper. If weapon design
documentation is retained-and as Mazzarr has observed there is no reason
\[0 suppose it has not been kept somewhere in Sweden for example-a
country could be said to retain its VNC. The tacit knowledge associated
with nuclear weapons will continue to exist in the minds of many
personnel formerly involved in the various nuclear weapon programs
following any nuclear disarmament treaty. Even without the tacit
knowledge of the cognoscenti, a wealth of material about nuclear weapons
will still be available in open print and in the electronic media.
SOl11e of that information includes surprising levels of detail on
nuclear weapon design, providing any country with a basic
\'do-it-yourself kit\' on the essential technical requirements of a
basic VND posture. Various internet sites demonstrate that the
essentials about nuclear weaponry, including detailed information on and
diagrams of weapons ,1 nd design principles, are freely available to
anyone with the dme and inclination to 10ok.S .lust as there is also the
ever-present threat of a sub-national group developing a simple and,
using the hackneyed term, \'crude\' nuclear weapon using less
challenging technologies, countries could conceivably choose to exercise
a \'virtual\' deterrence in the same way. One authority, quoting an
admittedly controversial analysis, has said that all it might take to
build a nuclear device is one year\'s effort or the efforts of two major
industrial companies.6 The Office of Technology Assessment (OTAI of the
US Congress concluded in 1977 that:7 A small group of people, none of
whom have ever had access to the classified literature, could possibly
design and build a crude nuclear· explosive device. They would not
necessarily require a great deal of technological equipment Of have to
undertake any experiments. Only modest machine-shop facilities that
could be contracted for without arousing suspicion would be required.
The financial resources for the acquisition of necessary equipment on
open markets need not exceed a fraction of a million dollars. The group
would have to include, at the minimum, a person capablc of researching
and understanding the literature In several fields and a jack-of·all
trades technician \... There is a clear possibility that a clever and
competent group could design and construct a device which would produce
a significant yield (i.e. a yield much greater than the yield of an
equal mass of high explosive). Because of the documentation and tacit
knowledge-never mind the fissile materialwhich might still be available
in a future \'nuclear weapon-free world\', it is very important to avoid
hyperbole in considering what \'free of nuclear weapons\' might actually
mean after a nuclear disarmament treaty is implemented. Even if worries
over the residual ambitions of the NWS and DFNWS are discounted
following conclusion of such a treaty (and these worries would he
considerable) any state with VNC (civil nuclear power-plants and a
reasonable industrial infrastructure and scientific skills base) will
still be theoretically able ro exercise a form of VND if it wishes-even
if it has never had any military nuclear ambitions in the past. In 1988
some 40 states were expected to be capable of manufacturing a nuclear
device by 2000.8 Of the states on that list, perhaps five or six could
still theoretically build a nuclear weapon in a matter of months,
depending on the concentration of national effort. Those states are
Belgium, Germany, Italy, Japan and the Netherlands. North Korea could be
added if, as suspected, it has retained sufficient weapons-usable
plutonium reprocessed from fuel rods clandestinely removed from the
Yongbyon reactor before meaningful IAEA controls were introduced.\') All
these countries are industrialised states with the requisite facilities
ro build nuclear weapons, even if all are NPT parties; North Korea has
also pledged not to build nuclear weapons under its arrangements with
the US. The original tnt list on which this estimate is based also
included India, Israel, Pakistan and South Africa. 10 South Africa has
disposed of its nuclear arsenal, while India and Pakistan have tested
devices and possess small arsenals. Israel is known to possess a
somewhat larger arsenal. If these states or the NWS ever disarm they
will still retain a theoretical capability, which has been well defined
by Jonathan Schell in The AiJOliti011 as \'weaponless aeterrence\'.l1
However, a so-called \'minimal\' or \'low salieqt\' deterrent under
which a small number of weapons might be retained by a NNWS or DFNWS, in
eirher assembled or disassembled form, would still not constitute a truc
\'virtual\' arsenal and should not be regarded as such. Such a
capability is fairly routinely-and mistakenly-assumed in some of the
literature to have the same meaning as the explicit VND posture
described in this report. Such a capability, which would certainly exist
in the period before \'zero\', is covered in more detail in the second
report in this VERTIC study. Some ideas on how minimal or low salient
deterrents might constitute a form of VND or virtual arsenal are very
curious, when considered in the context of nuclear disarmament. For
instance, one \'post-nuclear ethic\' which has been suggested is the
retention of 100-200 \'actual\' nuclear weapons, with a much larger
\'virtual\' arsenal of disassembled or deactivated weapons held in
teserve.12 Retaining 100-200 weapons plus a larger number of
disassembled weapons as a so-called minimal nuclear force would mean
retaining an arsenal which is probably close in size to those now
deployed by the United Kingdom or Israel-hardly models of virtual
nuclear deterrence. [[Retaining]{.mark}, say, anything between 10 and 80
weapons, or the [fissile material needed to produce]{.mark} them, might
e[quate to the present size of the Indian and Pakistani]{.mark} actual
or \'virtual\' [nuclear arsenals]{.mark}, and would be larger than the
past South African arsenal.]{.underline} In this context it is also
irrelevant whether or not a DFNWS is a \'screw turn\' or two away from
an assembled device. The DFNWS are nuclear powers in every substantive
sense of the term. Their remaining nuclear ambiguities (if there are
any) may even undermine their own notions of what constitutes
deterrence, especially if their new advances in nuclear weapons-related
technology (missiles, reactors and the like) only worsen tensions over
their de facto military nuclear capabilities. In a more extreme version
of the above, far removed from VND and yet described as providing the
building blocks of a so-called \'minimum deterrence\' or \'virtual
abolition\' environment, a \'stable\' US virtual nuclear arsenal in the
year 2040 comprising hundreds of warheads could effectively be a major
capability13. In the eyes of any nuclear power other than the US or
Russia, such retained weapons and capabilities would be a more than
adequate nuclear capability today. Such ideas are far removed from how a
truly virtual capability might better protect the interests of a country
which has signed a nuclear disarmament treaty. The worries of the
non-NWS parties to the NPT that VND might allow parties to a nuclear
disarmament treaty to keep their military nuclear capabilities for ever,
preserving the gap between the nuclear \'haves\' and the nuclear \'have
nots\', will have to be addressed within the architecture of the
disarmament treaty. This would mean that the treaty would have to be
unambiguous in reinforcing any existing ban on the production of
weapons-usable fissile material and would have to contain a process for
the gradual dismantlement of the nuclear weapon infrastructures of the
NWS and DFNWS. This period, perhaps ten years, is assumed to be the
operating period of the VND postures outlined in this report. The
unavoidable lesson of the foregoing is that all the NWS and DFNWS would
still be able to count on what they might perceive to be the
\'benefits\' of some form of VND posture following implementation of a
nuclear disarmament treaty if they wished-as might many other countries.
The next question is: to what ends might virtual capabilities and a VND
posture be put?

[[Virtual]{.mark} nuclear [deterrence]{.mark} could be the [best interim
form of security]{.mark} that parties [to a global]{.mark} nuclear
[disarmament treaty]{.mark} might have against \'hreakout\', thar is,
when another party breaches the treaty and reassembles nuclear devices.
The [assumption that VND]{.mark} could [become]{.mark} part of the
[disarmament \'end game\']{.mark} for the NWS and DFNWS could critically
[influence]{.mark} the nuclear powers\' [willingness to sign]{.mark}
such a nuclear [disarmament treaty.]{.mark}]{.underline} The varying
degrees of scepticism about disarmament which the NWS displayed since
they became nuclear powers demonstrate that the task of ever persuading
them to accept disarmament will be an immense political challenge. If
there is ever to be the faintest hope of meeting this objective it will
be necessary to show them that It could he in their security interests
to sign such a treaty. [The merits of [VND]{.mark} could be used to
[sweeten]{.mark} what the nuclear [powers]{.mark} Illay [regard as the
bitter and hazardous pill of disarmament]{.mark}.]{.underline} It could
help convince them that the signature of a treaty is not quite the leap
into the unknown they might fear, because there will be the temporary
security afforded by virtual nuclear deterrence. But VND should not he
regarded as an end in itself, only as a stage on the road to a world
unambiguously free of nuclear weapons. As proposed, any VND posture
would at most be a temporary security measure lasting up to ten years.
The former NWS and DFNWS would adopt VND precisely because it could be
presented as a temporary form of security against \'breakout\' from 3.
nuclear disarmament treaty by another former nuclear power. Just as it
will be very difficult to ever persuade the NWS to accept nuclear
disarmamene, there will be no chance of it ever being accepted if the
DFNWS do not sign up to it. Therefore a policy reliant on VND might
become the internationally recognised temporary security policy of the
DFNWS after such a treaty. The past stance of all of the DFNWS was that
they never admitted to possessing nuclear weapons. India and Pakistan
have now openly tested, although Israel still adopts a deliberately
ambiguous stance, despite its known capabilities. All these countries
can produce nuclear devices: India tested one in 1974 and five more in
1998; an astonishing wealth of detail about the Israeli programme was
revealed by formet Israeli nuclear technician Mordechai Vanunu in 1986,
while Israel may have clandestinely tested in the 1960s and 1979;
Pakistan tested six devices in\] 998, following a long-standing research
programme. But in the context of VND\'s role in getting to \'zero\', it
might nevertheless be very useful for the NWS to simply accept the
preferred stance of each of the DFNWS- whatever each may be- during the
negotiations on a nuclear disarmament treaty. Such an apparently relaxed
attitude on the part of the NWS would shift the argument over the
DFNWS\' actual nuclear capabilities to consideration of what their
desired future status should he following a nuclear disarmament treaty.
At that time, they too could adopt a VND posture. The temporary security
that this would afford them could become a major inducement to securing
their agreement to actual nuclear disarmament. This would essentially
mean that India, Israel and Pakistan would adopt the South African model
as part of the nuclear disarmamem \'end game\', unilaterally dismantling
weapons in thdf possession and placing the fissile rnaterial in storage
prior to its disposal. Given the extreme scepticism of the nuclear
powers about disarmament and the certainty that sustained political
pressure will have to be maintained to achieve that end, VND could well
provide what these powers may feel is a welcome alternative to the
politically increasingly embarrassing choice of doing nothing. (Ill this
context, NWS reactions to the Indian and Pakistani tests in 1998 provide
an interesting indicator of how easily minds can be concemrated on the
previously unacceptable idea of a level negotiating playing field with
the DFNWS). VND could therefore help tip the balance towards an
acceptance of complete nuclear disarmament. It could provide roday\'s
nuclear we.tpOll decision-makers with an alternative to the view that
nuclear disarmament is impossible. since it could give them confidence
that a reliable form of security could he maintained during the final
\'end game\' phase of the nuclear disarmament process following
implementation of a disarmament treaty. If the potential benefits of VND
are to be appreciated, the arguments against both it and other virtual
arsenal concepts must be faced. One persistent critic, Kenneth Waltz,
has written that: 14 Nuclear weapons have always formed part of the
scenery of international politics, which is the appropriate place for
weapons suited for deterring rather than for fighting. One may hope, and
virtual wea/Jcmceys do in {act hope, (emphasis added) that nuclear
weapons will continue to cast their shadow over international affairs,
thus providing a considerable assurance of peace among states enjoying
their protection. The notion that virtual weaponeers hope that nuclear
weapons will \'continue to cast their shadow over international
affairs\' does not take inro account the concept of tacit VND, or
something like it, as a stage on the road to complete nuclear
disarmament, as outlined in this report. In the same commentary Waltz
asserts that: IS For two closely connected reasons a system of virtual
arsenals is untenable. First, deterrence without second-strike forces
will not work. Second, a system of virtual arsenals would be unstable.
[The [idea that]{.mark} nuclear [deterrence]{.mark} is [dependent on
second-strike]{.mark} forces has [been]{.mark} a [mantra of]{.mark}
nuclear [strategists since]{.mark} the 19[50s.]{.mark} But it [does not
take into account]{.mark} the [deterrent]{.mark} qualities [of very
small]{.mark} nuclear [arsenals, whose]{.mark} second-strike
[capabilities are,]{.mark} at the very least[, questionable.]{.mark} For
instance, whether [Israel, with]{.mark} its small territory and
potentially [vulnerable nuclear weapon storage sites]{.mark}-whose
general locations are likely to be known to potential adversaries-has a
true second-strike capability may [not be important to the states which
are likely to be targets of an Israeli first
strike]{.mark}.]{.underline} This seems to be a much more significant
factor in Israeli deterrent thinking than second-strike considerations.
16 The assertion that virtual arsenals would be \'unstable\' rests on
the assumption that an arsenal which is not \'ready to go\' would have
uncertain deterrent vCllue. But the example of India\'s and Pakistan\'s
current nuclear capabilities following theirl99S tests, in which their
arsenals may still be small or very small, shows that the smallest
actual arsenal might yet provide a compelling deterrent in the mind of
an adversary whose thinking is guided by deterrence theory. [If only the
capabilities and infrastructure are there, but not the completed
weapons, the [potential may still represent a virtual
deterrent]{.mark}.]{.underline} A more self-interested criticism of
virtual arsenals is the threat they would represent to the international
scams of the smaller European nuclear powers, Britain and France. These
countries have relied on their possession of nuclear weapons to
demonstrate that they are still militarily more capable than other major
conventional powers, Germany being the obvious example. Shorn of
assembled nuclear weapons, in a world in which nations like Germany and
Japan may be pressing for eventual permanent membership of the UN
Security Council, the British and French virtual nuclear arsenals would
seem to be little different from the virtual nuclear capabilities of
other major industrialised countries. Nuclear disarmament would still be
a great leveller, leaving VND postures as the sale measure of the
different status of the former NWS and DFNWS. Britain and-though it does
not care to admit it-France have both relied on their nuclear arsenals
as a key to binding the US to a firm commitment to European security,
For that reason they might be very unhappy about a VND posture and might
therefore insist that the US make an even greater conventional
commitment to Europe\'s defenceadmittedly an unlikely prospect at
present. Trenchant criticisms might also be levelled at VND by Russia,
whose arsenal\'s reliability is being called into question while its
size shrinks. Russia has come to rely on nuclear deterrence as an
increasingly important element of its military posture, while its
conventional armed forces atrophy. Reliable operational nuclear weapons
have also become more important to Russia\'s perceptions of its status,
as the country hecomes weaker. \'I? These factors will also present a
major challenge to the task of persuading Russia to ever take part in
nuclear disarmament, For China, like Russia and the smaller nuclear
p.owers, another central issue in the VND debate would be the potential
of advanced anti-ballistic missile defences to alter the calculus of
VND-especially if, as is highly probable, they form part of a
dual-purpose air defence arsenal in future.IS Waltz raises a more
serious criticism when he suggests that virtual arsenals may not deter
an aggressor from, say, seizing territory and immediately announcing
that it had clandestinely assembled a small nuclear arsenal, thereby
undermining the deterrent potential of an opposing virtual nuclear
capability which would still take some time to transform into the real
thing19 . Schell however argues that the delay in activating a virtual
capability would not matter, as the extent of the delay could never be
known before the event. In effect, this strikes at the nub of a problem
which, in Waltz\'s view, may face virtual arsenals: they may not be
sufficiently threatening to deter \'breakout\' and, if they cannot do
that, how then can they be justified? The answer must lie in relying on
a sufficiently convincing form of VND to dissuade a would-be miscreant
from considering the idea of \'break-out\'. It is presumed that no
rational party to a nuclear disarmament treaty would consider
\'break-out\' unless it felt its vital security interests were at stake.
Even an irrational regime might be dissuaded from breaking out of the
treaty by the possibility of what others, especially former nuclear
powers, will be able to deploy against it. The irrational miscreant
would have no way of knowing that another treaty party has not retained
some form of VND capability. Writing in 1984 Schell presented the
options open to a former nuclear power as insurance against
\'break-out\'. \[n so doing, he also countered the arguments against
VNClVND advanced subsequently by sceptics. In describing the provisions
of an \'abolition\' agreement, Schell effectively argues for the
benefits of VND when he says that a provision of the abolition
agreement:20 \... would permit nations to hold themselves in a
particular, defined state of readiness for nuclear rearmament. This
provision would, in fact, be the very core of the military side of the
agreement. It would be the definition, in technical terms, of what
\'abolition\' was to be. And it would be the final guarantor of the
safety of nations against attack. However, this guarantor would not
defend. It would deter. The most important element in this readiness
would simply be the knowledge of how to make the weapons- knowledge that
nations are powerless to get rid of even if they want to. This un
losable knowledge is \... the root fact of life in the nuclear world,
from which the entire predicament proceeds. But, just as the potential
for nuclear aggression flows from the knowledge, menacing the stability
of the agreement, so does the potential for retaliation, restoring the
stability of the agreement. Its persistence is the reason that
deterrence doesn\'t dissolve when the weapons are abolished. So, if VND,
based on a nation\'s VNC, is a viable option, what types of VND might a
former nuclear weapon state, actual or de facto, expect \[0 have
available after implementing a nuclear disarmament treaty.

# CP Tool Box

### 2NC/1NR\-\--Solvency

#### Deterrence through hard power fails - only soft power prevents war and avoids the impact turn.

**Klingensmith 25** Klingensmith, Nicole S., Salve Regina
University Proquest Dissertations & Theses, \"Using Nuclear Forces to
Reconcile Status Deficits in China and Russia: Status and Identity
Perception as Motivating Drivers of Change\". ProQuest, 2025,
https://www.proquest.com/docview/3178457521/fulltextPDF/B04333DCDCD64299PQ/1?accountid=14667&sourcetype=Dissertations%20&%20Theses
//claire

Institutionalism and Liberalism -- **[[Arms Control Isn't
Everything]{.underline}]{.mark}** Constructivism is not the only theory
critical of realism. Liberalism and institutionalism offer their own
critiques of realist theory. Critiques from institutionalists have been
validated by the fact that realists failed to predict the willingness of
states to choose to obey the rules of international organizations in the
absence of an effective command mechanism.7 In his reassertion of
realist theory as a counter to institutionalism, Mearsheimer fails to
recognize the strength of international institutions like the European
Union, North Atlantic Treaty Organization (NATO), and the United Nations
and fails to predict just how enduring they are. It is true that not all
states comply with all of the rules of the United Nations Charter, for
example the United States in 2003 and Russia in 2014 and 2022 expose a
real weakness. Similarly, not every member of NATO spends the required
two percent of their gross domestic product on defense, but none of that
is done "under the radar" or as a secret form of cheating. But despite
their imperfection, no state has withdrawn from the organizations and
all states parrot their written obligations in the charter and/or treaty
as the rules to continue to shape state behavior in the future. Realists
are still waiting for it all to fall apart.8 In their poignant response,
institutionalists Robert Keohane and Lisa Martin point out that realism
is "replete with global generalizations" and the idea that "every state"
seeks to emulate the most powerful in the system is demonstrably false.9
The authors assert that institutions are created by states specifically
because of their "anticipated effects on state behavior."10 This is
contrary to Mearsheimer's realist perspective on relative gains and
cheating.11 In fact, the institutionalists validate part of the
independent variable in this research in relation to international
institutions: establishing norms of interaction among states creates its
own positive feedback loop that generates its own constitutive
power-shaping dynamic. The expectation of reciprocity within an
international organization is a desirable component of its appeal.
**[[Liberalism]{.underline}]{.mark}** offers a similar position to the
internationalists but with a different emphasis**.** [The theory
[posits]{.mark} domestic drivers and [transnational **social context
influence state behavior**]{.mark} -- that state preferences and the
**state-society relationship** matter **[more than]{.mark}** the
international distribution of **[power]{.mark}**]{.underline}.12
Liberalism's assumption that individuals and private groups are the
primary actors in international relations is a clear distinction from
realism.13 The value of liberalism's "bottom-up approach of interest
aggregation that disaggregates the state...into bureaucracies, parties,
or branches of government" is separate from the desire to cooperate
through international institutions but persists as an important
driver.14 The idea that individuals matter in decision-making and an
acknowledgement that states do not have their own agency are critical
pieces to the theory presented in this chapter. Before the theory, it is
necessary to explain a functional decomposition of the final and most
critical piece necessary for building it -- capturing this author's
assessment on the difference between status and power. The Distinction
between Power and Status [And how does status differ from power? This is
perhaps the most difficult question to answer in a research project on
status. [**Power is used to threaten,** with violence, actors to
**behave a certain way**]{.mark} or punish them if they do not.
**Defense is an application of power,** although it is typically
practiced in the form of deterrence first, then if deterrence fails, the
actor uses force to compel the aggressor to stop**.**]{.underline}
Deterrence theory helps explain this concept. Power often refers to
resources or capabilities, physical and material possessions.15
[**[Status]{.mark},** on the other hand, [is a **social**]{.mark}
**phenomenon** and a cognitive **[perception]{.mark}.** In international
relations, status **differs from power** because it is the preservation
[of]{.mark} influence and **[authority to establish]{.mark}** social and
[international **norms that reinforce** ]{.mark}the existing
**[hierarchy]{.mark}** and benefit those with the highest ranking in it.
[Regardless of how the present ranking came into existence, it is not
only power that preserves it]{.mark}. The effects of status become a
constitutive and reinforcing mechanism of **reciprocated identity** and
recognition behavior; meaning, [if an actor is]{.mark} consistently
[treated as a **"less than,"** but]{.mark} they **[perceive they deserve
better]{.mark},** then **compensating** for [that deficit will]{.mark}
be **a [motivat]{.mark}ing factor** in the actor's
[behavior]{.mark}]{.underline}. The target of the actor's behavior is
the "other" who they are trying to influence. Although there is obvious
competition within the existing hierarchies, it mostly falls short of
conflict. Thomas Schelling is one of the founding fathers of deterrence
theory. We often associate deterrence with nuclear weapons, which is
appropriate, but nuclear deterrence is not the only application of
deterrence theory. First, if we accept Schelling's theory, **[deterrence
is a form of coercion. One actor's input into the system is intended to
shape another actor's perceptions,]{.underline}** resulting in the
second actor refraining from an action that is undesirable to the first
actor. Let's call the actors State A and State B and let's postulate
that State A and B have a dispute over some territory in a contested
sphere of influence. Status quo would preserve the disputed territory as
neutral and is supported by State A. State B wants to change the status
quo and exert its authority in the disputed area, eventually absorbing
it into its own territory. Even though State B would benefit from the
action State A wants to prevent, because of State A's input into the
system of interaction, e.g. the international system, defense of the
disputed territory, and galvanized support for State A and the status
quo, let's assume in the short-term State B is deterred from taking that
action. There are a few possible reasons for this outcome. First, State
B believes that following through will result in unacceptable costs
levied by State A and its partners that State B is unwilling to pay or
unable to withstand. In this case, the cost of action outweighs the
benefit of action, in the short term. It is possible State B would begin
preparations to be able to absorb the cost in the future, but State A
can also adjust its activities to make that more difficult. A second
possible reason State B is deterred is their perception that the benefit
of restraint outweighs the benefit of action. Restraint will help them
bide their time until more favorable conditions or their objective
changes. In both cases, State A sets the conditions where State B
believes it is not the time to annex the disputed territory. In this
example, [[**deterrence** can take the form of **threats**]{.mark} of
punishment or denial**, [but the threat of violence is not conflict; it
is coercion]{.mark}.**]{.underline} Deterrence is intended to prevent an
outcome and in this example between States A and B, it worked. [If we
take the same example a step further, let's say that State A was unable
to shape State B's perception that the cost would be too much to bear if
State B were to annex the disputed territory**.**]{.underline} State B
was willing to pay whatever anticipated cost would be incurred, possibly
because State A's threats were not credible. Additionally, the cost of
restraint and 47 perception of future risk was so high that State B was
unwilling to accept the status quo any longer. State B invades the
disputed area. Now State A has to make a choice: accept State B's
actions or compel them to stop. Acceptance would damage State A's
credibility even more than State B's escalation already did, and
compelling State B to stop will require escalation from State A that
risks conflict with State B. Compellence requires the application of
force or other measures across the instruments of power (like sanctions)
to stop State B's behavior. This is another form of coercion, but this
time it is taking an action to force a change in State B's behavior.
Deterrence failed; the next step is to follow through on the threats to
compel State B to stop by imposing further costs and threatening
unacceptable risk.16 There is no clear, obvious, and distinct method of
applying status as an effective tool for compellence[**.** Status exists
in social interactions relative to the actors engaging with each other.
In the first part of the example, once can infer that State A enjoyed
the ability to preserve its influence and authority to reinforce social
and international norms that supported the existing hierarchy. [Even
if]{.mark} we assume [State A has more **material capability to
fight**]{.mark} **in a conflict**[, their power was **not enough to
convince State B**]{.mark} not to invade in the second part of the
example. [The **only variable that changed** was State B's
**perception.** Each state's ability to wield its material power
remained **static**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} When power manifests as
the employment of military or other capabilities, is becomes a tool for
compellence. When it is not used, it is a symbol of
status**[.]{.underline}** Remember, status evokes voluntary deference.17
[Status can be an effective tool in coercion, defined as the threat of
or use of force,]{.underline} but harder to apply when it comes to
compellence, which is the use of force to stop another's behavior and
return to the status quo ex ante or compel a new status quo. This
distinction is critical to the theory presented in this research. Once
the ideas of power and status are deconstructed, the nuance that status
and power are distinctly different can be observed. Leveraging status is
a form of influence, persuasion, manipulation, or possibly coercion. The
application of power to coerce is to compel. The ability to threaten
with military power to deny or punish requires actual military power,
but rather than assume conflict where there is a status deficit, as
other scholars cited in this work have done, this research makes no such
assumption. Plainly put, power enables more effective coercion in order
to deter or compel behavior in another state. Status enables influence
in the cognitive domain. [[If we consider]{.mark} a spectrum that spans
[**day-to-day competition** among states]{.mark}, from peace through
war, enjoying status superior to others is one possible way [to **shape
the perceptions of others**]{.mark}. It [could be through coercion,
**but it would not be through**]{.mark} **compellence by using [military
power]{.mark}**]{.underline}[.]{.mark} Coercion comes in many forms and
the states with the highest comparative ranking, the most secure
identities, and consistent voluntary deference enables many different
types of influence[**.** If we look at the nuclear weapons states,
possession of nuclear weapons does not equate to equal status among the
reference group**.**]{.underline} Nuclear weapons are not the only
variable that confers status in the system, but they are the variable
examined here. [**[No]{.mark}rth [Ko]{.mark}rea** [is a nuclear weapons
state]{.mark} labeled as a rogue nation, led by a dictator **[desperate

]{.mark}**its [status-seeking behavior ]{.mark}**[by continuing to grow
its nuclear arsenal]{.mark} until it is sufficient to achieve the

be messy and obscure and one should not assume all status-holders will
49 look or apply their status the same way. The shift will not happen in
a vacuum, and this is where material capabilities, (i.e. power) enhance
and enable the desired cognitive effect: voluntary deference to their
new status position. North Korea's actions can be classified as an
emulation of dominant powers, a social identity method for achieving

Proponents of international realism confronted nuclear technology with
mixed reactions. [**[The nuclear revolution separated]{.mark}** the
accomplish- ment of [military]{.mark} **[denial from]{.mark}** the
infliction of **[military punishment]{.mark}**. The meaning of this for
strategists was that [military **victory,** defined prior to the nuclear
age as the **ability to prevail**]{.mark} over opposed forces [in
battle, now was **permissible only well below the level of total
war**]{.mark}**.**]{.underline} Less than total wars were risky as never
before. [**[Nuclear realists admit]{.mark}** that these pro- found
changes have taken place in the relationship between force and
policy**.**]{.underline} They argue, however, that the new relationship
between force and policy strengthens rather than weakens some perennial
