#### Trump kills LIO -- Clinging to the order crowds ensures reckless US action and makes multilateralism impossible.

**Lazcano, 25** - Martinez Lazcano, Alfonso Jaime, FROM MONARCHICAL
ABSOLUTISM TO UNILATERAL HEGEMONY: \"I AM THE STATE\" TO THE CLAIM OF
\"I AM THE UN\" IN THE 21ST CENTURY (June 25, 2025). Available at
SSRN: https://ssrn.com/abstract=5320549 or http://dx.doi.org/10.2139/ssrn.5320549
/SHCP Haf9000/

[In contrast to this trajectory, the 21st century has witnessed the
emergence of political actors who, with challenging and often sarcastic
rhetoric, advocate a unilateralist vision that could be summarized in
the expression \"The UN is me.\"]{.underline} This phrase, far from
representing an evolution toward shared sovereignty or global citizen
participation, [denotes [an intention to subvert]{.mark} the
[multilateral order, to concentrate global authority]{.mark} in a single
figure or nation, and to [delegitimize]{.mark} the institutions of
[collective governance]{.mark}.]{.underline} This analysis delves into
this distortion of sovereignty, examining its implications and most
pernicious manifestations. II. THE DECONSTRUCTION OF \"I AM THE UN\": AN
ACT OF CHALLENGE TO GLOBAL GOVERNANCE The expression \"I am the UN,\" in
the context of contemporary leaders with nationalist and unilateralist
leanings, should not be interpreted as a manifestation of individual
empowerment or popular sovereignty at the global level. [On the
contrary, it represents a performative act of direct defiance of the
principles of multilateralism and the legitimacy of international
institutions.]{.underline} This stance is characterized by: a) [[Disdain
for multilateral institutions:]{.mark} There is a profound distrust and
disdain for the norms, procedures, and consensus collectively developed
within organizations such as the UN. [Multilateral action]{.mark} is
[perceived as a restriction on national autonomy]{.mark} or the will of
a leader. b) [Hegemonic centralization of global
power]{.mark}:]{.underline} Like the absolutist monarch, the leader who
adopts this stance seeks to project his authority and national interests
as the only relevant ones on the international stage, minimizing or
ignoring the diversity of interests and the need for compromise among
sovereign states. [[Global decisionmaking is perceived as a unilateral
prerogative.]{.mark}]{.underline} c) Appropriation of moral and
political authority: An attempt is being made to replace the collective
authority and moral legitimacy of an international organization with
that of an individual or a nation. This undermines the UN\'s role as a
forum for debate, negotiation, and peaceful conflict resolution,
relegating it to a secondary or irrelevant role. d) Exacerbated
nationalism and unilateralism: This rhetoric is framed within a
nationalist ideology that prioritizes self-interest in an exclusive
manner, often under slogans such as \"my country first,\" justifying
unilateral actions that contravene international treaties or agreements.
III. CONTRADICTIONS AND CONSEQUENCES: THE HYPOCRISY OF POWER IN THE 21ST
CENTURY The \"UN is me\" stance is exacerbated by a series of
contradictions and acts of hypocrisy that undermine the credibility of
the actors and the international system itself. It is at this point that
observations about the European Union and humanitarian crises take on
particular scientific relevance: 3.1. The paradox of peace and war
profit The criticism of the \"subordination\" of entities like the
European Union (EU) to a logic that, under the guise of a \"fool\'s
errand,\" promotes arms sales while aspiring to moral legitimacy ( e.g.
, the Nobel Peace Prize), reveals a fundamental ethical dichotomy. This
contradiction exposes a cognitive dissonance in the foreign policy of
influential actors: a) Peace as rhetoric vs. peace as practice: There is
a discrepancy between the narrative of promoting peace and international
security and the economic practices that support a robust arms industry.
The EU, despite its origins as a peace project, is a significant player
in the global arms trade, which creates an intrinsic conflict with its
aspirations for ethical leadership and its recognition with the Nobel
Peace Prize. b) interests over ethical principles: The implicit or
explicit promotion of arms sales suggests that economic and geopolitical
interests prevail over the principles of peaceful conflict resolution
and violence reduction. This dilutes the credibility of any actor
presenting itself as a defender of peace. 3.2. Selective blindness,
contradiction and the instrumentalization of human rights [The
observation of the \"blindness\" to tragedies and the i[nconsistency in
foreign policy of figures like Donald Trump]{.mark} underscores a
serious [deficiency]{.mark} [in]{.mark} the international response and
the [application of human rights]{.mark} principles:]{.underline} a)
Violation of due process and family human rights: The separation of
families, in the context of strict immigration policies, represents a
flagrant violation of the right to family unity and due process of law[.
The [justification]{.mark} of these actions under national security or
immigration control criteria often [ignores]{.mark} obligations under
[i]{.mark}nternational human rights [law]{.mark}.]{.underline} The
indifference or lack of strong condemnation by global actors who
proclaim themselves defenders of human rights reveals a selective
application of these principles. b) Indifference to the humanitarian
catastrophe in Gaza: The inaction or insufficient response to the
magnitude of the humanitarian tragedy in Gaza, including the massive
loss of civilian life, forced displacement, and the destruction of
essential infrastructure, evidences a global crisis of conscience. The
politicization of human suffering and the failure to achieve an
effective international consensus to protect civilian populations in
conflict situations highlight the limitations and inherent flaws of the
current multilateral system. Selective blindness to this tragedy
contrasts sharply with proclamations of moral and humanitarian
leadership. c) Contradictions in Unilateralist Foreign Policy: The
rhetoric and actions of a leader who proclaims himself \"the UN is me\"
often fall into blatant contradictions. [Clear examples include: i.
[Unjustified aggression]{.mark} and then peace order: The [decision to
attack Iran]{.mark} ( e.g. , the assassination of General Qasem
Soleimani ) without clear justification under international law,
followed by the [claim of being able to \"arrange peace between Israel
and Iran,\"]{.mark}]{.underline} underscores a personalistic and
unregulated view of diplomacy. [This unilateral approach [ignores
historical complexity]{.mark}, multiple [actors]{.mark}, and
[established diplomatic processes,]{.mark} assuming that the will of a
single actor can dictate the resolution of deep-rooted regional
conflicts. ii. Broken promises of conflict resolution: The promise to
resolve the war between Russia and Ukraine within \"24 hours\" during an
election campaign represents an extreme [simplification of]{.mark} a
complex [geopolitical conflict]{.mark}]{.underline}. The failure to
fulfill such a promise not only exposes a lack of understanding of the
dynamics of armed conflict, but also exploits peace for domestic
political ends, delegitimizing genuine diplomatic efforts and the role
of international organizations in mediation. IV. CONCLUSION The
transition from the absolutist maxim \"I am the State\" to the
contemporary, satirical claim of \"I am the UN\" by leaders who
challenge multilateralism is not only a paradigm shift in the conception
of sovereignty, but also a warning sign about the coherence and
accountability of global governance[. This stance, rooted in nationalism
and [unilateralism]{.mark}, is [compounded by]{.mark} the blatant
[hypocrisy]{.mark} of actors who promote peace while fomenting conflict,
and who selectively defend human rights, ignoring massive tragedies. The
evident contradictions in the foreign policy of figures like [Donald
Trump]{.mark}, who [attack without justification and then \"order\"
peace]{.mark}, or promise magical solutions to complex conflicts,
demonstrate the [danger of this personalistic approach]{.mark} detached
from international law.]{.underline} The \"crazy world\" referred to
reflects a crisis of values and leadership on the international stage.
[[To revitalize the multilateral system]{.mark} and [address]{.mark}
interconnected [global challenges--- from climate change to humanitarian
conflicts]{.mark}---it is imperative to [transcend the rhetoric of
absolute sovereignty]{.mark} and hypocrisy.]{.underline} What is
required is a genuine commitment to cooperation, the primacy of
international law, and an ethical coherence that places human dignity
and peace above particular interests and geopolitical gain. Only in this
way can the international community aspire to more just and effective
governance.

#### The LIO inevitable collapse under Trump 

Caio **Gontijo '25**, \[MSc in International Relations at PUC Minas,
Brazil. His work focuses on political economy, ideology, gramscianism
7-10-2025, \"Global Crisis and the Liberal International Order:
 Critical Nodes in a Totality, *International Studies Review*, Volume
27, Issue 3, September 2025,
viaf013, [[https://doi.org/10.1093/isr/viaf013]{.underline}](https://doi.org/10.1093/isr/viaf013)\]

The discussion above leads to several key syntheses. Although
the crises that have led to the emergence of far-right movements across
the West may appear to be primarily "national" events within individual
states, this interpretation captures only a partial aspect of a broader
totality. [[Crises of heg]{.mark}emony [are not confined within national
boundaries;]{.mark} like the concept of hegemony itself, [they
are]{.mark} inherently [international in character]{.mark}.]{.underline}

Yet, even this binary framing---national versus international---remains
partial. While it is a more advanced analytical position than a solely
national reading, it still rests on the perspective of external
relations. A more dialectically developed analysis sublates this
opposition by turning to the perspective of internal relations.

From this vantage point, the crisis is global---a structural feature of
a totality---yet it manifests concretely in time and space through
particular nodal points. [[Far-right emergences throughout the West
are]{.mark} thus [not isolated]{.mark} political phenomena [but]{.mark}
concrete [expressions of a deeper global process]{.mark}.]{.underline}
Each represents a node through which the broader crisis of passive
revolution reveals its historical limits. Likewise, the eruption of
recent proxy wars---in Ukraine, in Palestine---should be seen as
geopolitical or military expressions of the same crisis, provided we do
not ontologically separate "war" from "politics."

Just as every totality has predominant moments, so too must we clarify
that [while the current crisis is global, its effects are not evenly
distributed. [The]{.mark} contemporary [crisis]{.mark} of hegemony is
predominantly international in the sense that it [concerns]{.mark} a
specific hierarchical political arrangement: [the
LIO]{.mark}.]{.underline} While the overarching totality remains global
capitalism, the present crisis reflects a breakdown in the reproduction
of its political form---passive revolution. This breakdown is marked by
a shift away from war of position and toward war of manoeuvre: the
intensification of political violence both within Western domestic
contexts and between Western powers and emerging geopolitical actors.

Understanding the Brazilian crisis as a particularly salient nodal
point offers valuable insights into the global conjuncture. Brazil is
simultaneously entangled with the Western LIO, engaged with the BRICS
initiative, and affected by the far-right surge. Just as the universal
expresses itself in the particular, the concept of passive
revolution---while globally relevant---materializes as a national
process. In Brazil, this process appears to encapsulate the
contradictions of the broader global crisis. Its immediate appearance is
one of everyday "small politics" (Coutinho 2010; Gramsci 2015, Q13§5),
involving scandals, partisan disputes, and media spectacle. However,
this surface instability is underpinned by a deeper trajectory. From
1985 to the mid-2010s, Brazil experienced a relatively stable passive
revolution---a fusion of liberal democracy and neoliberalism---well
captured by Brazilian Gramscian thought (Vianna 1996; Coutinho 2012).

On one hand, [this postdictatorship democratic process achieved the
long-awaited political transition to liberal democracy.]{.underline} On
the other, it introduced neoliberal economic restructuring, marked by
deindustrialization, unemployment, and rising inequality. Within the
terrain of common sense, this dynamic produced a latent popular
resentment toward political elites and hegemonic institutions---an
affective pattern echoed across other Western states undergoing
neoliberal transformation. What followed was a series of
"national" crises of hegemony, activated by ideological elements
previously formed during the period of stability. These included
regressive tendencies within common sense---meritocratic individualism,
self-reliance under scarcity, and the erosion of class consciousness.
Even in crisis, hegemony persists as a morbid symptom. These ideological
voids are then filled by far-right intellectual projects, such as
Bolsonarism in Brazil.

[[Both the Western far-right and]{.mark} the [BRICS]{.mark} project [can
be interpreted as]{.mark} violent [negations of]{.mark} political
[liberalism]{.mark}. These negations can be seen, from different angles
of the totality, either as rejections of the recent passive revolution
of neoliberal globalization or as oppositions to the institutional
architecture of the LIO.]{.underline} While these forces may appear
contradictory in the short term---[[Trump's hostility to China]{.mark}
or [Bolsonaro's opposition to]{.mark} Lula and the [BRICS]{.mark}
agenda---they [are ultimately]{.mark} part of [the]{.mark} same
historical process. Their convergence lies not in ideological coherence
but in their [shared challenge to]{.mark} [liberalism as the ideological
framework of global capitalism]{.mark}]{.underline}. This convergence
has led scholars to deploy the concept of illiberalism to capture actors
as diverse as Trump, Putin, Orbán, Xi Jinping, and Bolsonaro.

## Climate Leadership

### 1NC

#### Climate leadership actively colonizes the Global South. 

**Hurri**, 20**22**, (Karoliina Hurri is a Postdoctoral Fellow at the
Center on Climate Politics and Security (CLIPS) at the Finnish Institute
of International Affairs). 1-28-2022. \"Climate Leadership Through
Storylines: A Comparison of Developed and Emerging Countries in the
Post-Paris Era\", Taylor & Francis,
<https://www.tandfonline.com/doi/full/10.1080/13600826.2023.2173560> -
[GL]{.mark}

The expectation of developed countries' leadership is institutionalised
in the United Nations' climate agreements. [Hence, [climate leadership
discussion often builds on the experience of the Global North and
ignores the non-western contexts]{.mark}. This article analyses how
[climate leadership is socially constructed through discourse by
developed and emerging countries]{.mark}]{.underline}. [Here, developed
countries were limited to Australia, Canada, the EU, Japan, New Zealand,
**[and the US]{.mark}**, and emerging countries to the BASIC group,
comprising Brazil, China, India, and South Africa.]{.underline} The
analysis was conducted by drafting storylines and discourse-coalitions
based on national speeches at the UN climate conferences in 2016--2019.
The results underline that the two sides diﬀer primarily in perceptions
of leadership responsibility and problematisation but share ideas about
transition as a problem solution. [Furthermore, neither side constructs
their own leadership on the basis of responsibility, [and the demand for
collective responsibility particularly beneﬁts the Global
North.]{.mark}]{.underline}

The ﬁrst UN agreement to combat climate change, the United Nations
Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC), was adopted in 1992.
[Following the principle of common but diﬀerentiated responsibilities
and respective capabilities (CBDR-RC), the Convention recognised the
varying national circumstances to act for climate and divided all
nations into developed (Annex I) and developing (non-Annex I)1
parties.]{.underline} [Developed countries were expected to lead the
eﬀorts due to their greater historical emissions and capacity to act and
developing countries' higher vulnerability to climate
change]{.underline}. In 1997, the Kyoto Protocol was adopted to
operationalise the Convention by setting emission reduction targets for
the developed countries. After Kyoto, the negotiations became
increasingly complex while the US never ratiﬁed the protocol, Canada
withdrew, and Australia, New Zealand and Japan announced that they would
not participate in a second Kyoto period (Gupta 2016). During this time,
the division between developed, and developing became more fragmented
and new negotiation groups began to emerge (Blaxekjær and Nielsen 2015).
The challenges in the negotiations led to the preliminary idea of an
agreement with targets for all the parties. Following this, Paris
Agreement was adopted in 2015 and requested all nations to submit their
nationally determined contributions (NDCs) to the UNFCCC secretariat[.
The CBDR-RC principle is institutionalised in all these agreements, but
in the Paris text, it was adopted with the phrase "in light of diﬀerent
national circumstances" weakening [the strict diﬀerentiation between the
North--South]{.mark} (Albuquerque 2021). Nevertheless, the
diﬀerentiation [has remained a perennial question in climate
negotiations before and during Paris]{.mark} (Okereke and Coventry
2016).]{.underline} The post-Paris negotiations have proven diﬃcult, and
the world is not on track to reach the Paris Agreement goal to limit the
global average temperature rise to below 2°C or preferably 1.5°C above
the pre-industrial levels (UNEP 2021). The year 2020 has appeared as a
signiﬁcant line in the post-Paris era dividing it into pre-2020 and
post-2020 action; by this benchmark, parties were expected to increase
their mitigation ambition and developed countries to mobilise USD
100billion per year to address the needs of developing countries. As
achieving these goals has proven challenging, the trust among parties
has decreased. The complexity of climate change and the large variety of
negotiating actors underline the signiﬁcance of leadership in the
success of climate negotiations (Underdal 1994). Raising the level of
ambition requires leaders, as leadership can reinforce trust among
nations and accelerate others' actions (Parker and Karlsson 2010).
Post-Paris time creates a new era for potential new climate leaders.
[Although the Paris Agreement includes the traditional expectation of
developed countries' leadership, it allows new leaders to step up by
committing all nations to act.]{.underline} Due to developed countries'
insuﬃcient leadership, the expectations have increased for emerging
countries; their growing capabilities and emissions have increased hopes
for them to take the lead in tackling climate change in the post-Paris
era. Research on climate leadership has tended to analyse developed
countries, particularly the European Union (EU) and the United States
(US) (Parker and Karlsson 2018), but emerging countries' potential to
lead is increasingly recognised (see for example, Hoch-stetler 2012;
Petrone 2019; Hurri 2020). Furthermore, a research gap has been
identiﬁed on how the emerging powers shape outcomes in international
organisations and how they understand appropriate behaviour in their
mercurial position (Weinhardt 2020). I contribute to this discussion by
deconstructing the narrow western view of climate leadership. I ask: is
there a shared understanding of climate leadership within or between the
Annex II and the BASIC groups in the post-Paris era? In addition, I ask
how these groups attempt to position each other in this argumentative
struggle on climate leadership ?To compare these two contexts, I utilise
argumentative discourse analysis (ADA) and storyline and
discourse-coalition concepts to identify patterns of coherence and
reduce the discursive complexity (Hajer 1995). Storylines and
discourse-coalitions were drafted based on material including national
speeches from the UNFCCC Conference of Parties (COP) and joint
statements from 2016 to 2019. I compare two groups: developed countries
are deﬁned as the Annex II parties, comprising Australia, Canada,
Iceland, theEU, Japan, New Zealand, Norway, Switzerland, and the US, and
emerging countries as the BASIC group, a coalition of Brazil, India,
China, and South Africa. The Annex I consist of the Organization for
Economic Co-operation and Development (OECD) members as of 1992 and
economies in transition (EIT). However, because of EIT countries' weaker
economic position, a new sub-group of Annex II parties was formed out of
richer developed countries (Gupta 2010). As of 20 May 2021, the UNFCCC
explains on its website that Annex II parties are obliged to provide
ﬁnancial support for developing countries' climate mitigation and
adaptation and "take all practicable steps to promote the development
and transfer of environmen-tally friendly technologies to EIT parties
and developing countries". Annex II has notrepresented as an explicit
coalition in the negotiations but for the aim of this study,they
represent the ones who were traditionally expected to take the lead. It
is only byresearching these parties, though as a loose group, that it is
possible to understandhow these traditional leaders construct leadership
in the post-Paris era.Power dynamics within the UNFCCC are changing as
emerging countries increasinglyinﬂuence the negotiations. The
establishment of the BASIC group in 2009 was motivatedby the wish to
highlight their non-western historical and cultural experience and
theirshared Global South identity (Weinhardt 2020). The future of the
BASIC group hasbeen debated since its foundation because, despite their
growing status in worldaﬀairs, the interests of these four countries
diﬀer widely and even conﬂict (Halldinget al. 2013). BASIC countries
have emphasised the CBDR-RC principle but have acceptedthe bottom-up
approach of Paris as it enables them to determine their climate
actionnationally (Albuquerque 2019). In 2020--2021, all four countries
announced their net-zero targets, which can be considered a sign of
willingness to carry more internationalresponsibility. Due to their
conﬂicting interests, the BASIC group cannot be expectedto share a
vision of climate leadership, and therefore, this study aims at
understandingwhich issues they can agree on about climate leadership
with each other and the devel-oped countries.In the following section, I
brieﬂy introduce climate leadership literature. Then, after anoutline of
the materials and method, I present the results, which underline that
the twosides diﬀer primarily in perceptions of leadership responsibility
and problematisation butshare ideas on problem solutions.

2\. Climate leadership

Gupta (2016) has divided the history of climate leadership at the UNFCCC
into the three stages: leadership paradigm (pre-1996), conditional
leadership (1997--2007), and leadership in recession (2008--2020). The
leadership paradigm adopted by the Convention in 1992 committed the
North to lead. The interest behind the leadership paradigm was to create
a regime that included developing countries. This paradigm indicated
mutual trust, the developed countries promised to act ﬁrst, and they
expected the developing countries to follow when the time would come.
The conditional leadership stage began in 1997 after the US withdrawal
from the Kyoto negotiations. Demand grew for the key developing
countries, such as China and India, to increase their participation
because they were claimed to have a competitive advantage over developed
countries who were obliged to change their fossil-energy-based growth
(Audet 2013). Finally, the stage of leadership in recession began with
the economic crisis in 2008, as settingbinding targets for developed
countries proved impossible (Gupta 2016). AlthoughParis Agreement
expects action from all, it nevertheless includes the traditional
expec-tation of developed countries taking the lead. These paradigms
describe the history ofclimate leadership at the UNFCCC, but the
bottom-up approach of the Paris Agreementhas the potential to change the
paradigm in the post-Paris era. Does the stage of leader-ship in
recession continue, or are there signs of a new paradigm with new
potentialleaders?Analytically and empirically leadership has proven a
challenging concept (for an over-view, see Kopra 2020). It is
challenging to deﬁne leadership as distinct from other bar-gaining
behaviour and to set benchmarks for identifying leadership (Skodvin
andAndresen 2006; Eckersley 2020). A leader provides vision and
inspiration, suggests,and implements solutions to a problem (Underdal
1994; Young 1991). [[Leadership in multilateral negotiations is "an
asymmetrical relationship of inﬂuence,]{.mark} where one actorguides or
directs the behaviour of others towards a certain goal over a certain
periodof time"]{.underline} (Underdal 1994, 178). Leadership is an act
between leader and followers; there-fore, it should be deconstructed by
analysing political processes (Nabers 2011). This research turns
attention away from the individual leaders typically researched and
instead understands leadership as "co-constructed by diﬀerent actors
that participate in ﬁnding solutions to collective environmental
problems" (Uusi-Rauva 2010, 75). [[Leadership is often presented as a
universal and achievable concept when in reality]{.mark}; the context of
leadership is seldom highlighted (Case et al. 2015). [This narrow view
and **ignorance of the non-western context can lead to ethnocentric
views** on leadership]{.mark}.]{.underline} Furthermore, single-case
studies have dominated discursive environmental research(Leipold et al.
2019). Although the comparative perspective increases the complexityof
the research, in this study, I wish to broaden the view on leadership by
comparingtwo contexts. A comparison between contexts can be the ﬁrst
step to better understand-ing the phenomenon but few studies have
analysed leadership perceptions in diﬀerentparts of the world (Moan and
Hetland 2012).Critical theorists have pointed out that UNFCCC
negotiations and climate change dis-course are guided by the interests,
values, and experiences of the North and that this oftenpasses unnoticed
(Doyle and Chaturvedi 2010; Chakrabarty 2017; Sealey-Huggins 2017).The
North--South aspect is signiﬁcant for this study since the groups
researched representboth sides of this division. Nevertheless, my
intention is not to reinforce this binary division(Acharya and Buzan
2007). Instead, we should broaden our perspective from merely
criti-cising the western perspective to detecting diﬀerent narratives
and storylines about theglobal (Hurrell 2016). I do not wish to
formulate a [non-western perspective on leadership; rather, this article
aims to contribute to a non-universalizing climate leadership
concept]{.underline}.

#### Zeros Solvency - Western-centric climate leadership prevents needed Global South actions.

**Hurri,'23** (Karoliina Hurri is a Postdoctoral Fellow at the Center on
Climate Politics and Security (CLIPS) at the Finnish Institute of
International Affairs. She is part of the Academy of Finland funded
project TRANSCLIM (2020--2024), which studies transparency and
effectiveness in global climate governance). Climate leadership beyond
the Global North.
<https://helda.helsinki.fi/server/api/core/bitstreams/86efb69e-02ce-4560-bbfb-4704d0f18827/content>
-[GL]{.mark}

This dissertation has applied a constructivist approach to role theory
and discursive methodology in an effort to understand how China's
climate leadership is constructed[. This construction is understood as a
gradual process whereperceptions and expectations of climate leadership
change simultaneously in various contexts.]{.underline} These changes
occur in debates that do not necessarily even concern China explicitly[;
expectations of China change when expectations change for developing
countries, major emitters, or emerging countries]{.underline}. In
addition, [expectations of China's climate leadership change when
perceptions of climate leadership change]{.underline}.This dissertation
has covered the first six years of the post-Paris era. China's claims on
the appropriateness of its climate-leader role gradually intensified
during 2016--2021. Based on the findings, [China does not see an
inter-role conflict between the auxiliary role of climate leader and the
master roles of responsible great power and leader of the developing
world.]{.underline} However, [the alter expectations do not seem to
recognize China's strengthening claims on climate leadership, suggesting
an intra-role conflict between the ego and alter
expectations]{.underline}. In addition, external expectations of China
seem to assume that there is an inter-role conflict in China's role set.
Hence, despite China's strengthened ideational and material capabilities
and leadership efforts, during these first six years of the post-Paris
era, the alter expectations of China's climate leadership do not
exemplify a radical change from the pre-Paris era. These ego and alter
expectations support the idea that gradual role changes are more
predominant than rapid shifts since the centrality of roles in a role
set increases or decreases moderately (cf. Breuning, 2019). Yet due to
the recency of China's leadership claims intensifying in 2021 in
particular, future studies are better equipped to analyze the influence
of these claims on alterexpectations. [[Western-centrism]{.mark} at the
UNFCCC [perpetuates a perception of climate leadership that is
constructed on the]{.mark} experiences and [interests of developed
countries]{.mark}]{.underline}. This is reflected, for instance, in
conflicting perceptions of whose responsibility it is to lead, how
successful leadership has been, and [who can play the role of a climate
leader. These conflicts make it [difficult to find shared understandings
of climate leadership that bridge **the Global North-South
divide**]{.mark}]{.underline}. Despite the promise of role theory to
bridge agency and structure in IR, and its versatile analytical tools,
the ability of role-theoretical analysis to identify these broader,
prevalent constraining structures, such as structural inequality, is not
yet strong enough. Although the scope of empirical cases in role theory
has broadened from the Global North (Breuning, 2019), particularly in
the past, [role-theoretical analyses have focused on the roles of the
most powerful nations]{.underline}. Therefore, the influence of
constraining structures requires more understanding in the future. I
argue that the analysis of [[climate-leader roles needs to take into
account the underlying Western-centrism in global climate
governance]{.mark} as a boundary condition of the social interaction in
which the roles are constructed.]{.underline} The discussion about
[[structures reproducing inequality is clearly more relevant for
developing countries that have fewer ideational and material
capabilities than China]{.underline}]{.mark}. However, in China's case,
the discussion suggests that [[even though China and other emerging
parties might have gained more power]{.mark} during the post-Paris era
negotiations, they might [not]{.mark} have [as much power as could be
assumed based on their growing material
capabilities]{.mark}]{.underline}. Breuning (2019) argues that role
theory has the potential to enhance understanding of emerging powers in
particular. Analysis of emerging countries has resulted in the
understanding that the increase in their material capabilities does not
unequivocally lead to a change in their identities and foreign policy
behavior (Thies & Nieman, 2017). Instead, the change happens gradually
in response to growing material capabilities and a range of domestic and
international explanations (Thies & Nieman, 2017). China's case provides
an example of how material changes, such as its growing emissions and
capabilities, generate needs for constructions, namely the need for
China's role to be reviewed. However, [these changes do not squarely
grant China a particular role [because climate-leader roles are
constructed in social interaction]{.mark}]{.underline}. In this sense[,
[China's case exemplifies the constructivist ontology]{.mark}, meaning
that although the focus on material power should not be excessive, the
material forces should not be dismissed as these social constructions
are not arbitrary or independent of the actor's material
capabilities]{.underline}. I contribute to the discussion on the
boundary conditions of social interaction by demonstrating how the
**[underlying [Western-centrism can]{.mark} even [create role conflicts,
and consequently narrow the idea of who can be acknowledged as a climate
leader]{.mark}]{.underline}**. As the structures of the UNFCCC
[[reproduce global hierarchies in favor of large, Western,
English-speaking delegations]{.mark}]{.underline} (Falzon, 2021), these
structures decrease recognition of China's climate leadership efforts
and [constrain the roles that China can play [in global climate
governance]{.mark}.]{.underline} Therefore, this dissertation argues
that taking the conflicts surrounding China's role for granted, and
assuming that China's adherence to a developing country identity equates
with its unwillingness to lead, creates [a narrow view of China's role
in global climate governance in the post-Paris era and may even
contribute to perpetuating a Western-centric perception of climate
leadership]{.underline}. I do not wish to emphasize the structure as a
determinant of China's roles, but rather to [[question whether a climate
leader can have a developing country identity]{.underline}]{.mark}.
These findings open up new questions that could be answered by the
approach taken by this dissertation. An approach combining discursive
methodology with constructivist role theory would be equipped to respond
to questions about how individuals, states, and others construct their
climate leadership at the international level. For instance, future
studies could ask how constructions of climate leadership vary at the
individual level between different US presidents, or at the state level
between democratic India and authoritarian China. Furthermore, moving
beyond the state-centrism of this research, this approach could be
applied to explore whether [the Western-centrism underlying global
climate governance becomes visible in recognizing the climate leadership
of non-state actors]{.underline}. As complex challenges such as climate
change call for more functionally specific roles (Harnisch et al.,
2011), future studies should pay more attention to understanding changes
and tensions particularly between different auxiliary roles and between
master and auxiliary roles. The identified role conflict between climate
leadership and developing country identity raises important questions
that deserve further attention beyond China and climate leadership.
Using the approach adopted by this dissertation, one could research
whether similar conflicts are visible in the role expectations of other
powerful actors outside the Global North. Besides China, this question
would also be relevant for other emerging nations such as Brazil and
India, or SIDS, for instance. Analyzing these potential conflicts could
enhance understanding of the kind of underlying assumptions that exist
about the roles that can be played simultaneously, and whether these
assumptions vary between the Global North and South. In particular,
[[the Global South expectations deserve more attention]{.mark} in order
[to raise awareness of]{.mark} how **[the structures favoring the Global
North]{.mark}** are reflected in them]{.underline}. The Global South
role expectations would be particularly interesting in China's case
because if it wishes to uphold the role of leader of the developing
world, these expectations will have a strong impact on China's roles. In
this sense, the self-declared leadership of China studied in Article III
could be supplemented with a further analysis of external recognition.
Given China's role as the leader of the Global South, an analysis of the
LDCs' or G-77 nations' expectations of China could improve understanding
of the variation within the Global South; not all developing or emerging
countries have similar expectations of China. Future analyses of
external expectations of China, based for instance on voting patterns in
other international institutions or attitudes toward China's investments
in the Global South, could help answer Torney's (2019) thorny question
about how followership materializes. Here, role theory could tackle the
challenges involved in identifying followership in the climate
leadership literature (e.g., Torney 2019) by analyzing whether
followership can be detected as part of an actor's egoexpectations, or
whether it remains a counter-role that prospective leaders seek to
altercast onto others. This dissertation provides insights into role
expectations based on forma lcommunication in the UNFCCC context, such
as the high-level segments. These choices inevitably influence the
outcome as roles are defined in a context, which makes them "limited in
time and scope" and "dependent on the group's structure and purpose"
(Harnisch, 2011a, p. 8). Supplementing the analysis presented here with
interviews would have allowed the inclusion of more informal
communication than the exploration of national speeches at the annual
COPs. Interviews could have complemented the analysis of alter
expectations in particular because they might have revealed more
explicit expectations of China. Hence, combining formal and informal
communication in future investigations could provide a more
comprehensive view of the alter expectations. Including interviews might
also serve to broaden the ego expectations. However, given China's
authoritarian regime, I believe that the value of interviews in
supplementing the speeches and documents prepared by the
foreign-policy-making elite would not have been as great as in the case
of alter expectations. My choice to approach climate leadership through
role theory naturally limits the questions that can be asked about
China's climate leadership. [This approach enabled me to answer several
"how" questions in depth and focus on the discursive understanding of
China's climate leadership, but not analyze leadership
implementation.]{.underline} Yet analyzing the roles of states furthers
understanding of how they wish to be seen by others and how they
position themselves vis-à-vis others. I reviewed the credibility of
China's leadership mainly based on the social interaction between China
and its significant others and how these others accepted China's claims.
Due to the recency of China's promises announced in 2021, these claims
have not yet materialized, which means that fully analyzing the
implementation dimension at this stage would not have been possible. By
including this dimension, future studies could compare role play and
role performance, and duly evaluate the credibility of China's
leadership claims more comprehensively. Nevertheless, from the
perspective of global inequality, the focus on the discursive
constructions of reality is justified because generalizations and
processesof inclusion and exclusion make these discursive constructions
"important realms of power" (Hajer, 1995, p. 21). During this project,
ADA enabled me to immersemyself in language nuances that appear to be
shared. Due to these nuances, sharedunderstandings are easily
interpreted ambiguously (e.g., Hajer, 1995). However, atleast in the way
I applied ADA, its limitation lay in its inability to uncover what
wasnot discussed. The method did not provide an explicit analytical way
to pinpointwhat is excluded from the storylines. On the one hand, I see
this limitation asresulting from my state-centric focus; with a broader
focus, the storylines at theelite policy-making level could have been
compared to storylines subscribed to by67scientists and journalists, for
instance, to better justify what was omitted at the statelevel. On the
other hand, this limitation derived from the aim to go beyond
thecritique of the Global North. In line with this objective, the aim
was to find commonground rather than exclusions. ADA allowed the search
for this ground in thecontext of the discursive complexity of global
climate governance. It facilitatedfinding both shared and conflicting
ways to talk about [[climate]{.underline}]{.mark} leadership
betweenactors and groups with differing interests and worldviews. In
another researchdesign, exclusions could be made more visible by
applying Critical DiscourseAnalysis (CDA) instead of ADA. CDA strives to
understand the underlyingideological agendas and discrimination (Leipold
et al., 2019), which would help to comprehend how [[discourses reproduce
historical inequalities and whose voices go unheard in the role
expectations of climate leadership]{.mark}.]{.underline} More generally,
by incorporating more critical reviews of Western-centrism, the climate
leadership literature could intensify its contribution to the
discussions on climate responsibility and justice. [Understanding
climate [leadership based on the experiences of developed countries
affects the efficiency]{.mark} and ambition [of climate leadership as
their leadership has proved to be inadequate and constructed on
favorable framings. Due to weakly defined climate-leadership
goals]{.mark} [and the]{.mark} excessive [focus on the negotiation
phase]{.mark}]{.underline}, the ambition of prospective leaders is not
linked to the Paris Agreement goals. Therefore, the aspirations of an
effective climate leader need to be defined in various contexts, in
accordance with the Paris Agreement goals, in order to emphasize the
relevance of climate leadership as an analytical concept. Such an
application will most likely decrease the number of recognized climate
leaders, but will steer efforts toward the 1.5˚C path and not remain at
the level of comparing the ambition of different actors. In addition, it
should be emphasized that the leadership expectations of the Paris
Agreement not only concern mitigation but also taking the lead in
climate finance and sustainable lifestyles, consumption, and production.
Analyzing discursive constructions of reality serve as a reminder of the
dangers of considering a perception to be universal. Such narrowness can
often be observed in the media during the annual COPs: the roles of
different actors are defined from the perspective of a particular
worldview. For instance, the newspaper headlines quoted at the beginning
of this dissertation define the negotiation position of developing
countries and China from a Finnish perspective. However, the positioning
might be entirely different if the headlines were written from a
Burundian or Chinese perspective. These differences must be recognized
in order to find common ground on climate leadership. In this
dissertation, common ground between [[emerging and developed
nations]{.mark} was mainly found in the context of climate solutions,
but the two sides [differ]{.mark}ed [in]{.mark} particular in [their
perceptions of climate problematization and leadership
responsibilities]{.mark}]{.underline}. In addition to cooperation and
more ambitious climate action by China and other nations, achieving the
Paris goals will require finding and building shared understanding,
especially between the Global North and South. [We all need China's
climate action to increase. To this end, ignoring and [rejecting the
importance of the Global South]{.mark} identity for China [is
not]{.mark} necessarily [the]{.mark} quickest or [**easiest strategy to
enhance**]{.mark} the [**global cooperation**]{.mark} needed to achieve
carbon neutrality]{.underline}.

### 2NC/1NR\-\--Global South Key

#### Global South climate leadership is key to climate change mitigation

**Frischmann, '22** (Chad Frischmann is the CEO and Founder of
Regenerative Intelligence, a global solutions activator super powered by
a network of world class system thinkers and doers.) Frischmann, C.J.,
Mehra, M., Alvarez, J. et al. The Global South is the climate movement's
unsung leader. Nat. Clim. Chang. 12, 410--412 (2022).
<https://doi.org/10.1038/s41558-022-01351-3> - [GL]{.mark}

[Numerous [examples highlight leadership and real climate action in the
Global South]{.mark}. With financial support from and partnership with
countries in the Global North, [this leadership can be a cornerstone
for]{.mark} getting on track to [meeting the Paris
Agreement]{.mark}.]{.underline} The headlines from the recent COP26
climate conference in Glasgow cast countries from the Global South in a
starkly negative light. The media criticized India's announcement that
it will meet its emissions targets only by 2070 (ref. 1), two decades
behind the United States and European Union, as well as Nigeria's pledge
to reach net zero by 2060 and plans to heavily use natural gas2 as a
transitional fuel until 2040. The Washington Post called Brazil a
"climate antagonist"3, while China --- the world's second-largest
economy and top greenhouse gas (GHG) emitter --- was largely absent from
the conference4. [These talking points overlook the ambitious actions
already taken by the Global South to cut GHG emissions and enhance
natural carbon sinks, despite the socioeconomic cards stacked against
it.]{.underline} Stopping climate change requires collaboration among
all stakeholders. [To foster such collaboration, high-income countries
must acknowledge the contributions of low- and middle-income countries
towards climate action, while working with and learning from them to
accelerate climate-positive practices and policies]{.underline}. Many
nations in the Global South face the most dire impacts of global
warming, yet emit only a fraction of the world's GHGs. Per capita CO2
emissions are 1.8 metric tons per year (t yr−1) in India, and 2 t yr−1
in Brazil --- far lower than the global average of 4.8 t yr−1. Even
China's annual per capita emissions of 7.4 t are less than half those of
the United States, which clock in at 15.2 t (ref. 5). [Furthermore,
[these nations are being asked to leapfrog fossil fuels on their paths
to development]{.mark} --- something no Global North country has ever
even attempted --- [without receiving the capacity-building and
financial support they will need]{.mark} to do so. Despite these
challenges, Global South nations are mobilizing innovative solutions to
cut emissions across all sectors.]{.underline}

Food, agriculture and oceans Although destructive practices in the
Global South, such as the destruction of Brazil's rainforest and
overfishing in China, have rightfully earned the ire of the world,
broadly speaking [[the Global South performs better than the Global
North within]{.mark} these sectors.]{.underline} First, [many Global
South [food systems]{.mark} have substantially smaller footprints than
those in the Global North. The Indian diet, for example, is largely
plant rich]{.underline} --- Indians consume only 5 kg of meat per person
per year, compared with the world average of 43 kg. [Smallholder
farmers, who are key to scaling regenerative agricultural practices with
lower climate impacts [and]{.mark} carbon drawdown potential, are well
represented in the Global South,]{.underline} producing 30--34% of food
grown there6. Moreover[, many Global South countries have adopted
resource-efficient methods of growing food]{.underline}; Brazil, for
example, has 77.3% of its total irrigated area under micro-irrigation,
[compared with 56.5% in the United States. The Global South is also
leading the way with respect to [the oceans]{.mark}. Of 15 countries on
the High Level Panel for Sustainable Ocean Economy, which aims to
sustainably manage 100% of its member nations' waters, more than half
are from the Global South. [Brazil prohibits bottom trawling]{.mark}
below 600 m in most of its national waters --- protecting both marine
fauna and the carbon stored in the seabed[. In contrast, the European
Union has the highest bottom-trawling footprint]{.mark} in the world.
Many [Global South nations are also turning their coastal waters into
carbon sinks through seaweed farming]{.mark}, a powerful ocean-based
climate solution.]{.underline} In 2018, China and Indonesia alone were
responsible for around 85% of global seaweed production. Meanwhile,
[coastal communities in Africa are building seaweed farms as well. In
addition to the carbon benefits, these are providing jobs, food and
capacity building for local communities]{.underline}.

Transportation and energy [[The Global South is also well positioned to
lead the 'race to zero emissions' in the transportation and energy
sectors]{.mark}. India has the fourth longest rail network in the
world10,]{.underline} and electrified more than 5,000 km of rail as part
of its plan to electrify its entire network by 2030 (ref. 11). It plans
to add more than 1,000 km of metro line by 2030, and has 9 high-speed
rail lines in development10. Only 6% of Indians own a car and 35% use
bicycles, compared with 88% and 13% in the United States,
respectively12. [Meanwhile, [renewable energy sources produce 37% of
India's electricity, compared with less than 20% in the European Union
and the United States]{.mark}13]{.underline}.Brazil is the birthplace of
bus rapid transit (BRT), an innovative public transit system that more
than 20 million passengers across South America ride each day. BRT has
had a significant impact on congestion and air pollution in many cities,
contributing to reduced emissions14 (Fig. 1). [[Brazil is]{.mark} also
[the world leader in biofuel and waste energy use]{.mark}]{.underline}
--- 50% of its total energy supply comes from these sources15, and 75%
of its light vehicle fleet uses ethanol fuel[. [China has more than half
of the world's electric cars]{.mark}, more than 90% of its electric
buses and trucks, and the largest electric-vehicle-charging
infrastructure in the world16. It also has the most high-speed rail
lines17]{.underline}. Only 17% of Chinese people own cars, and 25% use
bicycles12. [While it currently meets only 28% of its energy demand
using renewable sources18, China is making strides to further green its
electricity grid19, which could one day make it home to the world's
largest zero-emissions transportation sector.]{.underline}

Policy and investment [Many of these [innovations and infrastructure
improvements]{.mark} have been driven by government policy and
investment from both public and private sources. The impetus for acting
in the Global South reflects the need to address economic development by
protecting and sustainably using natural resources, improving
livelihoods and promoting more inclusive growth]{.underline}. Moving
ahead by adopting climate solutions to leapfrog fossil-fuel development
helps achieve these goals, while avoiding the need to decarbonize their
economies in the future, and [[position]{.mark}s [countries as leaders
in a global green economy]{.mark}]{.underline}. On the policy side,
Nigeria followed COP26 by passing its Climate Change Act, which includes
key provisions for a REDD+ registry, a climate change fund, natural
capital accounts, carbon budgets and a national council on climate
change. The act seeks to provide a legal framework for how Nigeria plans
to respond to the Paris Agreement by committing to protecting its forest
resources, improving sustainable transport and abating methane
emissions. [New [financial instruments are being developed across the
Global South]{.mark} to stimulate further green innovation]{.underline}.
For example, Pakistan may begin to issue nature performance bonds, which
could help solve the sovereign debt crisis by tying it to measurable
climate and biodiversity indicators20. In 2022, Chile made history by
issuing the first sovereign sustainability-linked bond, with investor
demand four times higher than its initial US\$2 billion issuance21. Many
countries in the Global South have also made significant policy
interventions and investments in education access and reproductive
health services over the past few decades. These rights-based
interventions can help avoid around \~40 Gt of GHG emissions by 2050
(ref. 22). In India and Nigeria, secondary school completion rates
increased from 10.9% to 37.2% and from 11.5% to 45%, respectively,
between 2000 and 2017 (ref. 23). Meanwhile, between 1990 and 2019,
voluntary family-planning uptake increased throughout Central and
Southern Asia and sub-Saharan Africa, and in Latin America and the
Caribbean. In addition to initiatives led by the Global South, recently
launched international initiatives, such as the Build Back Better World
(B3W) initiative and the Just Energy Transition Fund, may also play a
crucial role supporting solution implementation. However, these types of
initiatives are reminiscent of the unmet pledge made by high-income
countries at COP15 in Copenhagen to raise US\$100 billion per year in
climate finance by 2020. The success of these new initiatives relies on
evasion of the flaws that marred the realization of the COP15 pledge for
over a decade24.

Working in partnership [Climate mitigation and adaptation solutions in
the Global South are critical to stopping global warming and should
undoubtedly be a focal point of efforts to curb emissions, enhance
carbon drawdown and promote climate resiliency. [Countries in the Global
South offer perhaps **the greatest potential** for the rapid
implementation of natural climate solutions and are ripe markets for
carbon-leapfrogging technologies.]{.mark}]{.underline} There is also an
acute need to act quickly to avoid and/or adapt to climate-related
challenges already being felt. [However, the Global North should
recognize Global South countries for the strides they have taken,
particularly [given that the Global North's historic and current
emissions form the bulk of atmospheric GHG concentrations]{.mark}.
Countries in the Global North should further acknowledge the deep
historical economic inequities that contribute to the differentiated
implementation of climate solutions.]{.underline} They must recommit to
the 'common but differentiated responsibilities' principle formalized in
the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change in 1992 and,
while acknowledging existing progress, provide tangible financial
support for further advancing climate solutions. Climate change affects
us all. Partnerships at local, regional and international scales are key
to the successful implementation of climate solutions, and the strongest
partnerships are those built on mutual respect and shared goals25. To
achieve the ambitious 1.5 °C warming target, we will need all hands
working together in an equitable way. Only through true partnership
between the Global North and Global South, and shared recognition of
advances made in countries around the world, can we ever hope to advance
climate action.

#### China climate leadership fills vacuum left by the US

**Sun, 25** (Dr Yixian Sun is a Senior Lecturer-Associate Professor-in
International Development at the University of Bath. He holds a Ph.D.
and Master\'s degree in International Relations / Political Science,

[[The second Trump administration]{.mark} has announced various
anti-climate policies under its "America first" strategy. Leaving the
Paris agreement, kicking off a trade war, shutting down USAid and

international reputation but will undermine the global effort to combat

forward global climate governance"]{.mark}.]{.underline} But to take on
this leadership, Beijing must first strengthen [China]{.mark}'s domestic
policies along with its support for climate action in the global south.
[The [country has made remarkable progress on clean energy]{.mark} and
its carbon emissions may peak this year]{.underline}. But more than 60%
of the electricity generated in the country still comes from coal, and
it remains unclear how fast the government plans to phase out fossil
fuels. Meanwhile, some provincial governments are still issuing permits
to add new coal-fired power plants. There are things China can do almost

very ambitious pledges to reduce its emissions for the coming decades
ahead of this year's UN climate conference (Cop30) to be held in

administration. [What China ends up pledging will have a profound impact

emissions from their peak level by at least 30%. [This is]{.mark} still
[achievable if the country can maintain its current progress in
renewables]{.mark}.]{.underline} Despite the missed deadline, there are
some positive noises coming from Beijing. In a recent high-level meeting
organised by the UN secretary general, [[Xi announced that
China's]{.mark} next set of emission [reduction targets]{.mark},
covering the period up to 2035, [will cover all economic sectors and all
greenhouse gases]{.mark}.]{.underline} This will be a major progress
compared to China's previous pledges, which only covered carbon dioxide
(China is the world's biggest emitter of the potent greenhouse gas
methane, for instance) and did not integrate national targets into
individual sectoral policies. [[China has also been instrumental in
bridging gaps between developed and developing countries]{.mark} in
recent international talks]{.underline}. This was especially the case
during negotiations at Cop29 last year in Baku, Azerbaijan, for a new
global climate finance goal. [Climate finance, in this context, refers
to providing developing countries with the resources [to help them
reduce their emissions and adapt to climate change]{.mark}. [China still
has developing country status]{.mark}]{.underline} in the UN's climate
change convention and, as such, has no official obligation to provide
international climate finance. [[Despite this, it has already]{.mark}
provided or [helped raise around]{.mark} US\$[24.5 billion]{.mark}
(£18.32 billion) [for clean energy, disaster recovery]{.mark} and other
climate actions [in developing countries]{.mark}.]{.underline} That
makes it the world's fifth-largest climate finance donor according to
some estimates. But for this investment to have a lasting impact,
Beijing needs to be more transparent about where its funding goes and
how projects are financed. It should also get local people more involved
in designing and implementing the projects it funds. [China]{.mark}
should also play a major role in reforming the global financial system
to make it aligned with the Paris agreement. [As a strong supporter of
green finance, it [can influence upcoming international talks]{.mark}
such as the Financing for Development conference in Seville, as well as
the UN's negotiations on international tax cooperation. As co-chair of
the G20's sustainable finance working group, China also has the
opportunity [to push for more funding to support net
zero]{.mark}]{.underline}. [[China is]{.mark} by far [the world's
biggest producer of renewables, batteries, electric vehicles]{.mark} and
many other clean technologies, and is in a unique position to supply
them affordably]{.underline}. While it has already exported lots of
these products, many developing countries still don't have the know-how
or the basic infrastructure to make the most of them (solar farms are of
limited use if you don't have a battery capable of storing the

global climate leadership at risk, China has the chance to step
up**]{.mark}. As an emerging superpower with advantages in clean

### 2NC/1NR\-\--US Climate Leadership Fails

#### The AFF can't revive climate leadership

#### Trump Thumps.

**Zehndorfer, 22** (Elesa Zehndorfer PhD is the Research Officer for
