The role of anti-science rhetoric in right-wing populism

[Climate change denial has been found to correlate positively with
right- wing ideological political beliefs, according to a recent
multi-nation study eL e S A ZeHnD o RFeR12 6 by Jylhӓ & Hellmer (2020),
with political narratives that focus on climate change observed to be a
polarising subject (e.g. Whitmarsh & Cornel, 2017). In embracing this
denial, Trump was able to appeal directly to a conservative base.
Populist right-wing parties, usually led by a charismatic individual,
also tend to dismiss climate change (e.g. Lockwood, 2018, Forchtner,
Kroneder &Wetzel, 2018) as part of a wider strategy that embraces
socially conserva- tive ideologies (Mudde, 2007).]{.underline} RW
charismatic populism tends to embrace the communication of traditional
values whilst romanticising nationalistic notions, usually to the
detriment of minority groups. Given that RW populism tends to focus on
denigrating attacks on the 'other' -- that is, minority groups (such as
Jews in 1930s Germany, and Muslims and Mexicans under the Trump
administration) (e.g. Lobban et al., 2020), [the [conscious effort to
reject liberal, egalitarian values]{.mark} might [represent]{.mark} not
only an ideological approach but an associated, [conscious strategic
effort to appeal to a conservative base]{.mark}. Charisma, populism and
climate change denial [Right-wing populism]{.mark}, interestingly,
[is]{.mark} [generally characterised by]{.mark} a scepti- cism towards
climate policy and a [far lower readiness to support action on climate
change]{.mark} (e.g. Lockwood, 2018, McCright et al., 2016) with a
recent 12-country study of 13 right-wing populist parties demonstrating
a clear anti-environmental bias (Gemenis et al., 2012).]{.underline}
Further, Hamilton and Saito (2015) and Shao (2017) found intra-party
differences in the US Republican Party where Tea Party members (a
far-right faction of the Republican Party) held far more pronounced
views that related to climate scepticism than their more moderate
conservative counterparts. It is interesting that 'aversion to wealth
distribution partly mediates the correlation between Trump support and
climate change denial' (Jylhӓ & Hellmer (2020, p. 3) and that climate
change denial also correlates with racist and anti-immigrant views,
Social Dominance Orientation and Right- Wing Authoritarianism (ibid., p.
12). [They are also most likely to hold anti- immigration views, which
Trump's aggressive anti-immigration stance courts and amplifies
powerfully.8 It also appears to be [linked with a pro-conspiracy
mindset]{.mark} (Lewandowsky, Oberauer & Gignac, 2013) and is predicted
by anti-establishment attitudes,]{.underline} T He W e A P o nIS AT I o
n o F CL IM AT e CH A n Ge 1 2 7 traditional values and anti-egalitarian
practices, which in turn were found to be positive predictors of
pseudoscientific beliefs (Jylhӓ & Hellmer 2020, p. 8). [Together, the
conflagration of these observed beliefs forms a strong likelihood of
climate change and anti-science denial (Rutjens, Sutton & van der Lee,
2017) alongside a willingness to instead invest in, and welcome,
conspiracies and pseudo-science. [Trump's rhetoric clearly]{.mark} plays
to and [amplifies these]{.mark} preferences and [descriptors whilst
seemingly also betraying a personal tendency to conform to these
cognitive descriptors]{.mark} (e.g. 'Any and all weather events are used
by the GLOBAL WARMING HOAXSTERS to justify higher taxes to save our
planet! They don't believe it! \$\$\$\$' (@realdonaldtrump, 26 January
2014).]{.underline} The administration utilises propagandist techniques
to amplify Trump's environmental narrative; a recent Brown University
doctoral study found that roughly 25% of all cli- mate change denial
tweets are in fact produced by mechanised bot activity (Marlow et al.,
2020). Research also identifies that the Big Five trait of 'openness'
(McCrae & Costa, 2008) correlates negatively with climate change denial,
likely because it reflects a greater acceptance of the status quo and
predicts acceptance of group-based hierarchies (Jylhӓ, 2016), whilst
dampening a readiness to reject novel, complex ideas (such as those that
emerge throughout the sci- entific process). Since the early 1990s,
there is evidence that conservative think tanks explicitly formed to
counter environmental decision-making (De Pryck & Gemenne, 2017),
engaging with a wider network of actors in manufacturing uncertainty
(e.g. Dunlap, 2013) such as energy giants Koch Industries and the George
C. Marshall Institute think tank. Thus, the Trump administration is
capitalising on the anti-environmental work of a powerful, existing
sub-culture (the Republican Party and associated actors). The party has
(as it currently appears) largely ceded control to Trump, and it is
Trump who is now capitalising on that work, and on the embedded
anti-environmental beliefs of its followers. Science vs. rhetoric
Comparing scientific consensus relating to anthropogenic climate change
with Republican commentary offers a compelling insight into the forces
that drive both beliefs: Republican senator for Kentucky, Rand Paul, for
example, recently shared his disdain for scientific evidence of climate
eL e S A ZeHnD o RFeR12 8 change (e.g. Brulle, 2018; Dunlap & McCright,
2008; Kool, 2010) by saying that: I think this debate has become so
dumbed down beyond belief \... We have real data \[for\] about 100
years. So somebody tell me what 100 years' data is in an earth that is
4.6 billion years old? My guess is that the conclusions you make from
that are not conclusive. Politicians might make guesses about science,
but scientists, of course, do not. Anthropogenic climate change simply
describes 'a statistically significant variation in either the mean
state of the climate or in its variability, persisting for an extended
period' (VijayaVenkataRaman, Iniyan & Goic, 2012, p. 879). [It is
apolitical but has been rendered political, at least in the perceptions
of [right-wing voters]{.mark}, who now [view climate change as a
'liberal' concept]{.mark}. The evidence for, [and]{.mark} broad
scientific consensus around, the concept of climate change is immense
(e.g. NASA, n.d.), yet it runs counter to RW populist ambitions to
acknowledge it. Creationism vs. climate [change Creationist education
policy has grown notably during Trump's presidency]{.mark} (National Law
Review, 2019), representing a favouring of Christian prot- estant
evangelism and a courting of faith-based agencies by the administration
[at the same time as the scientific and academic organisations have been
attacked and vilified]{.mark}]{.underline}. The strategic favouring of
religion is a common feature of authoritarian and totalitarian states,
as is the denigration of science (Linz, 2004). The movement to support
creationism and faith-based academic approaches in favour of scientific
content has received high-level support within the Republican Party;
former Vice President Mike Pence and Secretary of Education Betsy DeVos
both vigorously supported the teaching of creationist theory, falsely
communicating the supposition that creationism simply offers a competing
theory to evolution. Both have furthered this narrative despite a US
Supreme Court ruling that forcing a requirement to teach creationism in
schools would represent a clear violation of the First Amendment's
Establishment Clause (a clause which exists to prevent 'sponsorship,
financial support, and active involvement of the sovereign in religious
activity').11 T He W e A P o nIS AT I o n o F CL IM AT e CH A n Ge 1 2 9
The weaponisation of truth: religion vs. science Trump's relationship
with religion, most notably the Evangelical Christian movement, remained
a powerful boon to his continued position as POTUS, even in the face of
a recent impeachment (a reported 81% of US Christian Evangelicals voted
for Trump in 2016).12 This enthusiastic support, confusing to many
observers, might hold its roots in American Evangelical history. Science
denial first gained traction in the context of Darwinism in the late
1800s in the USA, and less than a century ago, teaching evolutionary
science was still illegal in Texas. The GOP has historically formed a
close bond with the Protestant Evangelical movement -- a bond that the
current administration is exploiting effectively. White American
Evangelical Protestants are most likely amongst all religious groups to
be sceptical of climate change, with only 27% believing that climate
change is real (Jones, Cox & Navarro-Rivera, 2014). It is this sector of
the GOP votership that is often described as the 'base' of the
Republican Party (Brint & Abrutyn, 2010[). [If the campaign slogan 'Make
America Great Again' is]{.mark} to be [considered, rightly]{.mark}, a
form of [revivalist nationalism]{.mark}, [then it would]{.mark} be
sensible to [include creationism and the removal of evolution
theory]{.mark} as one key part of that patchwork American revivalist
nostalgia that the MAGA campaign has tapped so powerfully
into.]{.underline} Science, God and a cult of charisma The powerfully
symbiotic relationship that the Republican Party has historically
enjoyed with the conservative Christian right has undoubtedly
strengthened further under the Trump administration: 3 January 2020, for
example, saw the launch of Evangelicals for Trump in Florida, a key bat-
tleground state, with President Trump promoting a powerful and popular
Pentacostal mega-church evangelist named Paula White-Cain to lead the
White House Faith and Opportunity Initiative. White is a major presence
in the Evangelicals for Trump organisation, often hosting religious
prayer events where various Evangelical pastors pray openly for Trump,
sometimes even engaging in a 'laying on hands' activity when Trump
himself is present. As Trump's Presidential tenure progressed and
accusations of moral tur- pitude increased, support from mainstream
religion waned. The influential eL e S A ZeHnD o RFeR13 0 religious
publication Christianity Today, for example, denounced Trump as 'grossly
immoral' (Galli, 2019). A rejection by some in mainstream religion may
explain in part Trump's higher engagement with mega-church pastors such
as Paula White-Cain over more orthodox figures in the mainstream
Christian movement; it removes the bureaucratic checks-and-balances of
an established religious organisation, further removing limits on his
behav- iour, yet still conveys an image of a high-profile, close
relationship with religion. As the relationship has been cultivated, so
references to Trump's Messianic status have also increased; Trump, for
example, referred to his recent Covid-19 diagnosis as a 'blessing from
God' in a video posted to Twitter (@realdonaldtrump, 7 October 2020).
According to Eatwell (2004), the leader is driven by a special mission
(p. 106); he positions himself as a superman, a Messiah figure, endowed
with special powers (Weber, 1968). Trump's self-positioning as a man
endowed by supernatural qualities can thus be considered a common
charismatic leadership strategy. The 'laying on hands' imagery and the
direct equation of Trump with God himself invokes powerfully the
Weberian 'Messiah' image so sought after by the charismatic populist
leader (Weber, 1968). Controlling the narrative [[The goal of RW
charismatic populists is to control the political narrative and to
transfer expert power from]{.mark} experts (e.g. [scientists]{.mark})
[to that of his own voice]{.mark} and coterie (e.g. Homolar & Scholz,
2019). [Science]{.mark} has long been viewed as a pillar of democracy,
exerting key checks-and-balances that act to constrain (and facilitate)
executive power (Collins & Evans, 2019) -- thus it makes sense that it
[would become a natural target to]{.mark} a leader of this
kind.]{.underline}

#### Economic logic overcodes success

**Collomb**, **22** (JD Collomb is a professor of American Studies,
Université Grenoble-Alpes, France.) The Limitations of U.S. Climate
Leadership: A Realist Perspective. In: Stricof, M., Vagnoux, I. (eds)
U.S. Leadership in a World of Uncertainties. Studies of the Americas.
Palgrave Macmillan, Cham.
<https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-031-10260-8_9> - [GL]{.mark}

Realist scholars are wont to point out that, because economic growth and
wealth creation form the bedrock of a nation's latent and military
power, [public officials will tend to be reluctant to enter
[international agreements]{.mark} which, they fear, [might undermine the
ability of]{.mark} their [nations to create wealth]{.mark} (Mearsheimer
2003). [This]{.mark} fear [has been one of the factors behind
the]{.mark} frequent [lack of diligence of U.S. public officials with
regard to climate change.]{.mark}]{.underline} Until now, fossil fuels
have been identified as both the main cause of human-made climate change
and the driving force behind economic expansion in the U.S. and
elsewhere (McNeill 2016). Without exploita- tion of coal, oil, and
natural gas on a monumental scale, the indus- trialization first of
European countries, the U.S., and a few others, then of emerging
countries like China, Brazil, and India, would have been unthinkable.
According to the Energy Information Administration (2020), for instance,
80% of primary energy consumed in the U.S. in 2019 was derived from
coal, oil, and natural gas. In like manner, the use of fossil fuels,
particularly coal and oil, has laid the groundwork for China's
spectacular economic ascent over the last four decades. From a realist
standpoint, it makes little sense to expect those two geopolitical
rivals to adhere to climate agreements that have the potential to allow
one to make relative gains at the expense of the other. This trend was
very often on display when Republican Presidents were in office.
Although the evidence suggests that George H. W. Bush was more inclined
to take climate change seriously than his two Repub- lican successors,
his administration made it plain that no compromise would be made on
U.S. economic prosperity, and that preserving U.S. economic
competitiveness would be a core objective. On the one hand, he signed
the United Nations Framework Convention on Climate Change (UNFCCC) which
still serves as the main foundation for the current international
climate regime, and went on record as saying that the U.S. would
confront the climate challenge head-on, but, on the other hand, his
administration did its utmost to empty the framework of a large part of
its substance by ruling out any binding mechanism and refusing to
consider mutually agreed upon targets and timetables (Layzer 2012,
168--169). [An even more obvious illustration of this stance was Donald
Trump's quest for "energy dominance." Energy dominance meant that the
U.S. would try to go beyond energy independence, by extracting its vast
fossil fuel resources as much as possible so as to satisfy its own
needs, while being able to export some of them. This, according to its
proponents, would stimulate economic growth at home, and enhance U.S.
power and influence abroad.]{.underline} For instance, the Trump
administration hoped to boost exports of liquified natural gas (LNG) as
a means of making up for economic losses resulting from its trade war
with China, and of less- ening European dependence on Russian gas.
[Likewise, robust U.S. shale oil production made it possible to keep the
supply of oil steady even as the Trump administration slapped more
sanctions on Iran (Yergin 2020, 39--40). In a way that is reminiscent of
[realist thinking]{.mark}, President Trump [viewed economic competition
as a zero-sum game in which the U.S. was to make the most of its huge
fossil fuel resources, not just to enhance national prosperity, but also
to wield more influence overseas]{.mark}]{.underline}. This goal,
needless to say, can hardly be reconciled with bona fide U.S. climate
leadership. The attitudes of Democratic officials have been more
ambivalent. Bill Clinton, after all, did sign the Kyoto protocol in
1998, and Barack Obama was one of the main architects of the Paris
Accord. President Biden is set to make use of substantially larger
amounts of federal funding than any of his predecessors to foster the
energy transition through the Infrastructure Investment and Jobs Act
passed by the U.S. Congress in November 2021. It would be inaccurate to
argue that Democrats and Republicans are indis- tinguishable on climate
and that no action is ever taken in the U.S. [Yet, on further
examination, the [Democratic contributions to climate]{.mark} action and
[leadership have]{.mark} also [been severely limited by concerns about
relative economic gains and U.S. competitiveness.]{.mark} From 1993
onwards, the Clinton administration tried to move forward on climate,
but was thwarted by Republican opposition and by the reluc- tance of
some Democrats from coal-rich states]{.underline}. Ahead of COP3 in
Kyoto, the Clinton administration tried to lead by signaling its support
for a binding mechanism to bring about mandatory cuts in carbon
emissions. True to his word, President Clinton went on to sign the Kyoto
protocol, under which the U.S. was to pledge itself to reducing its
emissions 7% below 1990 levels by 2008, while developing countries would
not be required to cut back on their own emissions. His move sparked a
severe backlash in the U.S. Senate. The year before, at the initiative
of Democratic Senator Robert Byrd and his Republican colleague Chuck
Hagel, the Senate had passed Resolution 98 with unanimous support from
Republicans and Democrats, informing him that the Senate would not
consider ratification as long as developing countries were free to
pursue their economic development without having to lower their carbon
footprints. Senate Resolution 98 (1997) revolved mostly around concerns
about relative gains for emerging countries at the expense of the U.S.
to justify the refusal to endorse an agreement that "would result in
serious harm to the economy of the U.S." Four years later, the newly
elected Republican President, George W. Bush, formally announced that
the U.S. would not join the Kyoto protocol, also invoking the
unacceptable risk it posed to U.S. economic competitiveness. [The energy
policies implemented by the Obama administration also exemplify the
limitations imposed by concerns over economic growth on the ability of
the U.S. to exercise climate leadership. [While Barack Obama had pledged
himself to taking bold action on climate, his pres- idency saw a steep
rise in U.S. domestic production of natural gas and petroleum]{.mark},
thanks to extensive use of horizontal drilling and hydraulic fracturing
in regions like the Permian Basin and the Bakken formation, which had
begun in the mid-2000s]{.underline}. This crucial development quickly
turned the U.S. into the world's number one producer of oil and natural
gas. According to Yergin (2020, 26), this energy boom made possible
"America's manufacturing revival and its increased competitiveness in
the world economy." [Even though Barack Obama voiced his desire to exer-
cise climate leadership, [his administration was careful not to trammel
oil and gas production significantly]{.mark}.]{.underline} On the one
hand, he did impose regulations to address fugitive methane emissions
from natural gas extraction at the end of his second term, but, on the
other hand, he described natural gas as a necessary bridge fuel to a
decarbonized U.S. economy and left most of the task of regulating
fracking to state and local authorities. In 2015, he signed into law a
bipartisan Congressional compromise which lifted a ban on U.S. crude oil
exports dating back to the 1970s in return for extending tax credits for
the wind and solar industries notwithstanding opposition from
environmentalists (Yergin 2020, 55). In an effort to prove the bona
fides of the U.S. in the run up to COP21, the Obama administration opted
to predicate its climate policy on reducing the use of coal. It put
forward its Clean Power Plan, the aim of which was to use the
President's executive authority to regulate most coal-fired plants out
of existence. Because coal is the worst contributor to climate change
and because it has accounted for a large fraction of energy use in the
U.S. for more than a century, this could be seen as a way for the Obama
administration to exert climate leadership, by setting an important
example for countries such as India and China which still rely heavily
on this fuel. The Clean Power Plan, however, was never implemented
because it was put on hold by the U.S. Supreme Court in 2016 and was
later abandoned by the Trump administration. The Obama administration's
choice to focus on coal should give us pause because the coal industry
had already started to decline rapidly as a result of fierce competition
from cheap and abundant natural gas before Obama's election and has
continued to do so since, despite efforts by the Trump administration to
revive it. In other words, one may wonder if the center-piece of Barack
Obama's climate policy would have made a difference hadit been
implemented. This preference for low-hanging fruit as opposed to
potentially more destabilizing deep cuts is reminiscent of the Clinton
administration's insistence on the recognition of carbon sinks and its
demand that more flexible mechanisms be included in the Kyoto protocol
in the late 1990s (Aykut and Dahan 2015), which would have lowered the
economic burden of climate action on the U.S. economy, while making its
impact more limited. Such stances align with realist expectations that
great powers will protect their economic capabilities jealously. The way
the Biden administration will tackle shale gas production and fracking
will provide key indications as to the seriousness of its commit- ment
to substantial climate leadership. During its first year, the Biden
administration sent mixed messages. As Democrats seized all the levers
of power in Washington, they continued to tout their firm intention of
following through on their campaign pledges by adopting a three- pronged
strategy combining massive public investments in energy transition,
ambitious regulations and mandates, and an emphasis on climate change in
U.S. diplomatic dealings with the rest of the world. The Treasury
Department urged multilateral development banks to stop financing gas
exploration and production, while the administration promoted an
international pledge to cut global methane emissions by at least 30% by
2030. Given how much the U.S. economy has benefited from increased
natural gas output over the previous decade, promoting such an initia-
tive undeniably qualifies as a form of climate leadership on the part of
the Biden administration. However, the international framework needed to
make it a reality remains to be built, and other major emitters, such as
China, Russia, and India, have yet to endorse the initiative. As soon as
he became president, Joe Biden lost no time in pausing oil and gas
leasing on federal lands, but this move did not affect the many leases
on federal acreage which pre-dated his election, thus giving oil and gas
companies a lot of leeway to continue drilling on federal lands, let
alone on privately held ones where most natural gas extraction takes
place. [The Biden administration's unwillingness to discourage U.S.
liquefied natural gas exports offered another indication of its
ambivalence on this crucial issue. The energy crisis sparked by the
Russian invasion of Ukraine in early 2022 prompted the Biden
administration to lower its short-term climate ambitions. In order to
rein in inflation at home and to help European countries wean themselves
off Russian gas, it took several steps indicating that [tackling climate
change]{.mark} urgently [no longer ranked as high on]{.mark} its
[agenda]{.mark} as in early 2021]{.underline}. In late March 2022, for
example, the Biden administration pressed oil and gas companies which
controlled leases on public lands to produce more oil and gas. It went
on to call for additional E.U. imports of about 50 billion cubic meters
of U.S. LNG annually until 2030 (Fact Sheet 2022). Whether these policy
options will be quickly discontinued remains to be seen. In more ways
than one, the Biden administration's climate proposal hinges on the
success of green growth, i.e. the ability of the U.S. economy to
decouple GDP growth from a rise in emissions. Absent such a decoupling,
it would have to choose between inadequate measures and degrowth. If
recent history is any guide, climate leadership from the U.S. or any
other nation is severely hampered by their unwillingness to go beyond
efforts whose economic repercussions will be modest. This is why,
despite lofty rhetoric at large climate gatherings, the policies put in
place to mitigate climate change are very often found wanting. For
example, Liu and Raftery (2021) have found that nations' formal pledges
under the Paris Accord would need to be 80% stronger than current plans
for them to be able to meet existing goals. A report from the UN
Environment Programme (2020) suggested that the Nationally Determined
Contributions (NDCs) put forward within the framework of the Paris
Accord would need to be made three times more ambitious for the world to
have a chance to meet the 2 °C target by 2100, and five times more
ambitious to meet the even more virtuous 1.5 °C target. Sachs (2019) has
argued that as deeper cuts become necessary, the appetite for climate
action may subside accordingly[. This does not necessarily mean that the
[U.S. government]{.mark} will show no interest in exercising climate
leadership, but its [willingness]{.mark} to do so is likely to be
[constrained by the short-term economic cost of substantial
action.]{.mark}]{.underline}

#### Even without Trump, US is uniquely bad at climate leadership

**Osaka** **'22**, Shannon Osaka is a climate reporter covering policy,
culture, and science for The Washington Post, who received a master's
degree in human geography and published academic papers on climate
change perception and modeling. 1-6-2022. \"Is the US uniquely bad at
tackling climate change?\", Grist,
<https://grist.org/politics/is-american-democracy-uniquely-bad-at-tackling-climate-change/> -
[GL]{.mark}

A Democratic president was in the White House. The Democratic Party held
a majority of seats in both the House of Representatives and the Senate.
But a single senator --- a moderate Democrat from West Virginia ---
blocked the White House's preferred climate plan. No, this wasn't 2021
--- the year was 1993: Jurassic Park had just been released, Bill
Clinton was president, and atmospheric carbon dioxide was only 357 parts
per million (it's 415 ppm today). Senator Robert Byrd of fossil-fuel
laden West Virginia was the chair of the Senate Appropriations
committee, and without his support, the Clinton administration couldn't
pass a tax on carbon emissions to address climate change. The White
House opted to support an energy tax instead, which passed the House
but, faced with substantial opposition and fossil-fuel lobbying, never
became law.

It was the first climate policy failure of many. Four years later, Byrd
spearheaded a resolution that prevented the U.S. from ratifying the
Kyoto Protocol, an international treaty to cut greenhouse gas emissions.
[Other efforts to pass climate legislation stalled nearly every year
after. Indeed,]{.underline} [[the last three decades of U.S. climate
policy look like a graveyard of failed bills]{.underline}]{.mark}:
[Carbon taxes have died on the Senate floor and been torched by attack
ads. Cap-and-trade systems have been endorsed --- and then]{.underline}
[[abandoned]{.underline}]{.mark} --- [[by Republicans and Democrats
alike]{.underline}]{.mark}. [According to the Climate Change Performance
Index, the [U.S. is 55th in the world]{.mark} when it comes to climate
policy; another analysis by Yale University and Columbia University
ranked the country 24th for environmental performance]{.underline}. Now,
as Democrats struggle to regroup after current West Virginia Senator Joe
Manchin's refusal to support President Joe Biden's landmark climate and
social welfare bill, it seems to be happening again. The U.S. is within
reach of passing climate policy, but perilously close to falling short.
Biden's giant climate bill --- known as the Build Back Better Act --- is
still in play. Democrats have vowed to try and pass it regardless of
Manchin's stance, while the West Virginia senator has said publicly that
the climate sections of the bill may be easier to reach agreement on
than, say, the Child Tax Credit. But earlier this week, Manchin also
claimed that there have been "no negotiations" about the bill. For the
moment, Build Back Better looks like a grim bookend to decades of
inaction on climate change[. So what's wrong with the U.S. political
system? Is [American democracy uniquely incapable of tackling global
warming]{.mark}? "In most countries around the world, it is extremely
difficult to pass climate reform," said Matto Mildenberger, a professor
of political science at the University of California, Santa Barbara.
But, he added, there are a few things that make dealing with global
warming uniquely difficult in the United States; not impossible, but
very hard.]{.underline} In his book, *Carbon Captured*, Mildenberger
argues that the most difficult part about [passing climate policy is
that [fossil fuel]{.mark} interests enjoy something he calls "double
representation."]{.underline} That means that they are [[represented on
both the left]{.underline}]{.mark}, **[through labor unions and
industrial workers]{.underline}**, [[and on the
right]{.underline}]{.mark} **[through business interests]{.underline}**.
This "double representation" has been the death knell of climate
policies. That's because in most democracies, it's easier to block
change than to create it. And the United States government, with its
separation of powers baked into the Constitution, offers many more
opportunities for blocking than other democracies. "The United States
has a lot of what in political science are called '**[[veto
points]{.mark},]{.underline}**'" Mildenberger explained. "There's a lot
of different individuals in different places that [[can block a
policy]{.underline}]{.mark}, all the way from having a majority in the
House of Representatives to having a really conservative Supreme Court."
Those "veto points" have been visible in many of the U.S.'s major
climate flops to date. [In 2009, a cap-and-trade bill, the centerpiece
of President Barack Obama's climate strategy, died [in the
Senate]{.mark} after it became clear that Democrats could not rustle up
60 "yes" votes to overcome the filibuster. (The filibuster: a veto
point.) Later, Obama's back-up plan --- an order to require utilities to
switch over to clean sources of electricity --- was challenged [in the
courts and]{.mark} never implemented. (The courts: another veto point.)
Then, [in]{.mark} 2017, President Donald Trump announced that he would
pull the United States out of the Paris climate agreement, even though
the treaty was non-binding. ([Executive action]{.mark}: yet another veto
point.) Every time climate advocates have tried to push through change,
the story has been distinctly American: veto point, veto point, veto
point. The U.S. electoral system doesn't help much either. The United
States has [a presidential system]{.mark}, rather than a parliamentary
system]{.underline} --- meaning that the president, or head of state,
does not necessarily belong to the same party as the majority of
Congress. [That [means]{.mark} two branches of government are often
pulling in different directions, [dooming bills]{.mark} to a no man's
land of inaction.]{.underline} Studies have shown that parliamentary
systems are generally quicker to establish climate-friendly policies:
Many [[parliamentary democracies like the United Kingdom, Japan, and
Germany have already managed to establish carbon prices or similar
carbon-cutting measures]{.underline}]{.mark}, but there are exceptions,
like the climate policy-averse Australia. Then there's the U.S.'s
single-member House districts which create Congresspeople more focused
on representing their geographical areas than toeing the party line.
Taken together, the system creates what Roger Karapin calls
"centrifugal" forces that separate and divide legislators. "We have 50
states, a huge territory, and a huge population," said Karapin, a
professor of political science at Hunter College, City University of New
York. "By their nature, members of Congress are appealing to their base
back home --- and those bases are far apart." Karapin says the makeup of
Congress tilts the country toward fossil fuel interests: Two senators
per state means that states dependent on oil and gas or coal have
disproportionate weight while less populous states gain outsized
representation in Congress. Several of those less populous states ---
Wyoming, Alaska, the Dakotas --- also have substantial fossil fuel
reserves. Combine those barriers with an extremely polarized political
system and a powerful fossil fuel industry that can use cash to
influence politicians, and the United States' idea of democracy doesn't
look like a great place to enact positive climate policies.

#### BUT attempts to revive leadership propagate violent structures of racial and gendered hierarchies. 

**Stephens, '24** (Jennie C. Stephens is the Dean's Professor of
Sustainability Science & Policy, School of Public Policy & Urban Affairs
at Northeastern University); Stephens, J. C. (2024). The dangers of
masculine technological optimism: Why feminist, antiracist values are
essential for social justice, economic justice, and climate justice.
Environmental Values, 33(1), 58-70.
https://doi.org/10.1177/09632719231208752 - [GL]{.mark}

Technological optimism and climate isolationism

Recognizing the gendered realities of climate policy and climate
decision-making, this section reviews technological optimism and climate
isolationism to masculinity. Since the 1980s and 1990s, when climate
change first emerged as a global challenge, leading voices advocating
for action focused on the need to invest in understanding better the
science of climate change and in technological innovation to reduce the
impacts (Stephens & Markusson, 2018). [Technological optimism has
distorted climate policy and climate action in countries throughout the
world because the [science and engineering focus has dominated climate
discourse]{.mark} (Stephens, 2009]{.underline}). Review of the history
of climate action in the United States demonstrates that [the [US has
been a climate technology leader but a climate policy
laggard]{.mark}]{.underline} (Stephens, 2009). A major [[consequence of
this masculine technological optimism has been climate
isolationism]{.mark}. Climate isolationism refers to the narrow
technocratic lens that is prevalent in patriarchal decision-making
around both climate mitigation and climate adaptation]{.underline}. When
climate isolationism is applied to climate mitigation, decarbonization
is usually the goal (Geels et al., 2017), carbon accounting is the
primary metric, and incentives and costs of a variety of different
mitigating technologies are often projected and compared (Auel &
Cassady, 2016). [When [climate isolationism is]{.mark} applied to
climate adaptation, a [disproportionate focus on investing in technical
infrastructure]{.mark} (i.e., sea walls [and]{.mark} drought-resistant
crops) often [detracts]{.mark} attention and [investment from social
innovation]{.mark} and social changes [that could enhance climate
resilience]{.mark} (Rodima-Taylor et al., 2012).]{.underline} The
narrowness of climate isolationism results in limited opportunities for
people to connect and engage (Peterson et al., 2015). [The technocratic
focus limits public discourse because it excludes people for whom these
abstract, scientific terms, or the technological details may not be
meaningful and it makes the challenge seem distant and unapproachable
(Stephens, 2020). Not only does [this very technical way of discussing
climate change]{.mark} resonate with only a small subgroup of society,
it also often [projects the need for sacrifice]{.mark} and hardship
[rather than highlighting]{.mark} benefits and [opportunities]{.mark}
(Peeters et al., 2019).]{.underline} Climate isolationism is also
exclusive because many proposed technological "solutions" are also
expensive and perceived as options that are only accessible to the rich
(Biermann & Möller, 2019). Driving a Tesla electric vehicle, for
example, is not an option for most people, so the focus on this
technological innovation results in many people feeling disempowered and
disengaged (Stephens & Surprise, 2020). [This disempowerment is
compounded by [science and engineering being fields that continue to be
dominated by white men]{.mark} (Woolston, 2020). Despite efforts to
diversify science and engineering, [persistent racial, gendered, and
economic injustices]{.mark} of our economy and our educational systems
[perpetuate exclusive access to science and engineering]{.mark}
(Valantine & Collins, 2015)]{.underline}. Participating in science
continues to be a selective activity only accessible to a privileged few
(Lee, 2016). [The [lack of diversity]{.mark} within the fields of
science and engineering limits the scope of inquiry and
[constrains]{.mark} the types of connections that are made among
[science, technology, and society]{.mark} (Stephens, 2020).]{.underline}
As we move to incorporate innovative responses that promote social
justice to climate change beyond technological justifications for energy
transformation, there is a need to include other kinds of expertise,
experiences, and perspectives. [The technical focus of [climate
isolationism]{.mark} also obfuscates and **[diminishes the potential for
transformative social change]{.mark}** (Anderson & Peters, 2016) and it
limits the possibilities for investing in social innovation, social
infrastructure, and social justice (Stephens, 2020).]{.underline} When
the climate crisis is framed as a scientific problem with a possible
technological fix, the systemic societal and economic problems,
including the concentration of wealth and power among those profiting
from maintaining fossil fuel reliance, are all-too-often ignored
(Stephens, 2020). The prevalence of climate isolationism has encouraged
too many leaders to be blind to the important opportunities for
[improving people\'s lives and strengthening communities as we
transition away from a society reliant on fossil fuels (Stephens,
2019).]{.underline} [The narrow technocratic approach of [climate
isolationism]{.mark} has not only been ineffective in mobilizing
transformative change but it [has]{.mark} also [resulted in
climate]{.mark} and energy programs and [policies that exacerbate
inequities and perpetuate injustice]{.mark} (Jenkins et al., 2020).
Because the social dimensions of climate and energy have not been
adequately considered, [we have ended up with policies that]{.mark}
further exclude and **[disadvantage low-income communities, women, and
communities of color]{.mark}** (Reames, 2016)]{.underline}. The
quantitative technocratic tendency of climate isolationism reinforces a
dubious technological optimism (Basiago, 1994), which has led to growing
interest and funding for technological fixes to the climate crisis
(Stephens & Markusson, 2018).While technology is an essential part of a
transition toward a more just, equitable, and climate-stable future[,
investments in science and technology have not yet been balanced with
investments in social science, social infrastructure, social
innovations, and social justice. This [lack of investment in social
infrastructure]{.mark} and social innovations [has weakened our social
ties and reduced our societal resilience]{.mark} (Aldrich,
2012)]{.underline}. For decades, strategic governmental responses to the
climate crisis have been focused almost exclusively on investments in
science and technology (Stephens, 2009), while very little has been
invested in social innovation.By focusing almost exclusively on
technological innovation, climate isolationism obfuscates the potential
for transformative social change and diminishes the priority of
investing in climate-resilient innovations that simultaneously advance
social justice (Jenkins, 2018). [The persistence of the narrow [climate
isolationism]{.mark} perspective [has been beneficial for the polluter
elite]{.mark}, those wealthy individuals and organizations that do not
want transformative change because they are [profiting from fossil fuel
reliance]{.mark} and exploitative corporate business practices (Kenner,
2019).]{.underline} Climate denialism, which has been supported by
fossil fuel interests and the polluter elite, has also required climate
decision-makers to spend a lot of time and energy defending what is
known about the science of climate change (Oreskes, 2019). The polluter
elite\'s decades-long strategic misinformation campaign to confuse the
public about the science of climate change has been an effective delay
tactic (Frumhoff et al., 2015). [The prevalence of climate denialism has
confined climate discourse to the scientific realm and limited options
for nonscientific discourse about how to respond to the climate crisis
and reduce climate vulnerabilities.]{.underline} Continuing to increase
investment in technological innovation while underinvesting in social
innovation is preventing the transformative changes that are required
both for stabilizing the climate and for reducing social injustice. So
not only does climate isolationism result in missed opportunities to
advance social and economic justice, but it also results in insufficient
environmental protection (Jenkins, 2018).

Feminist, antiracist values

This final section provides [[a transformative alternative to the
ineffective and dangerous masculine technological
optimism]{.underline}]{.mark}. [To counter and resist the perpetuation
of masculine technological optimism and climate isolationism, [feminist,
antiracist values need be prioritized in climate and energy
policy]{.mark}. Feminist, antiracist values involve constant
consideration of power dynamics, that is, [paying attention to who has
power and privilege, who is being excluded or marginalized]{.mark}, what
legacy processes and priorities are perpetuating discrepancies in power,
and ultimately whether and how power is being concentrated or
distributed (Stephens, 2020).]{.underline} Vigilant and transparent
analysis and assessment of power, including understanding the multiple
ways that social, economic, and political power shape institutions and
social change (Kashwan et al., 2019), are fundamental to the
transformations that are needed for a more just, equitable, healthy and
prosperous future. Recognizing that social change and innovation can
disrupt or reinforce existing and legacy power dynamics, explicit and
continuous attention to different forms of empowerment and
disempowerment are critically important (Avelino, 2021). [Given the
disruptive time and the human suffering exacerbated by the pandemic and
the climate crisis, [elevating feminist, antiracist values is a
central]{.mark} priority for societal transformation.]{.underline} To
understand the term "antiracist," Ibram X. Kendi\'s powerful 2019 book
How to Be an Antiracist provides guidance. In his book, Kendi explains
that anyone who declares that they are not racist is signifying
neutrality, but, he points out, in the struggle with racism there is no
neutrality (2019). Kendi explains that [[the opposite of "racist" is not
"not racist" but it is "antiracist"---whenever we ignore issues of race
we are inadvertently perpetuating racism]{.mark}.]{.underline} Given the
deep legacy of racial injustice embedded in our culture, in our
institutions, in our communities, in our economy, and in our policies,
those who do not actively resist racism are in fact supporting it.
[Embracing antiracist values requires continual recognition and active
resistance to racism in all its many legacy forms and structures. A
similar argument can be made regarding feminist values which are
consistently resisting the power of patriarchy, misogyny, and gender
discrimination (Manne, 2018). Like racism, sexism is deeply rooted in
our society, and many of our institutions, norms, and values will
continue to reinforce gender discrimination unless we are continually
and actively resisting. **[Unless we are actively resisting racism and
patriarchy we are actually perpetuating these systems of
oppression]{.mark}**.]{.underline} According to Chimamanda Ngozi
Adichie, author of We Should All be Feminists, many men say that they do
not think much about gender or notice gender disparities (Adichie,
2012). Similarly, many white people say that they do not think much
about race or notice racial disparities. [[Those with privilege]{.mark}
who consider themselves successful within current systems [are]{.mark}
generally [less aware of]{.mark} the [structural oppression]{.mark} that
stratifies society.]{.underline} This is why feminist antiracist values
are so critical in society\'s efforts to confront the interconnected
crises facing humanity. **[[If society\'s response to the climate crisis
is based on proposals from those who are]{.mark} unaware of or
[indifferent to racism and sexism, climate action is almost guaranteed
to reinforce those inequities]{.mark}.]{.underline}** [[And]{.mark}, if
antiracist and feminist values are not prioritized, [society is unlikely
to succeed in designing]{.mark} inclusive and effective [responses to
the climate crisis]{.mark}.]{.underline} It is important to note that
anyone can embrace antiracist and feminist values. Every human being has
the capacity to learn, understand and have empathy for other human
beings, so all of us can resist systems of oppression regardless of
where we are positioned within those systems. Ultimately, everyone is
negatively impacted by racism, misogyny, and other forms of oppression.
So everyone, regardless of gender, race, or any other identities, can be
encouraged to embrace and prioritize feminist, antiracist values. [To
move mainstream climate decision-making beyond climate isolationism
toward climate justice, it is helpful to understand how climate
isolationism is being perpetuated and why a gender lens is so important
in advancing climate justice and resisting climate
isolationism]{.underline}. The prevalence of climate isolationism can be
attributed to multiple factors including the limited experiences and
perspectives of many climate experts whose knowledge is limited to
climate science and technology. [[White men have made up the majority of
climate and energy experts]{.mark} (Kempe, 2021[), and]{.mark} the
systemic [exclusion]{.mark} of diverse voices in mainstream climate
decision-making has [contributed to climate isolationism]{.mark}
(Stephens & Surprise, 2020).]{.underline} Since the climate crisis was
first recognized as an emerging problem in the late 1970s and early
1980s (Keeling et al., 1976, Marchetti, 1977, National Research Council,
1983), a [technocratic, reductionist, top-down approach to climate
policy has dominated]{.underline}. Large investments have been made in
technological innovations to mitigate climate change (Gallagher et al.,
2006, Holdren, 2006), but minimal attention has been given to social
innovation, power dynamics, and how climate and energy policy could
leverage change toward social justice (Webler & Tuler, 2010). One
prominent and influential privileged white man who has not embraced
climate justice but is instead perpetuating climate isolationism is Bill
Gates. His 2021 book "How to Avoid Climate Disaster" focuses exclusively
on technological innovations demonstrating the inadequacy and dangers of
climate isolationism (Gates, 2021). In this book, Gates openly
acknowledges that he does not "have a solution to the politics of
climate change." Rather he professes that new and existing technologies
can solve the climate crisis; all that is needed is more investment in
technological innovation to speed up the pace (Gates, 2021). Gates also
describes solar geoengineering as a "cutting edge, 'Break Glass in Case
of Emergency' kind of tool" that is valuable to have in case things get
so bad that there are few other options. He says "There may come a day
when we don't have a choice. Best to prepare for that day now." Gates
singular focus on technological innovation is [characteristic of climate
isolationism and represents a trend of privileged tech-savy men, the
so-called ["climate dudes"]{.mark} who [think they can swoop and solve
complex problem]{.mark}s that others have spent decades attempting to
address]{.underline} (Jones, 2021).The prevalence of climate
isolationism can also be attributed to a [male-dominated climate and
energy leadership that continues to prioritize scientific and
technological expertise to inform climate policy]{.underline} (Fraune,
2015, Pearl-Martinez & Stephens, 2016). Like many other aspects of
society, the science, politics, and economics of climate and energy have
been dominated by privileged white-male leadership which has tended to
[be technocratic, reductionist, patriarchal, and top-down]{.underline}
(Faber et al., 2017, Sorman et al., 2020). The technological optimism
that is characteristic of climate isolationism is also linked to
masculinity as the colloquial phrase "boys and their toys" represents
(Lohan & Faulkner, 2004).

Conclusions

To move beyond masculine technological optimism and climate isolationism
toward climate justice, [[climate decision-making has to embrace
feminist antiracist priorities]{.mark} by focusing more explicitly on
power dynamics and social innovations to redistribute power to people
and communities who are most vulnerable.]{.underline} Climate
decision-making needs to explicitly consider how policies, practices,
and priorities either reinforce or disrupt the systems that are
currently concentrating wealth and power. For less powerful groups to
gain a foothold in decision-making processes, renewed attention to the
multiple ways that social, economic, and political power shape social
change must be acknowledged (Kashwan et al., 2019). Feminist theory
offers expertise in the study of power (Bell et al., 2020), so embracing
[a feminist lens is one valuable approach to moving away from climate
isolationism toward climate justice.]{.underline} The social science
literature on sociotechnical transitions has been critiqued for
minimizing the role of power (Avelino, 2017), and a recent contribution
by Avelino (2021) identifies seven specific ways to consider power in
decision-making, processes of change, and innovation: (a) power over
versus power to, (b) centered versus diffused, (c) consensual versus
conflictual, (d) constraining versus enabling, (e) quantity versus
quality, (f) empowerment versus disempowerment, and (g) power in
relation to knowledge (Avelino, 2021). As jurisdictions around the world
grapple with the interconnected crises of housing and food insecurity,
climate disruptions, and economic precarity, narrow efforts to reduce
greenhouse gas emissions or control the global average temperature are
likely to cause more harm than good. New ways of strategically
integrating climate action into other social policies, in the way that
the Biden/Harris administration is "quietly" integrating their climate
agenda in 2021 into pandemic recovery and infrastructure investments,
provides an empirical example of the practical valuable potential of
moving away from climate isolationism (Osaka, 2021). Mary Robinson and
many others too have recognized that the most powerful decision-makers
in climate and energy are "too male, too pale, and too stale." [[There
is an underrepresentation of women in energy and climate
leadership.]{.mark} The [societal transformation]{.mark} ahead [requires
mobilizing the potential]{.mark} and expertise [of diverse
energy]{.mark} practitioners and [researchers.]{.mark}]{.underline}

### AT: US leadership Key

#### Leadership framing is flawed -- The aff treats policy as zero sum locks in climate change.

**Darkin '07** (Beverly Darkin was a *Senior Research Fellow at Chatham
House (Royal Institute of* International Affairs). Darkin, B., & House,
C. (2007). Climate change: the leadership challenge. Royal Institute of
International Affairs, Energy, Environment and Development Programme. -
[GL]{.mark}

The business of climate change negotiation can usefully be analysed in
terms of game theory, following the work of the Princeton economist John
Nash, who developed the theory of so-called 'non- cooperative games'.
[This theory demonstrated how [a 'game' such as a climate change
negotiation could result in a stable, sub-optimal equilibrium]{.mark} in
which no single player can benefit from unilaterally changing his or her
move.]{.underline} Nash proved that sub-optimaloutcomes could arise in a
much wider set of games than previously thought, in particular those
involving many players. [This phenomenon is clearly encountered in
climate change negotiations that attempt to set emission-reduction
goals.]{.underline} The idea is that negotiation targets are carved up
between different parties in order to achieve a global goal. This
automatically creates a situation where countries will tend towards the
Nash equilibrium -- [[no one country will benefit by changing its
approach while the other players keep their strategies
unchanged]{.mark}. So long as each party refuses to give ground[, the
equilibrium will be perpetuated]{.mark}]{.underline}. Climate change
leaders have attempted to breakaway from this zero-sum-game mentality by
committing also to regional-level actions outside the international
negotiations. The rationale is that taking unilateral action creates the
potential for everyone to benefit. The European Union's 20% reduction
target is one such example[. This target was agreed on the understanding
that the future lies in the development of a low- or even zero-carbon
economy, and a belief that all countries will want to travel down this
path eventually, so it is constructive for one party -- Europe in this
case -- to be at the forefront]{.underline}. Central to this approach is
a concept that is fundamental to climate leadership -- interdependency.
This is explored in detail in the analysis of 'Europe in the World' by
Burke and Mabey. Their analysis recognizes that 'the determinants of
Europe's peace and prosperity no longer lie predominantly within its
geographical borders. We cannot make a transition to sustainable
development on our own.' By understanding, and taking account of,
interdependence, it becomes possible to make decisions that will benefit
not only Europe but also the rest of the world. [According to this
reasoning, this will enable [Europe]{.mark} to [move towards a
collaborative approach]{.mark}, and seek ways of working with other
nations on energy and climate security [to achieve mutual
interests]{.mark}. [This contrasts markedly to the approach of the
US]{.mark} administration, [which has prevaricated over]{.mark} emission
reduction [targets]{.mark} (since withdrawing from the Kyoto Protocol in
2001) [out of a fear of damaging US international
competitiveness]{.mark}, not least [vis-à-vis China]{.mark}, with
attendant job losses.]{.underline} However, Krugman and others have
argued that the concept of competitiveness has little meaning at the
level of the nation-state. The argument carries serious weight only at
the level of the firm or sector, where it is possible to identify
winners and losers. Krugman argues that at the level of the nation-state
it is more appropriate to focus on productivity. In the context of
climate change, this includes the potential for the global carbon market
-- valued at \$30 billion in 200633-- to create new business
opportunities that will contribute to national growth[. Nevertheless,
the reluctance of the US administration to commit to a binding 50%
reduction target at the G8 summit on 7 June demonstrates that [the US
administration is]{.mark} still unconvinced about the overall potential
benefits of the carbon market to the US economy and [wary about handing
over a competitive advantage to China and India.]{.mark} There is still
more work to be done by climate leaders [to break down the zero-sum-game
mentality]{.mark}.]{.underline}

## HEG

### 1NC

#### Hegemonic collapse is inevitable due to failure to manage global crises.

**Xing 25** \[Li Xing, (Yunshan Leading Scholar & Distinguished
Professor at Guangdong University of Foreign Studies; Director of the
European Research Center & Distinguished Professor at the Guangdong
Institute for International Strategies; Full Professor of International
Relations, Department of Politics and Society, Aalborg University
(Denmark); Ph.D. in Development and International Relations;
Editor-in-Chief, Journal of China and International Relations; author of
multiple volumes on China's rise and world order), 1-21-25, "As the
rules-based order declines, hegemony takes on a new form," South China
Morning Post,
https://www.scmp.com/opinion/world-opinion/article/3295126/rules-based-order-declines-hegemony-takes-new-form,
SHCP AB\]

\<Spelling corrected\>

History shows that world orders, disorders or new orders often result
from the disturbing dynamics unleashed by crises amid the rise of new
powers and the resistance of existing powers. Current dynamics can be
understood by analyzing how the world order is transitioning from a
state of hegemony to one of "intertwined hegemony". Hegemony is a
concept that was employed by Italian Marxist theorist Antonio Gramsci
and is applied in international relations to describe the enduring
aspects of an existing order maintained by a leading actor through a
skillful combination of coercive and non-coercive power. Hegemony is
often associated with the United States, which has served as the primary
hegemon shaping the post-war order. [[US]{.mark} [heg]{.mark}emony has
been [exerted through]{.mark} its [econ]{.mark}omic [and
military]{.mark} power, [as well as]{.mark} through [international
institutions]{.mark} used as tools to uphold global norms, trade and
security structures.]{.underline} [[However]{.mark}, since September 11
and the 2008 global financial crisis, the [world]{.mark} order has
[undergone]{.mark} multiple [crises]{.mark} and pivotal moments, such as
[China's rise]{.mark}, the [US-China rivalry]{.mark}, the
Russia-[Ukraine war, China's]{.mark} increasing [role in the Middle
East,]{.mark} Israel's latest assault in [Palestine]{.mark} and Donald
[Trump's second term.]{.mark} These [crises]{.mark} and pivotal moments
have [exposed]{.mark} [vulnerabilities in]{.mark} the existing [world
order]{.mark} and challenged the hegemony of Western-led institutions
and norms.]{.underline} [The existing world order is facing crises
across four key domains. Multilateral cooperation is increasingly
elusive due to great power rivalries and interstate conflicts,
highlighting a crisis of functionality as global institutions
[struggle]{.mark} to build [consensus]{.mark} on critical issues, such
as [climate change, pandemics and cybersecurity]{.mark}.]{.underline}
[The [US-led]{.mark} "rules-based" global [governance]{.mark}
architecture has [shown]{.mark} its [inability to]{.mark} effectively
[address]{.mark} [worldwide challenges]{.mark}. The [failure to
deliver]{.mark} a secure and [equitable]{.mark} global [system
underscores a crisis of legitimacy.]{.mark} Finally, [shifting power
dynamics]{.mark} between emerging and established powers signifies a
[crisis of authority]{.mark}.]{.underline} In this context, intertwined
hegemony is a better way to describe the transition from the current
world order to an emerging one. [[Intertwined heg]{.mark}emony reflects
a [process defined by mutual challenges, constraints,
dependencies]{.mark} and [accommodation between]{.mark} existing and
emerging [powers]{.mark}.]{.underline} Defenders and challengers of the
existing order are deeply intertwined. [Firstly, intertwined hegemony
indicates that globalization is [steering the world]{.mark} economy
[towards]{.mark} an era of multiple development models characterized by
the coexistence of multiple [competing economic systems and modes of
governance]{.mark}. Global economic players now include not only private
entities but also states, exemplified by Chinese state-owned
enterprises.]{.underline} The Chinese development model, often referred
to as the Beijing Consensus -- understood as regulated state-market -
society relations -- stands in contrast to the US-led Washington
Consensus defined by liberal economic policies. Non-state entities like
transnational corporations, tech giants and social movements also play
an outsize role in shaping the world order. Secondly, intertwined
hegemony reflects a shift towards a post-hegemonic world, where no
single power, like the US or the West more broadly, unilaterally shapes
global norms or leadership. Decentralized platforms, internet
connections, the digital economy and tech competition have eroded
traditional monopolies on discourse, narratives and soft power. Global
norms are becoming more flexible and less universal, while alliances
form around specific challenges and interests rather than shared
ideologies. In this post-hegemonic era, no single power can enforce
global order, raising the risk of regional conflicts and power
rivalries. Thirdly, intertwined hegemony emphasizes the diverse
perspectives on mechanisms, policies and strategies that drive national
development and growth. [Today, [emerging powers]{.mark} have the
capacity to [formulate]{.mark} collective [positioning
strategies]{.mark} and balancing tactics [in the global arena.]{.mark}
For example, the expansion of [BRICS promotes]{.mark} a more
[inclusive]{.mark} [model of]{.mark} global [governance]{.mark} that
values cultural and civilizational diversity, providing an [alternative
to the universalism]{.mark} often espoused by the West.]{.underline} The
expansion of the BRICS grouping indicates emerging powers are navigating
the evolving world order through a selective interplay of strategies,
including watching or observing developments, actively engaging in
global affairs, and influencing the world system to align with their
interests. Last but not least, intertwined hegemony marks a new era in
which stratification between nations in the traditional core,
semi-periphery and periphery, as well as the conventional North-South
axis, may no longer fully capture the complexities of today's globalized
and interconnected world. While they remain useful for understanding
global inequality and historical patterns of exploitation, the rise of
emerging economies like China has reshaped the established structure of
global economic stratification. This is not the first time the world
order has encountered a post-hegemonic reality. Historical transitions,
such as the period following the fall of the Roman Empire and the
interwar years between the first and second world wars, experienced
similar phases of uncertainty and transformation. The unprecedented pace
of technological, economic and environmental changes are key
distinctions in today's world. [[Whether these transformations lead to a
new equilibrium or devolve into chaos depends]{.mark} largely [on
how]{.mark} global [actors address]{.mark} and navigate these
[challenges]{.mark}.]{.underline} Therefore, intertwined hegemony is
perhaps the best way to describe evolving global dynamics, where power
and influence are increasingly shared, contested and co-produced across
different regions and actors. The emerging world order characterized by
intertwined hegemony embodies a duality. As existing powers gradually
lose their dominance, emerging powers have yet to establish an
alternative hegemony grounded in universal principles and norms.

#### Clinging to dominance ensures nuclear war, econ collapse, and arms racing. 

Chris **Wright 23,** professor of history at the City University of New
York, Ph.D., 4/28/2023, \"The Second Cold War Is More Dangerous than the
First,\"
https://www.counterpunch.org/2023/04/28/the-second-cold-war-is-more-dangerous-than-the-first/

[Modern history shows that [the most dangerous periods are when two or
more great powers are struggling for heg]{.mark}emony]{.underline}. The
eighteenth century in Europe was a time of "multipolarity," as Britain,
France, Prussia, Austria, and Russia were almost continually at war,
competing for geopolitical advantage and to divide the continent between
them. The conflicts escalated in the era of the French Revolution and
Napoleonic Wars, as a mighty France, bursting with revolutionary energy,
strove for absolute dominance against, in the end, Britain and Russia.
The 1815 Congress of Vienna led to a century-long balance of power
presided over by an industrializing Britain, which soon became the
supreme world power. Once industrialization swept the rest of Europe,
however, particularly Germany, Britain's power began to be challenged,
not only in the Scramble for Africa but in Europe itself. German elites
wanted their country to be the next Britain, and to a great extent it
was their desire for hegemony that precipitated both world wars. [Since
1945, the United States has been virtually a global
hegemon.]{.underline} As John Ross notes in the recently published
Washington's New Cold War, even at the height of its relative economic
achievement in the mid-1970s, the Soviet Union's GDP was only 44 percent
of the U.S.'s. The Soviets had vast power in their limited sphere
encompassing Eastern Europe and Central Asia, but they were not a
capitalistically expansive, dynamically growing imperial power in the
mode of the United States---or, more recently, of a resurgent China.
China's GDP is 74 percent of the U.S.'s, and its growth rate is higher
(it has grown seven times faster than the American economy since 2007).
Measured by purchasing power parities, the U.S. accounts for only 16
percent of the world economy, and China's economy is 18 percent larger.
[In short, for [the first time since World War]{.mark} II, we [are
entering]{.mark} an [era of real]{.mark} [competition]{.mark} between
two mammoth economies, a [declining]{.mark} hegemon [and]{.mark} an
[aspiring hegemon]{.mark}. When people talk about "the China threat,"
this is, in effect, all they mean. In the long run, [China]{.mark} poses
a greater threat to U.S. power than the Soviet Union ever
did.]{.underline} Mainstream commentators and politicians prate about
China's threat to democratic values and human rights---there always has
to be an ideological rationalization for geopolitical strategy---but

[worth]{.mark} [risking]{.mark} [nuclear]{.mark}

[to]{.mark} [U.S]{.mark}. [Power]{.mark} The current flashpoint, of

[improve]{.mark} [its]{.mark} [position]{.mark} there, as illustrated by

China's burgeoning economic interests not only in the Middle East but

### 2NC/1NR\-\--UQ Multipolarity

#### Multipolarity is emerging, and multilateral institutions check instability. 

**Chase-Dunn,** C., **& Zhai**, E. (20**25**). Christopher Chase-Dunn is
from the Institute for Research on World-Systems, University of
California, Riverside. Emlyn Zhai is from the Institute for Research on
World-Systems, University of California, Riverside. US Hegemony in
Evolutionary Perspective. *Critical Sociology*, *51*(4-5), 1001-1023.
https://doi-org.proxy.lib.umich.edu/10.1177/08969205251337969 (Original
work published 2025) //HAF9000

Short-run Futures

[By short-run futures we mean configurations that are likely to emerge
in the next 25 years---from 2025 to 2050.^23^ Although there are
[important similarities between]{.mark} the late-19th-century and
early-20th-century [decline of the British hegemony]{.mark} with what is
happening with US decline, there are also important differences. One
[similarity is the **arrival of a multipolar configuration**]{.mark} of
power among the most powerful states, a configuration that, in the past,
has led to interimperial rivalries and hot wars among core states. An
important [difference]{.mark} is [between]{.mark} an interstate system
that is configured [as a set of sovereign nation-states rather
than]{.mark} as a set of [colonial empires,]{.mark} a more developed set
of [**multilateral governmental institutions**,]{.mark} a [more
**globalized structure of production**]{.mark} and class relations,
hugely [increased long-distance transportation and
comm]{.mark}unications infrastructure, the evolution of military
technology to include much [more]{.mark} lethal and [destructive
weapons]{.mark}, and the expansion of the arenas of power projection
that have added airpower, space power, and cyberwarfare to the older
land and sea power theaters.]{.underline} The answer to the question
about the future of great power war is likely to be influenced by all
these differences.¶ Inflection Points and Great Power War¶ Heim and
Miller (2020) used a modified version of the Pardee GPI to model future
changes in the relative distribution of power using a baseline model
that does not include international conflict to examine what differences
population explosion, a lost decade of Chinese economic growth, or
different degrees and speed of onset of global warming would make for
the relative power shares of national-states. They also employed Charles
Doran's (2012) version of power transition theory to try to predict when
the risk of war between the United States and the P.R.C. would be
highest. All the models of power transition imply that when a rising
power gets close to, or passes, the relative power of the old hegemon
the risk of war is high. But Doran's version sees other inflection
points when the rates of change increase or decrease. This produces
confusion and uncertainty and makes it difficult for decision-makers to
be clear about what is happening. And Doran contends that when several
of the great powers (core states) go through inflection points in the
same short period the risk of warfare is higher. The most obvious
inflection point in Figure 6 is around 2040 when China's GPI score is
likely to pass the United States. But when we track economic and
military power shares separately, as in Figure 5, we can see that it
will probably take China longer to catch up with the US military share
level than the US economic power share level. The risk of war is
probably more influenced by military inflection points, and this implies
that the dangerous period might not arrive until the 2050s. The uneven
onset of global warming and climate change disasters will probably alter
the probabilities regarding which configurations of global power emerge
in the next several decades. The Heim and Miller model of the GPI tried
different levels of temperature increase and models of which regions are
likely to be affected the most. The global warming scenarios show that
China will see a significant increase in global power relative to the
baseline model, Russia will see a slight gain and the United States and
India will experience moderate declines (Heim and Miller 2020: Figure
19, p.20; Figure 21, p.21).¶ Thompson's (2022) book on the decline of US
systemic leadership uses the power cycle theoretical research program
that he developed with George Modelski to update their estimates of
shares of long-distance military power projection (see also Lee and
Thompson, 2018) and to ponder the short-run future of the global power
system. Thompson thinks that the avoidance of the use of weapons of mass
destruction that has worked to prevent wars among the great powers since
World War II will hold in the next few decades because a war that uses
these would likely be disastrous for all sides of the conflict and for
large numbers of others not directly involved (but see Goddard and
Larkin, 2025). So far, the Russia--Ukraine war seems to encourage this
position. But Thompson also notes that the situation of mutually assured
mass destruction has been, and still is, vulnerable to accidents and
miscommunications of the kind that nearly led to nuclear war several
times during the Cold War between the United States and the Soviet
Union. We agree with Thompson that even with growing interimperial
rivalry between China and the United States and concurrent hot wars in
Eastern Europe and the Middle East, the likelihood is that there will
not be a war among great powers in which weapons of mass destruction are
used because decision-makers believe that such a war is unwinnable and
would be devastating for both sides. But we also note what Thompson said
about accidents and miscommunication during periods of stress. Another
world war is not likely but could happen. If it does, it would probably
not kill all the humans and so sociocultural evolution would start back
up again.¶ Power Configurations: G2 Bipolarity, Tripolarity, or
Multipolarity? [Thompson thinks that the [most likely **next power

century]{.mark}.]{.underline} It raises critical questions about the
role of traditional 7 security alliances in addressing non-traditional
threats, such as economic coercion, cyberattacks, and the strategic
implications of technological advancements. Finally, this thesis aims to
address a research gap in the study of strategic narratives by focusing
on NATO's construction of a strategic narrative about China. While
previous research has explored how China crafts strategic narratives
about NATO, limited attention has been given to the reverse. Although
there is extensive research on NATO's construction of strategic
narratives in general, these studies primarily focus on other actors or
issues, such as Russia or Euro-Atlantic security, rather than China. By
examining NATO's evolving narrative on China, this thesis contributes an
empirical example to the broader study of strategic narrative

CONCLUSION In order to be able to predict what the world economy has
experienced from the past to the present and the possible scenarios it
will experience in the future, it is necessary to understand the
phenomenon of globalization. With globalization, there has been an
increase in communication and transportation resources in particular.
This has led to an easier movement of goods, services, capital, etc.
between countries[. Therefore, in the postglobalization period,
[connections]{.mark} between countries have [increased]{.mark} and all
[countries]{.mark} have almost [become]{.mark} [competitors]{.mark}. The
competitive environment has [helped]{.mark} [countries]{.mark}
[develop]{.mark}. This situation has brought [new]{.mark} pole
[candidates]{.mark} to the agenda that can [shake]{.mark} [the]{.mark}
[economic power of the USA]{.mark}. The 21st-century [economy]{.mark} is
[progressively shifting away from]{.mark} a [unipolar]{.mark}
framework.]{.underline} A primary factor contributing to this transition
has been identified as the swift expansion of developing nations
following the global financial crisis of 2008. Consequently, the 2008
crisis is widely regarded as the onset of the decline of the United
States, the epitome of unipolarity. [Current assessments indicate [that
emerging global powers have begun to eclipse the United States]{.mark}.

unipolarity, they may establish themselves as a new global power[,
lead]{.mark}ing the 21st century [toward]{.mark} a [multipolar]{.mark}

is the diminishing centrality of the United States in economic cycles

will make it difficult for the reserve issuer to act arbitrarily. The
