#### Multipolarity is stabilizing and cooperative. 

Petrakis, P. E., Kanzola, A.-M., & Lomis, I. (2024). Adapting to
Multipolarity: Insights from Iterated Game Theory Simulations---A
Preliminary Study on Hypothetical Optimal Global Cooperation. Journal of
Risk and Financial Management, 17(8), 370.
https://doi.org/10.3390/jrfm17080370 /SHCP HAF9000/

[We found that [multipolarity]{.mark}, characterized by the presence of
several powerful states, could [enhance the likelihood of international
coop]{.mark}eration.]{.underline} Thus, H1 is confirmed. In essence,
[[large, powerful countries]{.mark} can [play a stabilizing role]{.mark}
in the international system [by promoting cooperative behavior]{.mark}
among states.]{.underline} [Large countries have more resources to
invest in cooperative ventures, which can serve as anchors for stability
and predictability (**Waltz 1979**). Additionally[, their greater
technological and economic capabilities may lead to higher gains from
cooperation, incentivizing them to pursue collaborative
strategies]{.mark} (**Keohane 1984**). These findings support the idea
that multipolar systems are more flexible and contain more interaction
opportunities (**Väyrynen 1995**). Under multipolarity,
[countries]{.mark} could [exhibit greater risk aversion,]{.mark} leading
to [a more pluralistic and balanced state--society]{.mark} relationship
due to greater variations in beliefs and institutions (**Väyrynen
1995**).]{.underline} This could decrease the global risks associated
with the economy, society, and geopolitical domains (**Acharya et al.
2023**). On the other hand, the main concern associated with
multipolarity is the isolation of states that are capable of building
advanced weapons, posing a significant threat to global welfare (**Posen
2009**). Negative risk perceptions could also trigger a feeling of
insecurity, followed by an organized shift towards self-sufficiency and
autarky (**Malcomson 2021**). Major-power autarky is defensive, and it
is not necessarily associated with a more dangerous world scenario
(**Malcomson 2021**); however, because of the inevitable locked-in
situations in the global system, this is a very concerning situation to
address. An unagonized return to protectionism and autarky could lead to
a situation known as a "security dilemma" (**Axelrod 1984**), in which
agents prioritize their own security over the security and welfare of
others. In the context of our analysis, global cooperation can falter
for several reasons. One significant factor is when cooperation reaches
a low point, creating a fertile ground for generalized disruption. Our
simulations consistently exhibit a pattern of alternating cycles between
increasing cooperation and increasing competition, akin to Kondratieff
cycles observed in economic theories, regardless of the chosen
parameters or number of large countries involved. Therefore, H2 is
confirmed. In such scenarios, minor disagreements or conflicts can
escalate rapidly, undermining trust and collaboration among nations,
leading to armed conflicts and destabilization (**Keohane 1984**).
Additionally, the introduction of new, aggressive strategies by
influential countries aiming to alter the status quo can destabilize the
existing balance of power (**Jervis 1978**). This shift can lead to an
environment in which competitive rather than cooperative behaviors
dominate, increasing the likelihood of widespread international crises
(**Mearsheimer 2001**). Multipolarity could take two possible
directions: (i) multi-growth and cooperation with sustainability, or
(ii) multi-threat of the world as we know it today (**Perskaya 2017**).
In an international geopolitical setting, multipolarity supported by
conflicting agendas is the most serious source of uncertainty concerning
global governance[. On the other hand, a more optimistic development for
international cooperation in a multipolar framework is cooperative
multipolarity (**de Aguiar Patriota 2017**). To achieve sustainability
and welfare at a global level, institutions and legislative frameworks
operating at a global level are in a position to define, apply, monitor,
and enforce technical and economic conditions and rules for a set of
operators (**Agrell 2015**).]{.underline} Consequently, if societies and
countries aim to promote social welfare, the role of institutions and
specific economic structures in fostering sufficient, sustainable, and
inclusive growth through the interconnection effect is crucial
(**Kanzola 2023**). [This discussion is already prominent because
[promoting stability under multipolarity is critical]{.mark} (**Roberts
2023**). In this direction, there is [an emerging need for the
configuration of conditions that favor cooperation]{.mark}.
[Multilateral organizations]{.mark} such as the International Monetary
Fund ([IMF]{.mark}), [World Bank]{.mark}, and the North Atlantic Treaty
Organization ([NATO]{.mark}) will play a pivotal role (**Tago
2017**).]{.underline} Nonetheless, the aforementioned international
cooperation platforms as well as the overall global governance
architecture need reform (**Sithole et al. 2024**). This argument is
supported by the historical evolution of these mechanisms. They were
established after World War II, when the dynamics of world powers
differed significantly from those today. It is questionable whether the
current institutional framework can adequately respond to the challenges
posed by the diverse influences on universal balance. The international
system of intervention and supervision of the global cooperation agenda,
such as the IMF, World Bank, and World Trade Organization (WTO), should
adapt to the new conditions. In this direction, it is important to
redefine the meaning of development, economic growth, global threats,
and sustainability in order to align with life values and, therefore, be
acceptable to all players (**Orliange 2021**). In economics, there is
also the rising question of whether the dollar, as a reserve currency,
is slowly but steadily losing its influence. This is a double-edged
sword because it raises issues regarding the global lender of the last
resort and questions global stability. For the time being, the USA
remains at the top of the global power hierarchy (**Ryan 2023**).
Another example of the destabilization of the world order is the
possibility of a food crisis. During the 2008 food crisis (**De Schutter
2009**), it was required to use new "unconventional" policy measures,
highlighting the need for the establishment of new cooperation tools
capable to prevent universal hunger (**Peres and De Souza Daibert
2017**). Hence, the absence of effective world back-stop mechanisms (in
economic and security relations) increases the difficulty of keeping the
ball from leaving the field---by increasing the risk and the possibility
of systematic world welfare leakages. A factor that potentially promotes
cooperation is global trade, but this does not include the globalization
of production (**Brooks 2024**). [Furthermore, [enhancing trust,
transparency, and respect for cultural and national values]{.mark} as
well as establishing [an accepted agenda for common global action are
indispensable]{.mark} for effective cooperation and conflict resolution
in our increasingly interconnected world.]{.underline} In the history of
human cooperation, forces and changes have bound us together, while
others have driven us apart. As a principle, the future is
simultaneously unknown and known because it is generated in the present
(**Kanzola 2023**). Taking action in the present will favor a desirable
future in terms of global developments, provided that individuals,
institutions, and governments are forward looking. Nevertheless, it is
important to remember that the future also depends on elements of order
and randomness (**McKinsey Global Institute 2023**; **Watts 2003**). In
other words, it is impossible to predict negative or positive
serendipity; we can only prepare for what we know. [Therefore,
[efficient cooperation is possible and promotes stability]{.mark} but is
not inherently guaranteed; [it requires time, trust, effort, and proper
dynamics.]{.mark} In return, it [offers stability, risk mitigation, and
security.]{.mark}]{.underline}

Petrakis, P. E., Kanzola, A.-M., & Lomis, I. (2024). Adapting to

5\. Concluding Remarks¶ In this study, we explored whether multipolarity
contributes to cooperation using an iterated game theory approach. [Our
findings suggest that the [presence of multipolarity tends to
increase]{.mark} the likelihood of [cooperation]{.mark}, at least from a
theoretical perspective. This finding is important given the current
discussion that the new multipolar order of the world economy could
undermine global stability.]{.underline} Nevertheless, in order to ward
off this dangerous possibility, proper institutional structures and the
alignment of social goal setting are required (Waltz 2000). Under these
conditions, a multipolar system could lead to the development of the
global economy by avoiding the use of military conflicts. In any case,
it should be mentioned that the promotion of cooperation is a difficult
attempt given that policymakers and governments often discard
theoretical models and insights for reasons related to the importance of
historical path dependence, national interests, and tradition.¶ To
ensure global sustainability, it is important to introduce a consistent
framework for the market economy that balances risks and rewards and
promotes systematic resilience (Roberts 2023). Otherwise, trying to
minimize risks reduces rewards and creates vulnerabilities (Roberts
2023). At the same time, trying to always maximize rewards could lead to
overlooking potential threats and risks (Roberts 2023). [Therefore, the
future of [global cooperation]{.mark} and welfare in a multipolar
setting [depends on]{.mark} finding mutually beneficial activities and
[a]{.mark} strict [legislative framework that prohibits one party from
overruling the other and mitigates]{.mark} several [conflicts of
interest]{.mark} (Axelrod 1984).]{.underline}¶ [Therefore, one pivotal
contribution of the present study is to [describe the conditions under
which global cooperation can break down.]{.mark} [Understanding]{.mark}
these conditions [is crucial]{.mark}, as it facilitates the
identification of [tipping points]{.mark} capable of [generating
disruption in international relationships and]{.mark} potentially
[triggering a global crisis]{.mark}.]{.underline} Cultivating this
ability to foresee the dangers and opportunities of a changing order of
things before their actualization (Machiavelli \[1566\] 2008)3 is vital
for maintaining international stability and peace (Oye 1986).

### AT: Transition War

#### No Lashout - Trump is willing to transition to a balanced order

**Desch and Dee 25** \[Michael C. Desch, Packey J. Dee Professor of
International Relations & Political Science, University of Notre Dame;
Brian and Jeannelle Brady Family Director & founding Director,
Notre Dame International Security Center; former two‑term Chair,

International
Politics 18 (2): 151--172, https://academic.oup.com/cjip/article/18/2/151/7990748 ,
SHCP AB\]

A balance of power reading of Thucydides suggests that US\' efforts to
maintain its post-Cold War hegemonic position, either unilaterally or
through broader consent, are bound to fail. [The [post-Cold War
"unipolar]{.mark} moment" was not a permanent fact but rather [a
temporary artifact of]{.mark} the sudden and [unexpected collapse of one
pole]{.mark} in a bipolar system.^187^]{.underline} By virtue of its
large population and high level of economic growth, China was destined
to replace the Soviet Union as another pole in the 21st century
international system. The interesting question is not whether
unipolarity will last but will it be replaced with a bipolar or
multipolar system?^[188]{.underline}^

One of the most important causes of hegemonic wars in the wake of power
transitions is the security dilemma, the unintended dynamic in which the
efforts one state takes to ensure its security often undermine that of
others.^[189]{.underline}^ [While in an anarchical international system,
the [security]{.mark} [dilemma can]{.mark} never be completely
eliminated, it can be [mitigated by]{.mark} the [policies]{.mark} states
adopt.]{.underline} Thucydides attributes the Peloponnesian War to the
breakdown of the balance of power between Athens and Sparta and he
applauds Pericles' efforts to try to re-establish it through restraint
rather than replacing Spartan hegemony. [Not only is a [balance of
power]{.mark} grand strategy [less likely to exacerbate the security
dilemma]{.mark}, it is eminently more feasible and [will better serve
American interests.]{.mark}^190^]{.underline}

A balance of power approach by the USA in its rivalry with China will be
effective because it builds upon a number of well-established tendencies
in international politics.^[191]{.underline}^ Most importantly, states
tend to balance against threats, rather than bandwagoning with
them.^[192]{.underline}^ As China grows more powerful and assertive,
states closest to it are feeling a greater sense of peril. Their
response will be to seek ways to respond to that threat by
counterbalancing it. [A [less]{.mark} forward and [assertive US
posture]{.mark} will not only mitigate the security dilemma with China
but also [dampen]{.mark} common pathologies of balancing such as
[buck-passing by allies]{.mark} to the larger power and otherwise
[under-providing for their own security]{.mark}.]{.underline} The common
problem behind both is what economists call "moral hazard," or the
propensity of actors to behave recklessly if another is insuring them
against its consequences.^[193]{.underline}^

Some fret that recent Chinese investments in anti-access/area denial
(A2/AD) technologies make it harder for the USA to continue to project
power close to China. However, these same technologies could also
bolster America's beleaguered allies in the
region.^[194]{.underline}^ Taiwan, the most probable *casus belli*, is
further from mainland China than Great Britain was from Nazi-held Europe
and about the same distance that Cuba was from Cold War Florida. Despite
these relatively short distances, neither was ever successfully invaded.
Today, China will have to project power across water, always a dicey
proposition but one made even harder now if US allies in the region
invest in their own A2/AD technologies to preserve the balance of power.

power?^195^ There are, in fact, grounds for thinking that [Trump might

**Chase-Dunn,** C., **& Zhai**, E. (20**25**). Christopher Chase-Dunn is

Inflection Points and Great Power War

Heim and Miller (2020) used a modified version of the Pardee GPI to
model future changes in the relative distribution of power using a
baseline model that does not include international conflict to examine
what differences population explosion, a lost decade of Chinese economic
growth, or different degrees and speed of onset of global warming would
make for the relative power shares of national-states. They also
employed Charles Doran's (2012) version of power transition theory to
try to predict when the risk of war between the United States and the
P.R.C. would be highest. All the models of power transition imply that
when a rising power gets close to, or passes, the relative power of the
old hegemon the risk of war is high. But Doran's version sees other
inflection points when the rates of change increase or decrease. This
produces confusion and uncertainty and makes it difficult for
decision-makers to be clear about what is happening. And Doran contends
that when several of the great powers (core states) go through
inflection points in the same short period the risk of warfare is
higher. The most obvious inflection point in Figure 6 is around 2040
when China's GPI score is likely to pass the United States. But when we
track economic and military power shares separately[, as in Figure 5, we
can see that it will probably **take China longer to catch up with the
US military** share level than the US economic power share level. [The
risk of war]{.mark} is probably more influenced by military inflection
points, and this implies that the dangerous period might [**not arrive
until the 2050s**.]{.mark} The]{.underline} uneven onset of global
warming and climate change disasters will probably alter the
probabilities regarding which configurations of global power emerge in
the next several decades. The Heim and Miller model of the GPI tried
different levels of temperature increase and models of which regions are
likely to be affected the most. [The global warming scenarios show that
**China will see a significant increase in global power relative to the
baseline model, Russia will see a slight gain and the United States and
India will experience moderate declines**]{.underline} (Heim and Miller
2020: Figure 19, p.20; Figure 21, p.21).

short-run future of the global power system. [Thompson thinks that the

#### Heg Drives prolif- Iranian Nuclear program proves. 

**Usman 25 \[Rana 
Usman, B.S. candidate & Researcher, Department of International
Relations, Federal 
Urdu University of Arts, Science and Technology (Islamabad, Pakistan); creator
on Zenodo; student‑author on SSRN working‑paper series,
6‑4‑2025, "Strategic Motivations Behind Iran's
Nuclear Program: A Critical Study of  Regional Power Dynamics," SSRN
Electronic Journal, https://papers.ssrn.com/abstract=5285267, SHCP
AB\] **

1\. Introduction At the center of one of the most ongoing and complex
geopolitical discussions in the modern Middle East is Iran\'s nuclear
initiative. Often seen through the binary prism of proliferation versus
compliance, the program is really the result of a deeply ingrained
strategic calculus molded by Iran\'s past, regional rivalries, and
changing security environment. By means of regional power dynamics,
strategic hedging, and ideological identification, this study aims to
expose the fundamental reasons behind Iran\'s nuclear aspirations. The
report underlines that Iran\'s nuclear policy is not only an attempt to
achieve military capability but also a deliberate and symbolic maneuver
to safeguard sovereignty, reinforce regional influence, and balance
asymmetric threats. Iran has chosen a strategy of nuclear hedging,
developing the technical means to reach nuclear threshold status while
staying inside the legal limits of the Nuclear Non-Proliferation Treaty
(NPT) instead of following an obvious road toward nuclear weaponization.
Tehran\'s ambiguous posture lets it benefit from diplomatic leverage and
deterrence without inviting the full results of international reprisals.
Combined with ongoing U.S. hostility and regional Sunni rivalries,
especially with Israel and Saudi Arabia, historical grievances,
including the 1953 coup and the Iran-Iraq War, have ingrained a sense of
existential threat. Iran\'s answer thus is based on a security rationale
that sees nuclear capacity as a defensive barrier against encirclement
and foreign intervention. But without considering Iran\'s domestic
political narratives and ideological construction, one cannot completely
grasp the reasons behind her nuclear approach. [[Iran presents]{.mark}
the [nuclear program as]{.mark} a symbol of scientific success, national
pride, and [opposition against Western hegemony.]{.mark}]{.underline}
Between reformists, conservatives, and the Revolutionary Guard, the
internal power dynamics help to further embed the nuclear debate inside
a struggle over legitimacy and national identity. Theoretical models
such constructivism and status theory help to clarify how Iran\'s quest
for international recognition and a sovereign technological identity
supports its will to keep its nuclear course. This paper also looks at
Iran\'s nuclear posture in view of its strategic hegemony ambitions for
a region. Iran wants to establish itself as an indispensable regional
actor by increasing its influence in regional theaters, including Syria,
Iraq, and Lebanon, and by diplomatic assertiveness in JCPOA
negotiations. In this regard, the nuclear program serves as both a 4
diplomatic tool and a deterrent, supporting Iran\'s strategic depth in
hostile surroundings. This study intends to provide a complete and
multidimensional picture of Iran\'s nuclear path by including
neorealism, detergent theory, constructivist models, and status-seeking
frameworks. It refutes oversimplified characterizations of Iranian
policy as aggressive or illogical and instead presents the nuclear issue
as a rational, ideologically reinforced, geopolitically located tactic.
By doing this, the study advances knowledge of Middle Eastern nuclear
politics as well as the strategic behavior of states negotiating
regional instability and worldwide power hierarchies. 2. Main Questions
of the Research • How do strategic security concerns, including
historical experiences and regional rivalries, shape Iran's pursuit of a
nuclear program? • To what extent does Iran's nuclear policy reflect its
ambition for regional hegemonic power, and how is this influenced by
international diplomacy and the global nonproliferation regime? 3.
Literature Review The strategic motivations behind Iran's nuclear
program are best understood through the lens of nuclear hedging rather
than an outright pursuit of nuclear weapons. [[Iran]{.mark} has
[developed]{.mark} extensive [enrichment capabilities]{.mark} that
[exceed]{.mark} its [civilian]{.mark} energy [needs]{.mark}, concealed
parts of its program, and resisted full cooperation with the
International Atomic Energy Agency (IAEA).]{.underline} These actions
suggest a hedging strategy that maintains the option to rapidly develop
nuclear weapons if necessary. This approach allows Tehran to project
regional power, deter adversaries, and secure diplomatic leverage while
remaining within the formal boundaries of the Nuclear NonProliferation
Treaty (NPT). By staying near the nuclear threshold, Iran complicates
international efforts to assess its true intentions, thus gaining
strategic advantages without crossing red lines (Bowen & Moran, 2015).
The hedging approach also plays a significant role in regional power
dynamics. [Iran [portrays]{.mark} its nuclear [program as a sovereign
right and]{.mark} symbol of technological progress, [reinforcing]{.mark}
its domestic [legitimacy]{.mark} and [challenging Western-dominated
nuclear norms.]{.mark}]{.underline} Frameworks such as the 5 Joint
Comprehensive Plan of Action (JCPOA) implicitly tolerate this ambiguity,
potentially encouraging similar hedging strategies by regional rivals
like Saudi Arabia. Iran\'s dual-track strategy of negotiating while
advancing technical capabilities demonstrates its intent to manage
external pressures while preserving strategic depth (Bowen & Moran,
2015; Yaphe, 2010). The security dimension of Iran's nuclear motivations
is central to analysis, which applies a gametheoretical model rooted in
Sagan's security, domestic politics, and norms framework. [Aydın argues
that [Iran's]{.mark} nuclear [ambitions]{.mark} are a rational r[esponse
to perceived existential threats]{.mark}, especially [from]{.mark} the
[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates and its allies. These fears are deeply
[rooted in]{.mark} historical [grievances]{.mark} [such as]{.mark}
Western [support for Iraq during the Iran-Iraq War]{.mark} [and]{.mark}
the 1953 [U.S.-backed coup]{.mark}. This enduring [threat perception
underpins Iran's belief in]{.mark} [nuclear deterrence as essential for
survival and regime continuit]{.mark}y (Aydin, 2009).]{.underline}
Domestic political dynamics also shape Iran's nuclear policy. Internal
power struggles between reformist and conservative factions, along with
public support grounded in nationalism and scientific pride, create an
environment in which nuclear development is both politically useful and
ideologically reinforced. And it deepens this understanding by arguing
that the nuclear issue is infused with cultural and discursive
constructs of honor. For Iranian elites, defending the nuclear program
is synonymous with defending national identity and revolutionary
principles. The narrative of honor explains internal opposition to
compromise, framing negotiations such as the Paris and Geneva Agreements
as betrayals of national dignity (Aydin, 2009; Rivera, 2016). This
domestic-ideological lens is further expanded by constructivist model of
"strategic identity." Rather than viewing nuclear policy through purely
realist or liberal perspectives, Khalili presents Iran's actions as
rooted in a triad of internal political identity, regional rivalry, and
international hierarchy. He emphasizes ideational elements such as
martyrdom and resistance, which contribute to a national discourse that
prioritizes autonomy and sovereignty over material calculations. The
strategic identity model shows how Iran's revolutionary heritage and
ideological independence shape its nuclear stance as much as
conventional security concerns (Khalili, 2018). While deterrence and
prestige are prominent themes, it introduces a sociological
interpretation by linking Iran's nuclear motivations to status
frustration. [He argues that [Iran]{.mark}, despite its scientific 6 and
cultural heritage[, feels denied recognition]{.mark} on the global
stage. The [pursuit of nuclear technology]{.mark} becomes [a symbolic
route to]{.mark} re[claiming its]{.mark} rightful [place among
influential states.]{.mark}]{.underline} Unlike deterministic realist
views, Prosser posits that Iran's behavior is not inevitably aimed at
weaponization but reflects a desire for respect and inclusion. He
suggests that pathways for deescalation exist through international
recognition, status incentives, and integration into global governance
frameworks (Prosser, 2017). Iran's quest for regional hegemony as
another critical driver. Nuclear capability is viewed as a tool to
enhance Iran\'s influence in the Middle East, counterbalance
U.S.-aligned Sunni Arab states, and resist encirclement by American
military bases. Iran's historical experiences, especially during the
Iran-Iraq War, have convinced its leadership of the need for strategic
self-reliance. Nuclear development thus becomes part of a broader policy
of asserting geopolitical agency while mitigating foreign intervention
and sanctions (Mahboub, 2013; Yaphe, 2010). A nuanced assessment of
Iran's strategic ambiguity. Rather than signaling offensive intent,
Iran's nuclear posture is a deterrent mechanism born from asymmetric
regional power structures. The ambiguity strategy allows Iran to remain
within NPT limits while gaining strategic leverage. links this to
broader nuclear deterrence theory and neorealist survival logic,
emphasizing that states facing superior enemies and lacking strong
alliances naturally gravitate toward latent nuclear capabilities. He
also refutes the notion that Iran's nuclear drive is about reviving
Persian imperialism, arguing instead that its motivations are primarily
defensive and regional in nature (Mahboub, 2013)

Third: Economic Sanctions as a Tool of Hegemony

Economic sanctions are no less dangerous than military wars; they
represent a silent cold war against the people, not the regimes.
Sanctions imposed on countries such as Iran, Cuba, Syria, and Venezuela
have weakened domestic economies, increased poverty rates, and deprived
millions of people of access to medicine and food. international law and
political blackmail by a great power.

through political, military, and economic support, covers up the

Palestinian people, and obstructs all UN initiatives aimed at a just and

helped fuel anger among Arab and Muslim peoples. has led many movements
to the option of armed resistance.

### AT: Backsliding

#### No Democratic Backsliding- its self correcting- studies and empirics. 

**Chase-Dunn,** C., **& Zhai**, E. (20**25**). Christopher Chase-Dunn is
from the Institute for Research on World-Systems, University of

\
Democracy and Autocracy Since 1900¶ The Varieties of Democracy Institute
(V-Dem) was founded in 2014 at the Department of Political Science at
the University of Gothenburg (Sweden) to study the changing
characteristics of national governments. V-Dem produces an annual
Democracy Report that codes regimes to study changes over time in the
strength of national democratic institutions. The V-Dem coding sorts
types of political behavior and regulation into two main categories:
*democracy* (mutual consultation and majority rule) and *autocracy* (a
small elite makes social decisions regardless of the majority[). A large
[panel of experts scores regimes]{.mark} into two levels of
[democracy]{.mark}, two levels of [autocracy]{.mark}, [and]{.mark} two
'[grey zones']{.mark} of uncertainty, and they [study how regimes have
changed since 1900]{.mark} using this scheme.¶]{.underline} The 2025
Democracy Report ([Nord et al.,
2025](https://journals-sagepub-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1177/08969205251337969#bibr54-08969205251337969))
is a fascinating study of the trends of countries and averages of
regions and of the world. The codings are done by five experts per
country-year observation, using a pool of over 4200 country experts who
provide judgments on concepts and cases ([Nord et al.,
2025](https://journals-sagepub-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1177/08969205251337969#bibr54-08969205251337969):
49).¶ Upward Trend of Democracy Interrupted by Autocracy Recoveries¶
[Figure
7](https://journals-sagepub-com.proxy.lib.umich.edu/doi/full/10.1177/08969205251337969#fig7-08969205251337969)
shows average V-Dem scores for their Electoral Democracy Index for all
the countries studied ('World'), which are weighted by their population
sizes from 1900 to 2024. [It also shows [the scores for the United
States, China, and India]{.mark} because we are studying the US
hegemonic decline and India and China because the quantitative studies
of economic and military power reviewed above show that these are [they
only countries that are converging on the declining US power]{.mark}
shares.\]{.underline}

### Russia/China Revisionist

#### The United States, Russia, and China are all revisionist nations---potential conflict inevitable.

**Goh 2025**-- Evelyn Goh is the Shedden Professor of Strategic Policy
Studies at the Australian National University, where she is also
Research Director at the Strategic & Defence Studies Centre. She has
published widely on U.S.-China relations and diplomatic history,
regional security order in East Asia, Southeast Asian strategies towards

revisionist powers," (01 June 2025)
<https://eastasiaforum.org/2025/06/01/navigating-a-world-of-revisionist-powers/>//

[We are living in a world with **three leading great powers** --- all
with **explicitly revisionist** aims when it comes to the international
'rules-based order'.]{.underline} For those accustomed to thinking about
global order as being centred on great powers, **this is the most
significant reinvention of that order since the end of the Cold War.**
[Since its formation, [**Russia** has **invaded** its
**neighbours**]{.mark} and **taken territory** and populations **by
force.** Since 1950, the United States and its allies have worried about
[**China's**]{.mark} stated **[imperative to reunify Taiwan]{.mark} and
assertions of its maritime territorial claims. In** 2025, the
[U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates under the second **Donald Trump**
presidency has announced an agenda [to **'take' Greenland** and **annex
Canada.**]{.mark} **Revisionist powers do not play by agreed rules.** In
[radically
revising](https://eastasiaforum.org/2024/12/17/trade-wars-are-neither-good-nor-easy-to-win/)
the terms of trade, Trump follows a long line of US leaders since 1945
struggling with the Triffin dilemma ---]{.underline} the provider of the
international reserve currency cannot sustain currency convertibility
alongside control over domestic currency valuation and trade balances.
[Previous US leaders wielded monetary instruments to control inflation
and improve current account deficits.]{.underline} [Trump is now using
the blunter instruments of raising tariffs and cutting foreign aid, with
the ostensible aim of improving US export access and competitiveness.
[Trump's tariffs ring the death knell of the low- or no-tariff global
trading]{.mark} system.]{.underline} Trump's giant shove is likely to
stimulate economic initiatives among 'the rest' excluding the United
States. The ultimate decoupling may turn out to be between the United
States and the rest of the world, not just the United States and China.
[A [revision]{.mark}ist regime [in Washington]{.mark} also presents
\[an\] opportunity to think equally [unpalatable]{.mark} thoughts about
a new [US--China economic-security bargain.]{.mark}]{.underline}
Transglobal technologies will continue to break borders but geography
may reassert itself too. [A more self-centred United States is much less
likely to conduct military campaigns on the other side of the world,
while [Europe]{.mark} will be [forced to deal directly with the Russia
threat.]{.mark} East [Asia must learn to live with]{.mark} a risen
[China]{.mark} without an unreliable offshore balancer]{.underline}. The
rest of the world is a large and amorphous bunch. They can and have been
followers, pawns or sites of great power rivalry and conflict. But they
can and have also been innovators, leaders, collective action
specialists and out-of-the-box thinkers and shakers. Consider the
Non-Aligned Movement, the European and African Unions, the BRICS' New
Development Bank and the Treaty on the Prohibition of Nuclear Weapons,
to name a few examples. Regions including East, South and Central Asia,
Africa, Latin America and parts of the Middle East are used to
value-diverse, dynamic and often dangerous environments. [They have had
to navigate regional and international orders not of their own making
and make their way without **neat categories of perpetual friends or
sworn enemies.**]{.underline} They may hate it, and dread the costs, but
the Global Majority is better prepared psychologically to face the
current world of relative revisionism. [At this juncture, being willing
to go beyond old assumptions and to ask different questions will help
[all countries to navigate]{.mark} the [new normal]{.mark} of an
international system dominated by [three [revisionist great
powers](https://eastasiaforum.org/2025/03/24/time-to-revise-our-idea-of-who-the-revisionist-powers-are/).]{.mark}]{.underline}
One supposedly worst-case scenario under discussion in Canberra is that
Southeast Asia falls into China's sphere of influence. Australia has
limited direct ability to prevent that development, yet it's a scenario
that's unlikely to eventuate. Fear about the potential 'loss' of
Southeast Asia is misplaced. Regional doubts about the United States are
not new. The United States has allies and firm friends but Southeast
Asia does not see it the same way as Australia --- it's regarded the
United States as neither a necessarily benign nor an altruistic hegemon.
Southeast Asian responses to Chinese power are also not conditioned
mainly by what they think about the United States. Southeast Asians
largely do not see themselves as living in a world circumscribed by two
great powers locked in rivalry. Most engage in hedging, omni-enmeshment
and complex balancing, and their conception is of a layered hierarchical
regional order. Because of China's vital role in economic production and
as a growing source of investment and consumption, the region's main
concern now is the consequence of a serious and prolonged economic
slowdown in China. This will impose a huge, additional strain upon
Southeast Asia's economic prospects on top of US-created volatility. The
Trump tariffs will compel some regional economies to manage an even
greater influx of cheaper Chinese products. Yet the major regional worry
is not centred on China's economic strength, which is viewed as good for
a region that has experience in managing the asymmetry of power with its
neighbouring giant.

### Prolif Good

#### Nuclear proliferation [strengthens]{.underline} deterrence\-\--it [raises the stakes]{.underline} for all sides [so high]{.underline} that nobody is [incentivized]{.underline} to escalate.

Rami **Marogy '24** \[(Rami Marogy holds a Master of Arts in Political
Science from the University of Windsor.) "Weapons of Stability:
Non-Proliferation Failures and the Strategic Benefits of Nuclear
Deterrence," University of Windsor,
https://uwindsor.scholaris.ca/server/api/core/bitstreams/b9125803-478d-4ee7-a3a2-c4cfc683d057/content,

Deterrence

[[Deterrence is]{.underline}]{.mark} the practice of [using [threats
to]{.mark}]{.underline} dissuade an actor from taking a certain action.
112 It often involves an effort to stop or [**[prevent an
action]{.mark}** from being taken]{.underline} rather than forcing an
action to be taken.113 Deterrence discourages states from taking
unwanted actions (in most cases, military aggression). Strategy is the
art of organizing an effort to achieve a certain outcome, often altering
an opponent\'s preferences so they align with one's liking. Deterrence
is a prime example of strategy because deterrence intends to alter an
opponent\'s actions without fighting in an effort to ensure peace,
preserve the status quo or ensure that diplomacy is the main course of
action and not war in order to conduct international affairs.114

Deterrence [intends to prevent war or]{.underline} the event of an
[unwanted action]{.underline}, not promote it **[and
[relies]{.mark}]{.underline}** almost solely **[[on]{.mark}
[capability]{.mark}]{.underline}**. Simply put, unless [a
state]{.underline} is willing to gamble on a bluff, it [must have the
military capability]{.underline} to carry out [its threats [in case
deterrence fails]{.mark}]{.underline}.115 The chances of deterrence
being effective are higher if the opposing side knows that the state
issuing the threat genuinely has the military power to do so.

There are many types of deterrence theories, but the main ones that will
be highlighted here are (1) conventional deterrence and (2) nuclear
deterrence. As the term suggests, conventional deterrence is based on
threats regarding conventional weaponry.116 Nuclear deterrence, on the
other hand, is based on threats regarding the use of nuclear weapons.117
[[Conventional deterrence]{.underline}]{.mark}, however, is
[often]{.underline}times **[[exposed to
weakness]{.mark}es]{.underline}**. For example, as mentioned earlier,
despite [[Pakistan]{.underline}]{.mark} having the military backing of
the West [in the [1960s]{.mark}]{.underline}, it was still un[able to
avoid war and suffered a **military loss**]{.underline} to its
arch-enemy, India. Even currently, conventional deterrence has flaws.

The U.S. military employed [conventional deterrence [in
Taiwan]{.mark}]{.underline} against China for several decades by
providing Taiwan with military arms. Because Taiwan is a leading
democracy and technological powerhouse, it is a key U.S. partner in the
Indo-Pacific region and is a leading country within the global
economy.118 Since the signing of the Taiwan Relations Act of 1979, the
U.S. has provided Taiwan with military arms and established a military
presence in the region.119 However, the U.S. attempt at trying to
minimize and combat China's growing influence in the region has
[[not]{.mark} been [without difficulties]{.mark}]{.underline}. Since
2016, [[China]{.mark} has been pursuing a **more [aggressive
policy]{.mark}**]{.underline} with regard to military activity within
the Taiwan Strait. China's [rejection of]{.underline} the claim of the
Taiwan Strait being "[international waters]{.underline}" has
[[led]{.mark} it [to]{.mark}]{.underline} flying across the Strait and
**[[provoking]{.mark} the Taiwanese and U.S. militaries]{.underline}**,
oftentimes playing war games.120 These war games include China's
deployment of naval vessels along the border, flying into Taiwanese air
space, and taunting Taiwanese and U.S. pilots in the area.121

There are a number of reasons why the U.S. is struggling with this
[conventional]{.underline} approach. First, any [support]{.underline}
the U.S. provides to Taiwan can be swiftly [countered by China due
to]{.underline} its **[[geographic proximity]{.underline}]{.mark}**. The
Trump administration took a strategy of military deterrence in the
Taiwan Strait by announcing the regular passage of U.S. warships through
the Strait, which the Biden administration has continued to do.122 For
example, in 2021, the U.S. called on China to cease pressure on Taiwan
and to respond by diplomatic dialogue after it sent 13 military aircraft
into Taiwan's Air Defense Identification Zone (ADIZ).123 The next day,
however, China sent 15 military aircraft into the ADIZ.124 Secondly, the
U.S. is limited geographically as to how many troops it is able to
provide Taiwan in a timely manner seeing as it is thousands of miles
away from the region. China, being a direct neighbour, is able to
continue its efforts with great ease and put constant pressure on
Taiwan. Although this conventional method has worked for a number of
years now, China's military capabilities continue to grow as it seeks to
increase its influence in the region. Additionally, its recent [[war
games]{.mark}]{.underline} have [brought into question]{.underline} the
**[limited abilities]{.underline}** that U.S. forces in the area are
able to provide **[for Taiwan]{.underline}**. These [weaknesses
in]{.underline} the U.S. conventional [deterrence]{.underline} approach
could have [[significant]{.underline}]{.mark} implications [[if]{.mark}
there were to be a **case of military [escalation]{.mark}**]{.underline}
between China and Taiwan and how efficiently the U.S. would be able to
provide support for Taiwan's defence.

[[Nuclear deterrence]{.underline}]{.mark}, on the other hand, [is far
[more effective]{.mark} as]{.underline} the **[[risks]{.mark} are
[higher]{.mark}]{.underline}**. American political scientist Kenneth
Waltz makes convincing arguments as to how nuclear
[[prolif]{.mark}eration]{.underline} can help [serve
strategic]{.underline} and defensive [purposes while]{.underline} also
crediting it toward its role in **[[promoting peace]{.mark} and
stability]{.underline}** among rival states. Waltz takes the realist
perspective (not surprising considering that he is the founder of
Neo-Realist school in International Relations) and [states
that]{.underline} the primary goal of **[[states]{.underline}]{.mark}**
is ultimately to **[[attain secu]{.mark}rity]{.underline}**.125
Additionally, Waltz argues that the influence of nuclear weapons is
unambiguously clear. Waltz states that "the absolute qu[ality of nuclear
weapons]{.underline} sharply [sets a nuclear world **off
from**]{.underline} a **[conventional]{.underline}** one".126 Waltz
argues that in [[conventional]{.underline}]{.mark} war,
[[miscalcu]{.mark}lation]{.underline} is [[always a
given]{.underline}]{.mark} because the [decision to go to war is based
on]{.underline} the interpretation of **[data available at the
time]{.underline}** hostilities are initiated. Not only can one
incorrectly assess the capability of their forces, but also that of
their enemy.127

An [example]{.underline} of this would be [Iraq]{.underline} in 1990,
when Saddam Hussein severely miscalculated the American capability. When
Saddam invaded Kuwait, he stated that he did not fear a U.S. response
because "the American society was not willing to accept 10,000
casualties".128 Learning from their lessons in Vietnam, U.S. General H.
Norman Schwarzkopf launched an extensive air campaign which saw them
gain superiority over Saddam's forces and led to a quick victory and
minimal casualties.129 History has shown many more examples of states
miscalculating their reasons to go to war, and as Waltz argues,
predicting the results of conventional conflicts "has proved difficult"
to say the least.130

While miscalculation is always a significant risk in conventional
conflicts, [there is [no potential]{.mark}]{.underline} for such
**[[in]{.mark} a [nuclear]{.mark} conflict]{.underline}**. Waltz argues
that "the probability of major war among states having nuclear weapons
approaches zero" and is of the belief that [a war between nuclear powers
would be difficult to maintain]{.underline} at the
[conventional]{.underline} level.131 In his article, "Nuclear Myths and
Political Realities", Waltz looked at the USSR and the U.S. during the
time of the Cold War and argued that [[as]{.underline}]{.mark} the
**[nuclear [arsenals]{.mark}]{.underline}** of both countries
[[**grew**, so did]{.underline}]{.mark} the [[chance
of]{.underline}]{.mark} mutually assured destruction
([[MAD]{.underline}]{.mark}), which forced both countries to a
stalemate.132

The reason the USSR and U.S. were able to emerge from the Cold War,
having not engaged in direct military engagement with each other, was
because of the risk of mutually assured destruction from their nuclear
weapons. Due to the factor that MAD posed and either side having a
second-strike capability, there was [no realistic scenario
where]{.underline} it was convincible for [either side]{.underline} to
[emerge victorious in a]{.underline} full-scale **[nuclear
exchange]{.underline}**. As James Wirtz states, "When it comes to
nuclear deterrence, the side issuing the deterrent threat may or may not
win, but the side facing the deterrent threat is most certainly going to
lose.133 Unlike conventional weapons, nuclear weapons are so destructive
that there is no way to avoid the destructive power they pose, which, as
mentioned, is simply too high of a risk to consider. In situations of
MAD, [where both sides have]{.underline} a **[second
strike]{.underline}** capability, there results in no benefit to the
side which deployed the nuclear weapon first as both sides would be able
to inflict massive retaliatory damage.134 Therefore, nuclear weapons are
able to ensure a level of peace and stability because of their
destructive potential.

In a strategic sense, [[nuclear]{.mark} weapons [introduce a
balance]{.mark}]{.underline} between two sides. Waltz states that
"deterrence depends on what one can do, not on what one will do. What
deters is the fact that we can do as much damage to them as we choose
and they to us. The country suffering the retaliatory attack cannot
limit the damage done to it, only the retaliator can do that".135 Even
if either side were to launch an attack on the other, they would have to
consider a number of possibilities. First, [would it be [strategically
realistic]{.mark}]{.underline} to risk the safety and the lives of their
citizens for an attack on an enemy? Such a [decision would]{.underline}
ultimately **[go against the primary goal of states]{.underline}**,
which, as mentioned earlier, is [[to ensure
security]{.underline}]{.mark}.136 Second, the [[weapon]{.mark} would
[need to be accurate]{.mark}]{.underline} enough [to hit its
targets]{.underline}. The moment a weapon (of any kind) is launched, the
oppo[sing side would be notified **within minutes**]{.underline} due to
multiple global defence monitoring systems. Thirdly, if the weapon were
to go [off course]{.underline}, it could hit an unintended target and
[have severe consequences]{.underline}, whether diplomatic or
environmental. Similarly, if the weapon were to be
[intercepted]{.underline}, it would be an obvious attempt at an attack
and [cause the opposing side to **act similarly**]{.underline} in
defence. Moscow is not the only location in Russia that can fire a
nuclear warhead, and neither is Washington in the U.S.

[In addition]{.underline}, a nuclear [[attack on

### NFU, Prolif

#### NFU undermines extended deterrence and causes proliferation

**Dodge Et al. 24** \[Michaela Dodge, Research Scholar, National
Institute for Public Policy; former Research Fellow for Missile Defense
and Nuclear Deterrence, The Heritage Foundation; Senior Defense Policy
Advisor to Senator Jon Kyl (2018); Ph.D. in Political Science, George
Mason University; M.S. in Defense and Strategic Studies, Missouri State
University (Ulrike Schumacher Memorial Scholarship); B.A. in
International Relations and Defense and Strategic Studies, Masaryk
University (Czech Republic); Publius Fellow, Claremont Institute; CSIS
PONI Nuclear Scholars Initiative scholar; adjunct professor, Graduate
School of Defense and Strategic Studies, Missouri State University, and
Keith B. Payne, President and co‑founder, National Institute for Public
Policy; Professor Emeritus and former Head, Graduate Department of
Defense and Strategic Studies, Missouri State University; former Deputy
Assistant Secretary of Defense for Forces Policy; Senior Advisor to the
Office of the Secretary of Defense; commissioner, Congressional
Commission on the Strategic Posture of the United States; award‑winning
author of nearly 150 publications; adjunct professor, Georgetown
University School of Foreign Service (1984--2005); recipient of the
Department of Defense Distinguished Public Service Medal, 6-7-2024, "No
First Use: Threatening Alliance Cohesion, Assurance, and
Non-Proliferation," RealClearDefense,
<https://www.realcleardefense.com/articles/2024/06/07/no_first_use_threatening_alliance_cohesion_assurance_and_non-proliferation_1036507.html>,
SHCP AB\]

[The U.S. [extended]{.mark} nuclear [deterrent]{.mark} is [underpinned
by]{.mark} the deterrent threat option to escalate to nuclear [first
use]{.mark} in the event of otherwise unstoppable aggression against an
ally. ]{.underline} For decades, major allies have testified as to the
critical importance they attach to this nuclear escalation threat behind
the U.S. extended nuclear deterrent.  [It is a [key]{.mark} reason,
[allies insist]{.mark}, that they are able to stand back from pursuing
their own national possession of nuclear weapons---and thus a key to
U.S. nuclear non-proliferation goals.  It is no overstatement to
conclude that, for decades, the U.S. extended deterrent, including the
nuclear escalation option, have been essential [to]{.mark} the cohesion
of U.S. [alliances and]{.mark} the relative success of nuclear
[non-proliferation.]{.mark}\[1\]]{.underline}

[Episodic U.S. initiatives to move to no first use (NFU) or "sole
purpose" nuclear weapon policies---that would preclude U.S. nuclear
employment in response to anything other than an opponent's nuclear
attack---would directly [contradict]{.mark} the traditional U.S.
extended nuclear deterrent [commitment to allies.]{.mark}]{.underline}
These initiatives are a prime example of how the U.S. pursuit of arms
control goals can unintentionally undermine the keys to alliance
cohesion---extended nuclear deterrence and the assurance of allies.
[U.S. allies have [consistently expressed]{.mark} sharp, substantive
[opposition to U.S. proposals for]{.mark} an [NFU]{.mark} or "sole
purpose" nuclear policy---two different titles for essentially the same
policy constraint on U.S. deterrent strategies, i.e., precluding a U.S.
nuclear response to an opponent's non-nuclear attack, including an
opponent's chemical or biological weapons (CBW)
attack.\[2\]]{.underline}

Despite this consistent, enduring allied opposition and a deteriorating
national security environment, recent U.S. presidential administrations
continue to signal their enthusiasm for an NFU or "sole purpose" policy
in an effort to showcase their commitment to reducing the number and
role of nuclear weapons.  For example, coincident with Secretary of
State Antony Blinken's most recent three-day visit in China, Assistant
Secretary of State Mallory Stewart reportedly declared with seeming
enthusiasm that the United States "is open to considering a proposal by
China that nuclear weapons states negotiate a treaty on the no-first use
(NFU) of nuclear weapons."\[3\] This renewed signaling by the Biden
Administration of interest in NFU is only the latest in Washington's
expressions of interest in an NFU policy, and will likely again be
followed by strong allied pushback. This cycle has been repeated
numerous times over the past five decades.  As a recent academic study
of the subject rightly concluded, "The question of whether the United
States should adopt an NFU pledge has arisen repeatedly in debates of
declaratory policy and is likely to recur..."\[4\]

Various administrations' efforts to move toward an NFU policy in
contradiction of the U.S. nuclear umbrella for allies typically have
been supported by some members of Congress who have proposed laws
articulating their own version of the NFU or "sole purpose"
policy.\[5\] Washington's continuing initiatives to adopt such an arms
control policy that allies expressly and repeatedly oppose, based on
their serious and understandable concerns about its degrading effect on
the credibility of the U.S. extended deterrent, contribute to growing
allied questioning of U.S. credibility as a guarantor of their security.

Ironically, perhaps, Washington's numerous arms control forays toward an
NFU policy contribute to allied doubts about extended deterrence and
undermine U.S. efforts to assure allies regarding their security
position. In short, Washington's repeated moves in the direction of an
NFU policy fan allied fears about U.S. extended deterrence credibility
that, in turn, undermine U.S. efforts to sustain allied cohesion and
non-proliferation goals. Rather than recognizing this problem and
finally curtailing its initiatives to advance an NFU policy, or spending
the enormous resources needed to provide a plausible alternative to the
traditional U.S. nuclear escalation threat backstopping extended
deterrence, Washington continually disturbs allies with its repeated NFU
forays---only to stand back following equally-repeated allied pushback.

**Allied Opposition to NFU**

[Allied [opposition]{.mark} to NFU and "sole purpose" policies is
[based]{.mark} largely [on]{.mark} understandable [fears that, at a time
of]{.mark} increasing [regional threats]{.mark} to their security, U.S.
[adoption]{.mark} of such policies would [undermine]{.mark} the
[credibility of extended nuclear deterrence]{.mark}.]{.underline}\[6\] 
This fear is almost certain to be accurate in plausible
circumstances.\[7\]  Yet, U.S. administrations have repeatedly expressed
interest in implementing NFU or "sole purpose" policies---raising
questions among allies about U.S. intentions and the continuing
credibility of the U.S. extended deterrent---and thus undermining needed
alliance cohesion and expanding the potential for nuclear proliferation.
[By continuing to pursue [NFU]{.mark} or "sole purpose" policies despite
consistent allied objections, Washington [contributes to allied
uncertainty regarding]{.mark} the U.S. commitment to extended deterrence
and to their [security]{.mark}, and thereby [contributes to]{.mark} the
very allied doubts and associated [proliferation]{.mark} problem
Washington seeks to avoid in the first place.]{.underline}

Washington should keep in mind the guidance that former Defense
Secretaries William Perry and James Schlesinger offered pertaining to
allied concerns regarding U.S. nuclear policy choices. Secretary
Schlesinger advised:  "[It is [important]{.mark} for us [to pay
attention to]{.mark} their \[[allied\] concern]{.mark} and not to judge
whether deterrence is effective by our standards, but we have to take
their standards into account as well."\[8\] Secretary Perry went on to
argue that "the failure to do this, as suggested by Dr. Schlesinger, the
[failure]{.mark} to do this [will be that]{.mark} those [nations]{.mark}
will feel that they have to provide their own deterrence---in other
words, they will have to provide their own [nuclear weapons]{.mark}. So
that will lead to a failure of \[non\]proliferation."\[9\]]{.underline}

[Alliance dissolution and the consequent likely cascade of nuclear
proliferation [would be a major blow to U.S. national security]{.mark}
and non-proliferation goals.]{.underline} Yet, by periodically floating
NFU policies that are anathema to allied perceptions of extended
deterrence and assurance requirements, Washington continues to fan the
prospects for alliance dissolution and a cascade of nuclear
proliferation. This ongoing problem is wholly avoidable if only
Washington would recognize the implications this arms control aspiration
holds for extended deterrence credibility---and its corresponding
potential effect on alliance cohesion and proliferation incentives.

### NFU Links to NB.

#### NFU bolsters deterrence. 

**Center For Arms Control and Non-Proliferation, nd**. (The Center for
Arms Control and Non-Proliferation is a nonpartisan nonprofit dedicated
to reducing and eventually eliminating the threats posed by nuclear,
chemical and biological weapons through Congressional engagement and
public education.)
<https://armscontrolcenter.org/issues/no-first-use/no-first-use-myths-vs-realities/> -
[GL]{.mark}

Reality: [[Adopting a No First Use policy will not reduce the United
States' commitment to extended deterrence**. Extended deterrence is
about much more than just nuclear weapons**]{.underline}]{.mark}.
Forward deployments of [[U.S. conventional forces paired with]{.mark}
strong and unwavering [political commitments play the most immediate
roles in deterring aggression]{.mark} against the United States and its
allies. [The]{.mark} [U]{.mark}nited [S]{.mark}tates [maintains
thousands of troops and hundreds of ships]{.mark} in commands [around
the world to]{.mark} balance crisis zones and [deter aggression]{.mark}.
The United States employs advanced conventional capabilities including
[intelligence, surveillance, and reconnaissance tools and
precision-guided weapons]{.mark} in conflicts, but also to [help deter
potential attacks]{.mark}. The United States' robust conventional forces
are more than sufficient to deter or counter non-nuclear attacks on U.S.
allies, and conventional military threats are more credible than threats
to use nuclear weapons first.]{.underline} Current and former military
officials have emphasized the crucial role that U.S. conventional forces
play in projecting power globally and credibly deterring aggression.
[[The credible U.S. second-strike capability]{.mark} is and [will remain
a deterrent to nuclear attack, but the **threat of nuclear preemption
is** ]{.mark}unnecessary and **[dangerous]{.mark}**. [If]{.mark}
nuclear-armed [adversaries believe]{.mark} [that the U]{.mark}nited
[S]{.mark}tates [would use nuclear weapons first, they will]{.mark} be
incentivized to [use their nuclear weapons before]{.mark} a devastating
U.S. strike degrades the capability. [This]{.mark} dynamic [is
destabilizing and increases the chance of a nuclear
miscalc]{.mark}ulation.]{.underline} Maintaining extended deterrence
guarantees requires consistent communication and coordination. Any
change to U.S. declaratory policy should be made after consultation with
allies about why this change is in our collective security interest.

### Hotlines Fail

#### HOTLINES FAIL LAUNDRY LIST OF REASONS

#### CHINA DOESN'T ANSWER 

**(Ruhl, 24)** \-- senior researcher at [Founders
Pledge](https://founderspledge.com/) and the fund manager of the [Global

Fund](http://founderspledge.com/funds/global-catastrophic-risks).
